“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at Sydney, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
"•A." la. e BnII e ti n,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis-
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
8 P roteot i° n against
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
Ohmaman, the cheap nigger, and the
excluded r ° Pean pauper t 0 be absolutel .y
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
otate monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System - one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
-Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
.ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “ nobility. ’
, public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless news paper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
Ihe Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
mpre or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily anc
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
I thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus
tralian journal which, throughout its career, ha
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Iris!
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of nationa
progress : it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the chums of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise ol
any human authority which has not been grantee
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast.mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

Dear Bulletin, —Your reference to Dr,
Kerr’s nugget, or specimen, the first found up
Bathurst way, awakens ancient memories. Tlit
doctor was being shown, by some of his men, a
few quartz-specimens carrying gold freely, when
the blackfellow (“ Tommy,” I think he was called)
joined them. Dr. Kerr, turning to the neu
coiner, said, “You know what?’’ at the same
time pointing to the shining metal. “ No. What
name that peller?” was the darkey’s reply.
“ That’s gold, Tommy. Plenty money that
fellow,’’ said Kerr. “I know where budgeree
peller like that sit down, big as bullock’s cobbra,
the black remarked, as he eyed the stone indiffer-
ently. Tommy’s words made some impression on
his master, who kept his eye on the native, till,
some time after, a favourable opportunity occur
ing, Kerr questioned the blackfellow more closely
concerning his find. Tommy rode out with his
master and showed the specimen sticking out on
the surface, but joined to stone underneath. Kerr
appropriated the gold and made the black a pre-
sent of a team of bullocks (Tommy was a first-
class bullock-driver) and a flock of sheep
Tommy thereupon put up a shanty at
Louisa Creek and started butchering, and
he and his lubra lived at the shop. But it
was nob many days before the whole tribe of
blacks, to which Tommy and his lubra belonged,
camped around the establishment, and a wild
orgie commenced that only terminated with the
consumption of the flock of sheep, team of bul-
locks and the darkey’s happiness. When Tommy
first opened the business things boomed amazingly.
Many sheep were killed, sold and paid for; but,
when the tribe pitched their gunyahs near every-
thing was altered, and the business was always
too drunk to attend to and look after its own
interests. Diggers who went for mutton had to
catch and kill the sheep, while many took the
animal away alive. These latter seldom paid for
their meat. Things went on thus till one day the
opposition butcher bought the balance of the
flock. That night a wild corroboree commenced,
and ran on till the cash was done and the liquor-
supply out off—ere which melancholy consum-
mation poor Tom, blase with the enjoyment
his wealth had so lavishly provided, left
the gay and festive scene and went to
seek his wife, but nob finding her he retired
to his bed. In the morning, when Tom’s
lubra (she had been away administering consola-
tion to a poor countryman who had lately lost his
wife in the flood) went to her doss, she found
another woman in company with her “ola man.”
Loud were the yells and shrieks that broke upon
the morning air at this discovery of her husband’s
perfidy. All the blacks that were not too drunk
to crawl gathered round the dwelling, some sym-
pathising with the wronged wife, while others,
who knew where she had been, smiled at the
coincidence. In the tumult Tom awoke and,
seeming to understand the state of affairs at a
glance, picked up a butcher’s cleaver that lay by
and struck his lady-love—l don’t mean his wife—
several times on the head, almost killing her.
During the day he was arrested and sent off to
Bathurst. As a rouseabout, Tommy was con-
sidered a first-class man, but as a financier he
proved himself an utter failure. —H.W.
The Bulletin’s par. re the heaven-in-
spired idea of the Church of England Congress
printing half a million portraits of its bishops for
distribution among English cottagers brings the
following from a correspondent. The Marist
missionaries to the Tokelau group once left a
Samoan teacher there, to reside on the island
(Nukunono). He was a dull, unimaginative man
and made bub few converts. One day a trader
came in to chat, and although nob a Catholic,
sought to cheer him up. Hanging on the wall
was a print illustrative of the martyrdom of P&re
Channel (killed on Fatuna in ’47) and depicting
the murdered missionary ascending to heaven
escorted by a retinue of angels. The print was
old, faded and yellow with age and exposure.
“ If you had a new one in colours—bright colours —
and put it outside your house instead of keeping
it inside, these natives would come and look at it;
then you could talk to them and tell them what it
meant, and then you will get them interested in
your lota (religion)," said the trader. The teacher
shook his head. That was his one picture and he
wasn’t likely to ever get another. However, the
trader was going to Samoa and he promised to see
what he could get there. Taking the print with
him to Samoa, as a guide for a better picture, he
called upon a man with a local reputation as an
artist (painting sign-boards, etc.). When he went
back to Nukunono he took with him a picture
3ft. x 3ft. There was the ascending missionary
surrounded by a large flock of gaudy angels, most
of whom were playing furiously on concertinas and
accordeons ; some carried a string of flying-fish,
others baskets of taro and bundles of twist tobacco
and pipes, and one in the van humped what was
intended to represent a 501 b. keg of beef ; in fact,
everyone carried some kind of edible. Overhead
was depicted the Kingdom of Heaven, a large flat
place with a number of cocoanut trees bearing
ooooanuts as big as the keg of beef aforesaid, and
beneath a number of women preparing ovens for
the food being conveyed thither by the angels.
Sitting in a row were a number of white-bearded i
old men (all coloured brown like the other persons,
to be in accordance with the idea of a purely Tok-
elau heaven) who gazed stolidly at the approaching
visitors. The teacher unpacked the picture and
put it in the hitherto-unvisited little church. In
two hours the whole population surged round and
pushed each other down to get a better view while
the teacher improved the occasion. In three days
he had made fifty converts and it was only a
matter of a few weeks before the whole island was
baptised en masse.
By the Sea.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Day is at noon, and a cloud,
A glory of snowy rings,
Over the city is bowed,
Poised on ethereal wings,
Like a stainless spirit and proud,
Scorning earthly things.
The sea is about my feet,
Folding in shallow waves
Musio as sad and sweet
As a bruised spirit craves,
Like voices when angels meet
Over children’s graves.
But the flower of my soul’s content—
Not the cloud, nor the sea,
With all their loveliness blent,
Can -restore unto me—
For the flower of my soul with its scent
Is with thee —with thee.
Wellington, Maoriland.
HUBERT CHURCH.

A Domain Lullaby.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
This is the lay of the loafers gay,
The song of the idle crew ;
Tim Anthem of Hope to that lazy throng,
The crowd who but spit and chew.
Oh ! jolly and sweet is the life we pass,
_ g aiJ y the hours go by,
vVith never a thought of work undone
To summon us hatefully.
And who would slave in a cramped-up room,
Or sweat in some ill-lit den,
W hen the lamp of heaven awaits without
To lighten the lives of men ?
Why blame the love of freedom and ease f
Why censure the hate of work ?
We only follow our views of life
Our ingrained gospel of “ Shirk.”
So lazily live, my brother drones,
And then (not lazily) die;
Our only home God’s earth of green,
Our only roof the sky.
A DOMATNER.
EPILOGUE; BY AN UNDOMAINER.
Just so, gay loafer ! Why should you sweat
For the privilege of a crust ?
I would I could live like you, you bet,
But I’m married and—“ I must.”
That work is noble is rankest bosh,
The idle man never gets hurt;
Why need you worry to dress and wash
Since ease is a matter of dirt ?
Why need you toil when cadging is clear,
And gratis the gift of “ weed,”
And I am the greeny who buys your beer,
And parts for the price of a “feed.”
Lie low, sing high, and mock at the wrath
Of the men who disturb your wool ;
May your pint never lack its head of froth,
And the bowl of your pipe keep full.

Melbourne paper : “ Candidates for the
police force must remember that they are ex-
pected to be good runners.” Do they expect the
burglars to chase the policemen, then ?
On last Shrove Tuesday—the academical
day for such ceremonies—not one divine went up
for the degree of B.D. or D.D. in Trinity College,
Dublin. Up till *9l these degrees were purchase-
able in that University, but since then they can
only be gained by examination. Hence, in ’94, no
one. In ’91 —the last of the purchase years—there
was a terrible crush for the degrees; and those
curious in these matters will therefore be able to
value the pretentious clerical scholar by running
down the date of his displayed advt.
A Maoriland policeman writes to the
papers giving away some of the methods of the
force. One of his stories is interesting. An old
man—a very old man in a harmless state of
lunacy—escaped out of an asylum, and started on
the tramp. Three vast constables were sent after
him, and they presently found their aged prey
beaming placidly in a ditch and thinking of
nothing at all. He raised no objection whatever,
and one of the policemen arrested him with one
hand without the slightest difficulty, and the pro-
cession started for home, but on the way the
“ coppers ” got up a friendly wrestling-match, and
a rib was broken in the confusion. This was
where the trouble came in, for it was necessary
to show that the policeman Lad been bashed
and maimed in the execution of his duty in
order to get medical expenses and sick pay
out of the Government, So the three officers
solemnly reported how the grey-headed wreck
there present in custody had gone for them with
a pole and banged them horribly before lie could
be captured and disarmed, and the party got a
present of .£6 6s. for its “ bravery, tact, and dili-
gence.” And the average police-inspector is so
accustomed to believing tough statements that
even the sight of the little, aged prisoner who was
smiling affably at all the world and trying without
much success to count his own two legs didn’t
raise a doubt about the story.

SATURDAY, APRIL 2fi, 1894.
Example and Precept.
All Governments prescribe virtue to their
peoples. In Europe, within the last hundred
years, the application of the death sentence
has been limited about a hundred fold. But
the murderer is still murdered, the thief, the
ravisher, the perjurer, and persons guilty of
other anti-social offences are degraded and
imprisoned. Civil authority alleges the
common interest as justification for its penal
codes, and the various Priesthoods endorse
the acts of authority with ecclesiastical bless-
ings. Still villainy in all its developments
continues unabated after countless centuries
of such repression. Crime modifies its
detail, and accommodates its forms to meet
the times. But it is only its quality which
varies. Its quantity remains unaffected. It
would be very odd if the facts were other-
wise. The State expects and demands that
all its component parts shall be virtuous,
while itself continues in its corporate capa-
city to violate every moral law and to practise
every infamy. The loftier its pretensions
the more atrocious are its hypocrisy and its
villainy. The monarch and the statesmen,
in effect, demand, and seem to expect, that
society shall reform itself from the bottom
and by atoms while the upper layers exist
upon corruption and diffuse it through
every tissue of the general orga ism. The
individual must personally be virtuous
on pain of punishment, but the aggre-
gation of individuals, known as a nation,
may wallow in every iniquity. The
two positions are utterly incompatible.
Where precept and practice are at variance—
where the excellence of virtue is inculcated
while the teacher owes his position to suc-
cessful rascality—little result can accrue
from his moralisings.
• # »
If history were honestly recorded and
candidly taught to the young it would be the
most pernicious study which could engage
their attention. The story of the rise and
fall of dynasties and of nations is like the
Lord’s Prayer read backwards, or a series of
melodramas reversed. The devil is raised
every time, and the bold, bad villain triumphs
without fail in the last act. Often, indeed,
all the leading characters are villains, and
the interest consists in their struggles for
pre-eminence. In such cases it is usually the
boldest and worst villain who prevails, and
the curtain falls on the Church, in the
character of the virtuous heavy father,
bestowing a blessing on the atrocious hero,
while the stage is littered with the corpses of
victims and resounds with the jubilations of
his accomplices and the anxious acclamations
of sycophants and fools. It is not to be
wondered at that individuals and organisa-
tions have proclaimed themselves to be
at war with society. Such proclama-
tion they are logically justified in regard-
ing as discharging them from every
moral obligation, since their masters
and pastors habitually so regard it.
‘Thou shalt not kill !” Thus, peremptorily,
pronounce the Law and Prophets. “ Unless
you are at war ” is a qualification which the
State insinuates and the Church defers to.
“I am at war—with society,” the outlaw
and assassin declares, and has a logical right
to complain thbt when he has killed some of
the enemy he is hanged instead of being
presented with a medal, a vote of thanks,
and a pension. “ Thou shalt not steal !”
Excellent maxim ! The practical observance,
however, reminds of Lord Chesterfield’s
fatherly counsel to his son : “ You must not
lie. But should circumstances render it
necessary to lie, lay your hand upon your
heart, and say, ‘ upon your honour.’” And
so on throughout the Decalogue. The laws
of England punished adultery during the
reign of Charles 11., while that illustrious
monarch was originating several distin-
guished houses of the “nobility” of Great
Britain. There were similar laws in force,
and in operation too, in France, while
Louis XY. was amusing himself with the
beauties collected in the Parc aux Cerfs. It
was regarded as infamous and cowardly to
strike a woman, and snatching a lady’s purse
would bring a man to the gallows anywhere
in Europe when Frederick Ik of Prussia
made a dash with 50,000 men to tear Silesia
from the Empress Maria Theresa at a time
when he judged she was an unprotected
female.
* # *
Nothing so embellishes a character as
truthfulness and integrity, according to the
code of honour. Yet the functions of a
national representative in a foreign country
are so antagonistic to such sentiment that
mankind chuckles appreciatively over the
epigram that an ambassador is a State officer
who “ lies ” abroad for the good of his
country. Forgery and fraud are the stinking
vices of the despicable rogue. Yet the
forgery of a British admiral’s name—a name
representing the nation—by Clive, preceded
and hastened his elevation to the peerage,
and the high-minded sailor who had indig-
nantly refused to degrade the nation by
attaching his name to a fraudulent document
enthusiastically declared, after the swindle
had been successful and had brought him
some £50,000 of plunder, that the forger
was the finest fellow in existence. The syn-
dicate of trading adventurers who stole half
of Hindostan became “the Honourable”
Company. No other company had ever
been so distinguished, or had stolen so
mightily. Had any individual member of
that historical guild been caught taking a
purse on Hounslow Heath, he would have
hanged at Tyburn. History confers the
title of Great upon a few men. Everyone
so distinguished was a robber on a large
scale, and a wholesale spiller of blood
and promoter of arson. Le Grand
Monarque stole from all his neighbours.
The great Turenne, at his instance,
swept the entire Palatinate with fire and
sword, consuming in one sheet of flame cities
and hamlets, and creating, where a thriving
province had been, a wilderness covered with
ashes and ruins and peopled with corpses in
lieu of men, women and children. The
Great Peter, the Great Frederick, the
Great Napoleon, Alexander, Caesar, Gen-
ghis—the measure of all their greatness was
the immensity of their stealings and the vast-
ness of their murders. But it is not mur-
derers and robbers—that is to say, generals
and conquerors—only who derive terms of
approbation from their iniquities. The rule
is equally applicable in most other walks of
life. Talleyrand and Mbtternich, two
eminent masters of diplomacy, dissected, are
but two monumental liars and cheats, en-
dowed with a devil’s gift of dexterity in
swindling and imposture. Fouche, the
greatest of Police ministers, investigated,
proves to be merely a scoundrel of suoreme
artfulness, transcending in rascality any
individual whom his officers ever haled to
prison. “To deal with bad people,” re-
marked that imbecile, George 111., “it is
necessary to e uploy bad men,” that being
his excuse for making a Minister of the
Earl of Sandwich, a man reeking from the
orgies of Medmenham.
• • #
But, it may be objected, these are old
stories. Their application to present con-
ditions is not apparent. States no longer
mock private citizens by demanding virtue
while they practise vice. Sovereigns do not
now sign death-warrants for criminals whose
lives are better than their own. People are
not now-a-days required to join in adulation
of princes who pursue practices for which
persons of private station are justly sen-
tenced to infamy and sequestration. Is this
so 1 How many princes of Europe are known
to have committed bigamy, openly, fla-
grantly, simply calling it by another name—
Morganatic marriage ? If private citizens of
Belgium pursued the same career as the
Royal local imrnolator of English girls,
would the gaols have accommodation for
them ? By what words will the Re-
cording Angel express the dealings of
the French nation with Siam, and the British
with Egypt and Matabeleland ? When the
landless vagabond, peering over the park
railings of some noble’s domain, hoarsely
spouts to his fellows that if he could have
his will he would despoil the owner of the
property because he could turn it to more
advantageous use, a hundred moralists of
the classes wrathfully denounce his senti-
ments as subversive of order and as shame-
lessly criminal. Yet, these sentiments are
precisely identical with the arguments
habitually employed to extenuate and justify
the action of nations in expelling aboriginal
owners from huge portions of the world’s
surface. It is thus, mainly, that the parti-
tion of Africa is defended.
• * «
While nations steal and murder, it is vain
to hope that individuals will refrain. The
example is too emphatic and too comprehen-
sive to be without influence. The keener
the intelligence of the people the more
clearly they appreciate the immoral con-
veyed. While they are told that it is wrong
to steal and cheat and murder, and that
violation of the prohibition against these
crimes will surely involve them in destruc-
tion, they learn that these are offences only
punishable when committed on a small scale,
and that the true interpretation is not that
it is ruinous to commit them, but that while
they are dangerous when ventured in detail,
they are attended with enormous advantages
when perpetrated on a sufficiently large
scale. To be Wholesale is to be Respectable,
A Sunday Trade Union.
Ten years ago, lecturers were driven off the
Sunday platform in Sydney, and now the
parsons have banded together to outlaw the
Sunday musician. No attempt was ever made
to show that the lecturers were injuring the
public by their teachings. Men like Tyerman,
Bright, Denton, Gerald Massey, Moncure
Conway, Stout, Proctor, and others who at
various periods occupied the rostrum, taught
at least as high an order of morality as is
commonly served out from the pulpit. Their
sole fault was that they competed—and that,
too, successfully—with the churches. They
spoke to men of the day of a dispensation in
accord with contemporary science, and so it
happened that thinking people preferred to
pay to hear them rather than suffer, gratis, a
millionth repetition of “the old, old story,” with
its accompaniment of hell fire for independent
souls, and crowns of glory for sycophants. It
is the same with the musician. The prize fool
has yet to be discovered who will soberly assert
that a concert of sweet and enjoyable sounds
is a bad thing per se. On any night of the
week, from the second to the seventh, it is not
merely tolerated, but enjoyed, by persons who
term themselves “society,” and who go
on Sunday, some of them, to listen
to the winnings of a chartered proser—
not because they like him, but because church-
going is deemed the “ proper thing ” for well-
dressed people on the first day of the week.
The reason for the condemnation of the Sun-
day concert is identical with that which brought
about the extinction of the Sunday lecture. It
competes with the pulpit. Congregations are
steadily decliningin bulk,andare composed more
and more exclusively of devotees in bonnets ;
and the agonising question—“ Why do not the
men attend church?” is being asked with ever-
increasing anxiety. If Sunday is allowed to be-
come a day of reasonable gladness, if well-
lighted halls and theatres open their doors to
the multitude, otherwise relegated to an aimless
promenade of darkened streets or the back par-
lours of third-rate pubs., if, in brief, the people
are conceded a fair measure of Nineteenth
Century liberty, young men and respectable
girls may meet each other without loss of caste
in other places than where sermons are drawled
out, and t he sermonisers will have an even worse
time of it than they at present experience.
* * *
The onslaught on the Sunday concert-givers,
then, to which the perplexing Premier Dibbs
seems for the moment to have committed him-
self, is a question of trades unionism, pure
and simple. Parsons do not relish freedom of
contract when it cuts into their collection-
plates. And, as they dare not speak out their
full sentiments, they exhibit the insufferable
meanness of sheltering themselves behind a
miserable subterfuge. They do not assail the
concerts as mischievous in themselves. They
do not pretend that a song or a symphony
which is innocent on Monday, becomes a guilty
thing, fit only to be suppressed by the police,
if rendered on the preceding evening. If they
did, they know that every layman would laugh
at them. So they protest against a charge for
admission being made at the door, and call up
the ghost of a musty old law abhorred by all live
people of enlightened minds, to terrify the
reluctant civil authority into action. Of course
they are aware that concerts worth hearing can
only be carried on so long as they are paid for,
and that the trade spirit of the Age, of which
they themselves are shining exemplars, is so
pronounced that the mass of people will not pay
for anything which they can have for nothing.
Concerts, unlike creeds, cannot support them-
selves by offering policies of insurance against
eternal fires as a set-off to present premiums.
They are plain, straightforward commercial
enterprises, so much good music for so much
good coin, and with no incantation lottery for
the elect super-added. Free admission kills
them, as a matter of course, and the parson
wants to kill them, though he shrinks from
striking an honest blow. They compete with
him for Sunday patronage, injure his prestige
and reduce his income, and so he forms a union
of preachers and their abettors to drive the
interloper out of the market.
• * *
Apart from this trade rivalry, who has a word
to say against the Sunday concert ? Not the
police. They know right well that just in pro-
portion as the Sunday fetters are withdrawn,
the Monday morning charge-sheet diminishes.
The Sunday art gallery, museum, public library,
excursion, lecture-room and concert-hall are so
many agencies which militate against larrikin-
ism and ruffianism, by offering a rational means
of spending a few pleasant hours on the Wle
day of the week. Throughout America and the
continent of Europe, Sunday brings no additional
work to the police, because the people are allowed
to enjoy themselves pretty well in their own
way, and prefer to enjoy themselves in a reason-
able, sensible manner. Nor is there anyone in
authority here who, we venture to affirm, would,
if left to his own unwarped judgment, move a
finger to suppress the Sunday concert, any
more than he would close the Art Gallery or
Public Library. It is the opposing trade that
does it, helped, in ghoul-like fashion, by the
hands of dead men. Because, in a far-away
land, at a time when a festive monarch was
busily engaged launching fresh lines of aris-
tocracy with the help of an orange-girl, servile
legislators laid down the limits of Sunday
traffic ; or, at a later date, when Sunday clubs
met to discuss the wisdom of continuing to be
ruled by a madman, a British Parliament
passed an Act to protect the Lord’s Day, there
can be found no valid reason for preventing
Australian citizens from attending a musical
entertainment if they are disposed to pay for
the privilege. If the first day of the week is to
have its restrictions, let them be our own
restrictions, not such as perhaps suited a remote
people who were eaten by the worms centuries
ago. We live in a bright, new land, sur-
rounded by sunny, airy influences unknown to
those dead folks, and surely possess suffi-
cient sense to frame our own rules of
living, and not allow ourselves, at the
dictation of priests, to grovel under the
mortmain of less enlightened ages. Life has
not so many joys for the ordinary run of man-
kind that they can afford to forego any of them
in order to bolster up the failing vocation of
clericism, which is founded on dead men’s words
and kept going by the help of dead men’s
statutes.
Individual versus Party.
We learn much of New South Wales Labour
representatives in Parliament, much of New
South Wales Labour delegates in conference,
and precious little of the New South Wales
Labour-party. The personal strife between
leaders of opposing factions has blotted out of
sight the interests of the voters who elected
them. On the eve of a general election, when
unity, concentration and organisation of forces
are above all things desirable, the officers
wrangle over pay and precedence, and the men
are in that state of indecision and perplexity
which is the certain preliminary of rout. And
this is the pass to which the “ success ” of the
Labour-party has brought us !
• • #
The Bulletin doesn’t care two straws who
represents the Labour-party, so long as the
Labour-party is thoroughly represented. It
their votes are not split by selfishness or
treachery, the electors may be relied upon to
return the best men available. Whether the
best man is named Brown, Jones, or Robin-
son, whether he has been in Parliament before
or not, matters very little, so long as he is the
best man, and has sufficient comprehension of
his duty to pull with the party, and not against
it. But unity, before and after the elections, is
essential to success. The present Labour repre-
sentatives have done much, but they have
done nothing like what was expected
from them, what was possible to them,
and chiefly because they were all so
competent (or incompetent) to command that
nobody cared to obey. Yet there is nothing
clearer than this, that if the Party is to succeed,
the individual must be sacrificed. He need not
sacrifice his independence or his conscience, but
he must sacrifice his self-love, his piques, his
jealousies, and his personal interests. Hitherto
New South Wales labour representatives have
shown themselves incapable of the sacrifice, and
hence their comparative failure
* * *
What is the quarrel now ? It is the old
quarrel between the Haves and the Have-nots,
between the Ins and the Outs, between the men
who enjoy pay, place and power and the men
who hunger—yes, it must be said, for it is so
obviously true—to enjoy them. Where does
the Party come in? Nowhere; it is a conflict
between individuals, each fighting for his own
hand. Smith the member, or Smith the dele-
gate, of course wishes that his constituency
should return a Labour representative. But he
wishes much more vehemently that the consti-
tuency should return him. Smith, as its Labour
representative. Smith is quite willing to re-
present Labour, but the thing of primary
importance is the representation of Smith. If
the Party indicates a preference for Jones,
perish the Party !
♦ * *
It seems queer that the Labour representa-
tives in Parliament, who owe their election to
the Labour Leagues, should address their
manifesto, not to the organisations of Labour- 1
voters which put them where they are, but to
“ the electors of New South Wales,” many of
whom tried hard with vote and influence to
prevent them from getting where they are.
They appeal from “ the unrepresentative,
proxy-packed, and largely-self elected Confer-
ence,” not to the Labour Leagues who elected
both themselves and the delegates to the Con-
ference, but to “ the democratic voters of
the country.” That is to say, the Labour-
members propose to waste all the fruit
of the organisation of the past five years,
because they fear that this fruit, formerly
enjoyed by themselves, may now be plucked by
others. They break with the leagues which
have elected them, relying on the personal
influence which their election has gained them.
They kick away the stool on which they have
climbed to power, because they think that
power has made them secure without it. They


a re no longer Labour members, united in a
Labour-party, and wo are back again in the
happy state of things before a Labour-party
existed, when everybody played a lone hand,
and the devil took the hindmost democrat.
• • •
The pledge, vague and impracticable, would
no w appear to be a mere pretext. On
other grounds it is useless, since the straight
man will go straight without a pledge,
and the crooked man will go crooked in spite
of the pledge. Precautions after election
are wasted. All that can be done is to take
precautions before election, make the best pos-
sible choice, and let every mistake teach how
to make a better choice next time. But if the
Labour members were dissatisfied with the
pledge, why did they not appeal from those
grotesquely self-seeking persons, the league
nominees, to the leagues to whom they also owe
allegiance? If they wish to maintain the
Labour-party, why refer the dispute to the
classes outside the Labour-party ? Because, it
would really seem, they distrust the issue, and
prefer rather to wreck the Party than their indi-
vidual chances of re-election. What is the use of
labour-leagues and labour-organisation at all if
the Parliamentary representative members of
the leagues, nominated and elected by the
leagues, choose, at the end of every three years’
term, to throw the leagues overboard and
“appeal to the country ? ”
« • •
A priori, the present representatives of the
Labour-party in Parliament are probably a far
better all-round set of men than any who can
he found to take their place. The reason is
that they have had the benefit of three years’
parliamentary education. They know their
own ignorance far better than those who seek
to supplant them know theirs. Three years
ago their notions of government were just as
crude as those of which to-day they deplore the
advocacy by the new men. But between the
men of three years ago and the men of to-day
there is a great gulf fixed. The Labour repre-
sentatives to-day are mostly wiser and better
men than they were three years ago. Surely
the Labour electors may be trusted to see this.
If the Labour-representatives were loyal to the
Party, they would cheerfully submit themselves
to the decision of the Party. But they fear the
issue of a referendum ; and hence their mani-
festo, not to the Party but “ to the country ”
* * *
The pledge is a mistake and should be frantuy
acknowledged as a mistake. Just as obvious is
toe mistake of seeking to supplant an old
representative by a new one, where the former
retains the confidence of a majority of Labour
(lectors. It cannot be conceded fora moment that
there is any vested interest in the Parliamen-
tary representation of Labour. The member
who is a failure must take the penalty of failure.
If he refuses, he must be fought as a traitor to
the cause. But where an old man has done
well, it is neither just nor expedient to prefer a
new man on the chance that he may be better.
The point is to win the election. If it is toler-
ably certain that the old representative can do
that, let him be unanimously supported, and
et the would-be new representative go and
conquer another constituency from the enemy.
Yet it is plain that, old or new, if a man aspires
to be called a Labour representative, he must
bow to the decision of the majority of
Labour electors, which can only be ascertained
through a’plebiscite of the Labour League con-
cerned. If, in spite of a majority against him,
he fights the election and splits the Party, he
must be denounced without hesitation, and
opposed without mercy. The success of the
individual is nothing : the success of the Party
is everything. The Labour Leagues must
separately decide who shall represent them,
and let minority work with majority to make
the decision final. For without the Leagues
there can be no Party, without a Party no
unity, without unity no success. /
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Protection’s True Purpose.
The stupid old adage that it is possible to have
too much of a good thing, has been laid hold of
by the “moderate tariff” party in Victoria,
and a flood of illogical reasoning threatens to
sweep awajf the important distinction between
duties that protect industries and taxes that
chiefly work for the protection of Government
revenue. A prohibitive tariff in the case of
foreign boots, hats, haberdashery, and all
articles that can be, and are, efficiently manu-
factured on the spot must benefit the country
more than the moderate tariff which allows
thousands of superior people to get their boots
from abroad, paying a fee to Government oh
each pair. The shopkeeper profits no more by
selling British boots than by selling Victorian
ditto, so he doesn’t come within the range of
argument. Say the revenue now gets ss. out
of the imported boots for which the purchaser
pays 30s. fie could buy an equally serviceable
pair of “ colonial-made ” feet coverings for 255.
if there were a prohibitive taififf to stimulate
competition and manufacturing art in the local
trade. The odd five shillings would not be lost to
the State if it remained in his pocket, whilst
the sovereign would unquestionably be devoted
to the support of labour here instead of else-
where. fience the capitalistic cry that Pro-
tection is “overdone” in Victoria can only
justify itself by means of the partial fact that
certain goods have become dearer without any
material increase of local production. It is for
want of full protective encouragement that
there are not more bootmakers, more competi-
tion, more skilful handicraft, and more value
for one’s money in the land. To cheapen the
cost of living in Australia there was no need
for starvation wages and human degradation.
“ e want labour to cheapen itself by acquiring
more skill and cunning, the result of com-
petition which can be secured by the
raising 0 f a H genuine protective duties.
Hitherto the Protection policy of Victoria
has subordinated to the Treasurer’s hunger
mr Customs receipts. Manufacturers were
encouraged to make certain goods, but a
large importation of similar goods was being
looked for and prayed for by the Government
at the same time. Each successive Ministry
has bowed to the opponents of direct taxation
instead of keeping things square by mulcting
Fat Man of the equivalent of the very sum
which he pays as duty upon goods that should
never be imported at all. Let the former
Customs revenue from goods, shut out by a
prohibitive tariff, be replaced by direct taxation
of the class that preferred to pay high duties
upon the imported article rather than buy the
“ colonial make.” Even Fat Man can’t detect
any injustice in the idea. He voluntarily pays
a ss. fee for the privilege of wearing foreign
boots and so forth. Force him to save the ss.
of indirect taxation, and then make direct
application for the two half-crowns.
To “ Oblige ” the Jury.
Re the plundering of the public by their trusted
servant, the Law, it was recently stated in The
Bulletin —“lmportant suits are hung up, to
the great mental and financial distress of
litigants, and the ‘ usual adjournment over
Saturday ’ is, in at least one case out of
every five, a scandalous shame.” A few
hours after the publication whereof, there
arose a question as to whether a Victorian
Court should attempt to hasten the climax of
Hayes v. Australasian libel-case by dis-
pensing with the usual adjournment aforesaid.
On the Thursday afternoon, Chief Justice
Madden blandly enquired if it would be pos-
sible to conclude the case on the following day,
an apparently childish question to put, seeing
that the examination of witnesses had not then
concluded, and the speeches of counsel, not
to mention his Honor’s summing up, would
inevitably be long. Purves, Q. 0., hazarded an
opinion that the case could not be concluded on
Friday, whilst Duffy, for plaintiff, begged to
know whether the Court proposed to sit on
Saturday. The public ought to be well aware,
although they are probably hazy on the subject,
that the Court does as it chooses with regard to
the occupants of the jury-box. An empanelled
juryman finds his attempts to excuse himself
from duty sternly swept away by the Judge.
There are pains and penalties to be visited upon
the lay citizen who absents himself when called
upon, and in effect a juryman is made a slave of
the Court until the case he has to give an
opinion upon is finally disposed of. The jury
must sit on Saturday if his Honor so determine.
Therefore, it was as humorous as usual to hear
Chief Justice Madden affect to relieve himself
of the responsibility for this particular adjourn-
ment by asking whether the jury would prefer
to come back on Saturday or Monday. The
foreman, being possibly a tradesman with his
little shop on his mind—it doesn’t matter what
he may have been—replied “ Monday.” So the
Chief Justice without a quiver of his learned
eyelid, said, “Then we will finish this case on
Monday, and I will adjourn the Criminal
Court,” after which the mob of lawyers gathered
up their papers and—it being then four o’clock
—gaily dispersed. The “ usual Saturday ”
suspension of business was arranged by the
legal trade, but it was made to appear
that the jury foreman had settled the matter,
whereas there are numerous precedents for
assuming that in the event of the foreman hav-
ing said “ Saturday, by all means,” his desire
for a speedy conclusion would have been over-
ruled by the objections of one or more counsel
present. In a word, the Court expected the
foreman to vote for a postponement into another
week, and he fulfilled anticipation. If anyone
doubts the absurdity of this solemn farce let
him consider what would happen to a jury who
expressed a wish to adjourn over a Friday and
sit on the Saturday instead. Then we could see
that a jury’s convenience is as naught to the
Law, unless the Law wishes to use it as a cloak
for one of the lawyers’ own time-honoured,
arbitrary customs.
♦
Libel-law Lunacy.
The libel-action brought by jockey Jimmy
Hayes against the Australasian newspaper
has ended in a “ draw,” and each side pays its
own hundreds or thousands of pounds of costs.
This result is not much more unsatisfactory
than that of the first Speight v. the Age case
which was brought to a miserable termination
by a three-fourths verdict upon one out of
eleven separate counts. As regards the re-
maining ten-elevenths of the railway libel-suit
the jury failed to agree. Jockey Hayes pro-
ceeded on one count only, so the odds were 10
to 1 against a verdict considering that the
Judge’s summing-up was so vague and be-
fogging in the latter instance as in the former.
Before railing at these two notable examples
of jury incompetence—in both of which the
jury did certainly proclaim their dulness
through their foreman—it behoves one to read
the charge of the respective judges to the men
they are popularly supposed to guide and
direct. Then there appears to be much excuse
for the lack of common determination shown
by the representatives of Liberty’s palladium.
The guidance and direction vouchsafed by Jus-
tice Hodges to his jury, and by Chief Justice
Madden to his jury, were equally perplexing
and provocative of a conflict of opinion. Both
juries were given to understand that damaging
newspaper statements, if unproved, would con-
stitute a libel on the damaged party ; also, both
juries were informed that a libel might be fair
comment, under which circumstance it was not
libellous, and neither jury could possibly derive
a distinct understanding of fair comment from
the Judge’s definition of the legal meaning of
the term. Judge Hodges, in the Speight case,
succeeded in fuddling nine jurymen until they
found that statements could be libellous,
untrue, and fair comment at one and the
same time. Judge Madden’s success in
the same direction was made clear by the
foreman, who enquired of him : “ Supposing
the jury agree to a verdict that the particulars
are not proven, but agree that it was fair com-
ment formed on insufficient knowledge without
malice, what would be the effect of such a ver-
dict?” When the learned Justice assured the
jury that such a finding would be “a perfectly
good verdict for the defendant ” they went
back to their room and finally decided not to
give a verdict of any description whatever.
Seemingly they, like the other lot of arbiters,
were fuddled by the “ fair comment ” enigma.
They might have agreed that the Austra-
lasian had with insufficient knowledge of
facts made statements which the evidence
failed to prove, but they could not agree as
to where fair comment began or ended.
Ere dismissing this non-productive jury,
their guide and director laid stress upon the
sorrow he felt for their failure to find a decision.
He might well have wept in open court over
this latest exhibition of costly folly in libel-
law as it is expounded to average mortals by
Victorian judges. A law more profitable to
lawyers and unprofitable to the public could
not have been invented to invite ridicule. An
interesting law to watch in its course through a
number of other libel-cases now pending. A
desirable kind of law, as far as newspapers are
concerned, and therefore not a law to be
despised in print. No intelligent, honest editor
would allow his paper to publish the damaging
statements of an untrustworthy contributor
upon any matter of public importance, and the
Law seems to suggest that fair comment is the
comment of a commentator who can show
reasonable cause for believing his comment to
be fair. But, perhaps, this ruling applies par-
ticularly to “ great ” journalism.

The Luck of Lazarus.
A London paper, professedly Radical in senti-
ment, has come to the comfortable conclusion
that to die from hunger is one of the best pos-
sible ways of effecting a decease. “Hunger,”
says the editor, “ is only an intimation that the
time has come for the recurrent meal. If the
meal does not come, the hunger passes away
and a lowering of the vital functions follows, as
the result of which, if the privation continues,
all sensation is deadened. By many of us the
stigma of pauperism, the fear of the workhouse,
is thought to be the restraining motive with
these poor people who give no hint to their
necessities, I believe the explanation to be
this, that under a course of privation they
really lose all desire to live, all initiative, all
sensation, physical or intellectual, and allow
themselves to die because it is the easiest and
pleasantest course.” Toryism, pure and simple,
could suggest no better excuse for leaving the
struggling poor to their fate. Feed a hungry
man and you fill him with a harassing desire to
get more vittles and live on. Chase the lean
petitioner from your doorstep and his hungerpre-
sently passes away, giving place to a sweet and
gentle lowering of his vital functions. He loses
all physical sensation of rats inside, and all
intellectual disposition to curse the existing
state of society, and, retiring to his heap of
dirty straw in the far corner of a damp cellar,
he chooses the pleasant course of pegging-out.
A beautiful expression lights up his protuberant
cheek-bones, and he makes an enviable depar-
ture to the Better Land. What an example for
Fat Man, who is apt to prolong his death-bed
sufferings by picking at sweetbreads and swill-
ing dry champagne 1 In his wealthy ignorance
he misses the happiness of following the easiest
course, and he probably chokes himself with an
oyster at the finish, instead of collapsing in an
enjoyable manner upon an emaciated stum-
mick. Positively, the poor have much to be
thankful for when they get nothing at all.
Mr. Richard Teece “had no hesitation in saying
that those who passed through Sydney University
were the minds which in the future would guide
the province and be the masters of its destiny.”
Mr, Teece shouldn’t be too sure. The state-
ment that a university, as a matter of neces-
sity, turns out “master minds” is not borne
out by experience. University ism is pedagogy ;
pedagogy is grooved ignorance. The university
system abhors originality and kills it wherever
possible. The average collegiate is merely a
learned pig who doesn’t understand how or why
he euchres his opponent. As a matter of fact,
he very often fails to euchre his opponent.
Somehow, the “master-minds of the com-
munity ” rarely get to the university. The
drones send their spawn to the universities,
and the spawn-entities develop into learned
fools, who gabble but don’t comprehend, who
become doctors that fill graveyards, lawyers
who damn cases, legislators who bungle laws,
financiers who ruin millions, and alleged
“masterminds” every one. It is astonishing
how little the university really does for the
strong man—the man who was born to fight
and to sharpen his sword on his own teeth.
But the social-caste stamp of the university is
invaluable to the weakling ; for to hold a
university degree is to have spats on your
mental boots, and to have the trousers of your
brain tucked up in public.
New South Wales Premier Dibbs’ Banking
Bills didn’t go quite so far as to make tram-
tickets legal tender, yet it is a common occur-
rence in provincial State schools, these hard times,
for three dilapidated “ trams ” to be tendered
in payment of the weekly school-fee. Under
the Act provision is made for scholars, whose
parents are unable to pay for their education,
being placed on the Exemption List. Many,
however, to whom the relief would be a god-
send, are unaware of the fact, and when unable
to pay the 3d. fee they keep their children at
home. Others, duly acquainted with the relief
clauses of the Act, steadily set their faces
against branding their children paupers. It is
the undemocratic system of payment for com-
pulsory State education which makes such a
stigma possible. Few people apply for
exemption from school-fees until absolutely
forced; which fact makes sad reading of
the late heavy returns under this head.
Another eloquent commentary on the times
is the ragged condition of a number of the
pupils who, awhile back, were well, even to
smartly, clad. Then further down the social
scale —the children of the poor —poor in times
which are known as “good,” and how im-
measurably poorer these dull days ! It is a
literal fact that many of these are sent to school
insufficiently fed. Small wonder, then, that
complaints are frequent in large schools of
lunches being stolen ! And when this happens
and the culprit is caught it is usual for him to
be caned before tlie whole school—presumably
as an awful warning against becoming hungry.
This is very like censuring God for having built
a boy with a stomach. Besides, it proclaims
the boy “Thief” before the whole school, and
children, like their elders, have a trick of living
up to an ugly name.
Editor Bulletin,— Perhaps one reason why
the housekeepers, mentioned by Paul Blunt
in yours of last week, preferred to deal with
Chinese vegetable-vendors is that, compared to
the average “ white” hawker, the heathen is a
gentleman. Don’t start, or think of justifiable
homicide 1 Did you ever observe the harmless
necessary John dealing with the “ missis ” or
the housemaid ? If you are not absolutely
blinded by prejudice, you cannot have failed to
observe the dignity with which he bears him-
self. He never (or very rarely) loses patience,
he bears each housewife’s little peculiarities of
taste carefully in mind; he is honest, punctual,
reliable; he never presents himself under the
influence of liquor, is never offensive in his
choice of language, and is, comparatively, cleanly
in person. Now, no true-hearted woman could
fail to appreciate these good qualities. And if
you add one great charm, which only an
observant male would notice, you will have the
full secret of John’s popularity. Every white
woman is a goddess in John’s eyes; and his
manner to her is as full of innocent admiration
as a child’s, as full of delicate chivalry as a
poet’s to his mistress. And this bearing of
admiration and respect is absolutely free from
the least touch of grossness. These are the
virtues which make John Chinaman popular
with women in the capacity of a hawker.
And then, every true-hearted woman is merci-
ful and humane. She hears John, her husband,
violently abusing Chinamen and charging them
with all sorts of faults and vices. She
looks at John, her hawker, and she has a
dim (or strong) perception of the truth. She
knows that her husband is a man, she
loves him and reveres his very failings—
but, in her illogical woman’s way, she is
kind to the victim of her man’s injustice
and race-prejudices. It is true that those pre-
judices are instinctive, necessary, and, viewing
the possibilities of the future, perhaps even
laudable. But the woman sees the human
being, and (let us thank God for it) her woman-
liness overpowers other considerations. Do you
remember the old story of our school-bag days
about the ancient negresswho succoured Mungo
Park, and under what circumstances ? Every
Chinaman in our midst is liable, in a measure,
to repeat the experiences of Park. —Yours, &c,,
Bill Sikes.
A long-drawn-out and perennially-recurrent
conflict has been waged at Broken Hill over
the question of holding a special audit of the
municipal accounts. Over a year ago a petition
demanding the audit, under section 184 of the
Municipalities Act, was forwarded to the
Sydney authorities. The council, ostensibly on
the score of economy, set itself to thwart the
movement. It was found that sufficient of the
signatories were non-ratepayers to reduce the
number of qualified petitioners below 50, the
minimum, and the two special auditors nomi-
nated by Government in terms of the petition,
who had travelled to Broken Hill from Sydney,
were refused access to the council’s books.
The next petition was similarly proved in-
formal. Meanwhile, the desire of the council
to burke the audit convinced numbers of rate-
payers, previously indifferent, of its necessity,
and the third petition was regular. The coun-
cil, as a dernier ressort, called a meeting which,
it was hoped, “ all citizens having the welfare
of the town at heart” would attend. They
did; the set motions were rejected, the Mayor
left the chair in disgust, and under a new chair-
man the meeting solemnly sanctioned the audit
and wired the special auditors to come along.
Also, it decided that the cost would be £lOl3
13s. (54 days, eight hours, at £1 Is. each
auditor per hour ! with travelling and hotel
charges ! 1), instead of £3734 (33 weeks, with
“ exes ”) as estimated by the council. The
municipality, which is already miserably stone-
broke and can afford neither sum, had recently
to dismiss all its day labourers consequent on a
sudden demand by its bankers for payment of
an unsecured overdraft. It is at present liqui-
dating this at the rate of £5OO monthly, and is
paying interest on another of £12,000. The
persistency of the demand for the audit is evi-
dence of the falling-off of popular faith in the
infallibility of the orthodox brand of auditor
consequent on recent financial revelations. In
this case the books of the council have been, as
usual, audited half-yearly by the annually-
elected officers. Revision may be necessary,
but the law which permits fifty ratepayers,
who may be stupidly obstinate or influenced by
persons having sinister motives, to harass a
stone-broke municipality to the extent of pay-
ing at the rate of £1 Is. per auditor per hour,
with expenses, for such revision is simply
monstrous.
The idea that God is an Englishman with a
preference for Englishmen and a yearning to
spend all his time in meddling with English
affairs, is rapidly going the way of all the other
crude blasphemies with which ignorant barbar-
ism has disfigured rational conceptions of deity.
It lingers in England, of oqurse; it will die
hard, no doubt; but its doom is nevertheless
sealed. “No man with a sense of humour ever
founded a religion ; ” and the obtrusive profes-
sors of piety are just as dense as the founders.
No one seems to have been struck with the
grotesque indecency of Queen Victoria’s action
in calling upon the “ Father, Son, and Holy
Ghost ” to help her as minor assistants at the
knighting of the hard-swearing ex-Republican
Dibbs. But this lately happened in England.
And when Justice Stephen sentenced burglars
Montgomery and Williams to death for
hurting a constable’s head, he either omitted
the customary shibboleth “ May God have
mercy on your soul,” or the papers omitted to
report it. The Bulletin hopes the former was
the case. Nine times out of ten the Judge
doesn’t believe that criminals have a soul, and
doesn’t care what becomes of the soul, if there
is a soul; and the hypocrisy of inviting the
Creator and Ruler of the Universe to save the
soul of the man whom, arrogating to yourself
the functions of God Almighty, you are cold-
bloodedly butchering, is indeed revolting hypo-
crisy.
