My Dear Moorabinda—
Q’sland society was lately somewhat astonished
when a lady whose stuccoed mansion adorns a
most irreproachable suburb, introduced her two
daughters into a law-court to defend a suit brought
by a fashionable milliner suing for about six
pounds. At the same time, though, the society
madam showed her acuteness in thus trotting the
dear girls into the witness-box, for the lawyer who
complained for plaintiff didn’t dare to cross-
examine two pretty maidens who had but to open
their eyes at him to reduce him to stammering
apologies, and, of course, plaintiff lost the case.
Had she thought to introduce two pretty mil-
liners into her side of the question, the battle
might have been waged for weeks. However,
six pounds versus leaving two youthful damsels
to the uncertain mercies of horsehair really looks
as if nowadays society thought quite as much of
its ducats, as of its daughter. Nevertheless and
notwithstanding, let the Right prevail.
I really didn’t mean to overlook the departure
of Captain W. H. St. Clair, R.N., who was
“senior naval officer in charge of the steam
reserves and naval establishments at Sydney
station ” —an arduous-sounding post, which, nath-
less, left its slave heaps of time to turn up at small
tea-parties in the afternoon, and take a working-
interest in private theatricals. I once saw him
play a bluff sort of r6le very well —if he had only
known what to do with his hat.
Anyhow, he has gone ’Ome, where all the good
officials go eventually, no matter how “ glorious ”
they find this country, and tars from the warships
dragged his vehicle to the railway-station, while
ignorant passers-by peered into the carriage to get
a glimpse of the bride —for there generally is a
scarlet-cheeked virgin on promotion in a circus of
that kind. Bub alone the oap’n departed, and
Sydney society is once more on the qui vive. The
authorities can’t shuffle Imperial officers often
enough—within a certain limit—to suit Australian
belledom on the matrimonial warpath.
Let us be joyful that the trustees of Sydney,
Melbourne, and Adelaide Art Galleries have
finally agreed to exchange their little stocks-in-
trade from time to time. Sydney, I am
sure, is more than willing to barter the
“Queen of Sheba” for “ Ulysses and the Sirens ”
with their hair blowing against the wind.
It is economical, too, for instead of waiting till
“ our London buyer ” has paid four figures for a
champion oleograph ere we can hope for novelty
in the catalogue, we will have movement on the
walls merely for the trouble of borrowing it.
Besides which, imagine the chances of somebody’s
brolly getting run through a few highly-coloured
canvasses that never would be missed as they
were en route? There could be no better way of
destroying the evidences of the utter lack of
discernment in Blank (trustee) than by arranging
with a porter to spill his gorgeously-framed
“national purchase” under the first available
gravel-train.
On dit that a Southern newspaper is about to be
sued for libel by the “society” wife of a boom-
ster. The lady in question, whose gowns have
frequently been detailed in the paper she
threatens (which, to my mind, fully accounts for
her implacableness), is perfectly sure of getting a
verdict. “My case is good in any of the Courts,”
she said, ecstatically, to a friend ; “I have danced
with all of the Judges !”
And therein lies another proof of how cosy a
billet it is to be a woman. Could the festive bank-
clerk but have a schottische with his Honor just
before the accounts went wrong, who knows how
slender his prison bars might be? And imagine
the effect g. previous polka might have on even the
sternest Judge ! The law can have few terrors for
a woman wiio readily remembers that the horse-
hair-swamped apparition seated severely on the
Bench had really such a nice way of holding one in
a waltz.
N.S.W. Horticultural Society’s ’Mum Show
happened at Sydney Town Hall, the other Thurs-
day, when Miss Duff was billed to make her first
appearance as a show-opener, and didn’t dis-
appoint the moist crowd that braved a wicked
barometer to see her do it. But like mamma like
daughter, and Miss Duff—looking as if death
were sweeter —backed up by her sisters and
governess and the Homeric Galton, merely
whispered to a blue-ribboned committee-man to
set the flowery proceeding going, and there was
an end to it.
It strikes me as being a stupid thing to yard
up a bread-and-butter miss just because she is the
Governor’s daughter and pose her before the
nation merely as another exhibit to attract the
general public and adorn the advts. If the
juvenile patroness could do more than twirl her
bouquet and look helpless there might be some
sense in offering her up in the absence of the usual
official martyrs, but as the hapless maiden is
on view merely as a gaping-stock and to appease
the vanity of committees, the whole thing looks
ridiculous. By-the-by, when “Miss Duff and
suite” entered the hall the National Anthem
blared forth, although the strict official etiquette
would have substituted some other entrance
music. People won’t learn that “Gorsave” is
sacred to His Ex. only because they’d rather not.
Any sprig of vice-royalty is a good enough excuse
to advertise the presence of Govt. House in the
neighbourhood with the sounding chords that
bring “loyal” audiences to their hind legs with a
jump.
Otherwise, the ’Mum show was done very well
this Autumn, though I rather think the followers of
Adam are sacrificing daintiness to abnormal dimen-
sions in these aromatic winter flowers. Size isn’t
necessarily beauty—except to Chinamen—and
why our gardeners should set themselves to work
to rear things which look like skye-terriers on
stalks and glow with pride in the presence of a
bloom straddles a soup-plate is folk-lore to
me. Evidently the ’mum isn’t meant for personal
adornment. I can’t think of anybody—barring
Johnny Lucas—who could sport one of these big,
bold blooms and have his boutonniere taken
seriously. But, stop!--yes, I do. He is a delight-
ful pianist, and, in point of figure, might be
Arthur Orton’s own brother.
City-organist Wiegand has nearly come to the
end of his tether as Grand Shah of Sydney’s big
organ, but there is no fear (or hope, as the crowd
of disappointed organ-grinders hungry for the
post would put it) of bis leaving Botany Bay to
the tender mercies of more or less brilliant local
talent. Mayor Manning has decreed that his
position is “ practically permanent;” therefore;
Wiegand we have always with us—bar accidents.
At the same time, I think other people might,
now and then, be allowed to play upon the un-
manageable instrument, if only to show who is
really master of it. Organist Best, the white-
fisted bon viveur, made the gigantic musical-box
sound like a riot in a tin tank, with occasional
intervals of asthmatic coughing, and disappointed
Sydney at the opening of the instrument.
Wiegand is the only person who has ever reduced
the big organ to melody—though the frozen-out
geniuses of Sydney will tell you that they haven’t
had the chance, of course. Apart from ttie artistic
aspect of Mons. Wiegand, he is also a financial
success. In 300 recitals he has made .£BOOO.
A second visit to the unvisited “Mrs. Tan*
queray No. 2,” at Sydney Lyceum, brings trifles
into prominence that one doesn’t notice to begin
with. For instance, it is a lucky thing that doors
take the place of portieres in the T. mansion,
because Mrs. T. is so addicted to banging ’em
that if she had to flounce out through silently-
drooping curtains her wrongs wouldn’t be half
appreciated by the feminine part of the house.
By the by, they say it is only a perfect Brute of
a husband who takes all the portals off their
hinges and hangs up noiseless rag instead, leaving
his helpless victim nothing to argue with.
A touch of nature in “Mrs. Tanqueray ” ia
where Mr. T. gently disappears from the scene in
the second act when the two women are quarrel-
ling. A man always flees from the feminine
hysterics to come even when he has raised the
whirlwind—which is, on the whole, the wisest
thing to do. People who intervene between a pair
of angry women generally achieve the fate of King
Laurent the Second, who receives Fiametta’s
blows meant for Bettina in his own “bread-
basket if I may lapse into the lingo of fistiana.
Australia isn’t thoroughly broken in to un-
alleviated misery in its theatrical moments—it
still secretly longs for the joyful denouement that
makes melodrama so supernatural. At “Mrs.
Tanqueray,” the other evening, I was amused at
a woman in my neighbourhood who settled her-
self comfortably for the two-minute wait—before
the last scene, and said complacently, “There’s a
lot to clear up in one act, isn’t there?” She
wanted Mrs. T. to appear as the guest of the
county by the simple, melodramatic explanation
at the eleventh hour that it was her twin sister
who navigated Peter Jermyn’s yacht, I suppose.
Or else there was lacking an apotheosis—Paula
going up into the flies in a stained-glass attitude
between two night-gowney angels with wadding
wings. Evidently naked realism isn't femininely
appreciated.
By the way, I am lost in admiration at the
staying powers of Mrs. Brough’s hair-pins. Not
one actress in a barrel-full could romp through
Mrs. T. (a r6le that, a well-known actress opined
to me, would take Bernhardt and Ellen Terry
rolled into one to strike electric sparks out of)
like Mrs. Brough does without one curl escaping.
Mrs. Potter’s hair—you remember—was always
between her teeth in moments of histrionic frenzy,
and Bernhardt’s golden tresses used to come down
as naturally as a washerwoman’s. Mrs. Bernard-
Beere’s scanty locks were always about to tumble,
and, somehow, one looks for loose love-locks in
the more frisky heroines of the drama. It may be
that Mrs. Brough’s neatly-barbered poll detracts
from the abandon of her mumming—but, all the
same, I can’t help wondering how she keeps her
hair on.
A little story illustrative of the divided sym-
pathies of the sweet girl graduate. A very clever
science-studentess lately became engaged to a
bachelor of science, and almost in the same breath
gained a place in the honour-list—but in the
second-class. On commemoration-day one of the
he-students met her, and, wishing to felicitate her
upon her engagement, began to reel off the in-
evitable, “Allow me to offer my congratu ”
when the lady calmly interrupted him: “Oh,
thanks, hut of course it was only a second-class
affair, you know—better though, I suppose, than
none at all ? ” As the scientific fianc£ is a
nervous and myopic youth who wears overproof
spectacles and seems undecided what to do with
his feet, the he-student wonders right up to this
moment what she really meant.
Many a man owes his success to the possession
of a pretty wife. A high-up Sydney official, lately
asked by a very charming woman indeed for an
appointment for out-of-work hubby, answered :
“Tell him to be at my office at 2.30 p.m. to.
morrow, and I will do something for him for your
sake, dear Mrs. .” Next day, at 2.40, the
lady was surprised by a visit from the sympathetic
one. “ Why,” said she, “Mr. has gone to
your office” “Just so; and now we shall be
able to have a nice quiet chat together.” That is
portion of the lady’s story, anyhow.
A quarrel between His Ex. of Thereabouts, up
North, and the private secretary is more than a
mere difference of opinion over the spelling of a
word, for the secretary has been a blighted l being
who cannot get into even the outskirts of the
master’s train since weeks, aye, months agone.
The very desirable way of viewing the country,
which a vice - regal jaunt to some distant
butter-factory or other presents, has not been
open to the p.s., who now mournfully tails-up the
ladies of the household when they go abroad
officially, and otherwise fritters his secretaryship
away in more poodle-work than usually falls to
the lot of even a Government House attache.
“Pipple say” that his Excellency—who isn’t
exactly the rage on his official dunghill—has ceased
to smile upon his quill-driver on that very account.
There may be faults on both sides, after all, and it
is plainly the duty of an able-bodied secretary to
go on his marrow-bones, if the individual under
his care turns restive, and use every imploring
argument in the dictionary to bring his man up
to time. It strikes me that the p.s. is somewhat
to blame when a Governor makes too big a fool of
himself, or freezes off his subjects by some easily-
avoidable blunder. A really smart secretary
should regulate a Governor’s manner as well as his
mail-bag, and model him to suit his public whether
the great man knows it or not. The price of that
sort of secretary would be about =£2ooo a year.
And his value would be <£sooo.
I hear that what promised to be a very peppery
scandal, had it got into horse-hairdom, was hushed-
up down South recently by the good sense of the
wife—the stylish and musical better half of a pro-
fessional man who was lured from the track
of virtue by the Hebe of a historical hotel.
Historical because it was there, my child,
that a lordling since fell upon evil times,
and I hear that the self-same shanty caused a well-
known squatter many a sleepless night, not long
ago. In the present case the scandal was averted.
What a peptonized world it would be if every
wife had the common-sense to go and do likewise !
Somebody suggests, in the S.M. Herald, that
a howling want in Sydney is a home, with a
lethal chamber attached, for stray dogs. But
what is more wanted is a lethal chamber for stray
men. I wonder just how many Domain denizens
there are at this very moment who, with the rain,
damp grass, and tightened waist-belt of hungry
winter staring them in the face, would walk
into the death - dealing atmosphere cheerfully
conscious that they would be carried out of it the
mere husk of an angel, so to speak ? Death hasn’t
many fancy terrors for a hungry man whose hair
is sticking out of the crown of his hat.
Yours faithfully,
SAPPHO SMITH.
Melbourne Confidences.
Melbourne, April 24, 1894.
The gilt-buttoned officers of the Austrian war-
ship Fasana have been flitting from one feminine
flower to another in true naval butterfly style, and
their knowledge of “English as she is spok ”
being tolerably up to date, they have managed to
tell their love with pleasing results.
A lieutenant on the Saida (an Austrian cruiser
which visited Melb. last year) used to sigh over a
misadventure that befel him at an up-country
Victorian station through the short-comings of his
English and the mistaken kindness of his hostess.
When invited to partake of any dish he always
contended himself with replying “Please” if he
meant “Yes,” and “Thank you” when he in-
tended “No.” The evening of his arrival he was
asked to take a second cup of tea and declined in
his usual formula, “Thank you.” Nevertheless,
his cup was replenished, so not wishing to be un-
gracious, he disposed of its contents, only to be
offered a further supply. Though particular on
this occasion to distinctly enunciate his refusal,
his wishes were misinterpreted for the second time!
Anxious to infringe no rule of Australian table
etiquette, the swelling hero lapped up six liberal
jorums of netve-destroyer, bestowed upon him
despite many heartfelt “Thank you’s.” At last
he could stand it no longer, when, for the seventh
time, he beheld an inexorable hand extended
towards his cup, he exclaimed, beseechingly and
almost with tears: “Thank you!” “Thank
you /” “ Please, no !” “ No, please 1 The cup is
large, and I am small!”
The “Fasana” is being kept in countenance by
“ the Kreisser ” with its “ Russian bears,” a mis-
leading title, the braves in question being, like
most of their nation, polite to a degree.
Some years ago, I’m told, a Russian cruiser was
leaving its anchorage at Albany, and making its
way out of the harbour to the strains of the
national hymn, sung by the crew. It was early
morning, and the local Government Resident,
taking his daily ride on the beach with some
friends, came under the notice of the ship’s com-
mander, who immediately caused his men to
change their tune into “ God Save the Queen,” as
a neat little farewell compliment to their late
entertainers. Could politeness any further go ?
One of the pretty sisters, who returned from
Europe by a recent P. and 0., is engaged to a
young squatter of the Stawell neighbourhood,
about as eligible a parti as any girl could desire!
His condition of orphanhood (all in his favour as
any bride will testify), added to the circumstance
of his <£200,000, stamps his capture as the matri-
momal event of the season. Though not more
than twenty-three summers have left their traces
on his open brow, the bridegroom-elect would, no
doubt,_ have been quite a confirmed benedict by
this time had not a constitutional bashfulness
been his safeguard against feminine wiles. So
marked was this idiosyncracy, that the appear-
ance of a petticoated vision bearing down affec-
tionately upon his sisters’ Toorak abode, was
always the signal for his hasty exit by a back
entrance. However, fate overtook him at last
and the days of his liberty are numbered—indeed*
he has already done a trial trip round the
continent in gilded hobbles.
It is said that nobody is so useful as to be in-
dispensable. There are always ten pairs of feet
itching to fill one pair of vacated shoes, which is
perhaps the reason why a matron nowadays, when
she feels premonitory symptoms of cold or head-
ache, summons her husband to her bedside and
conjures him, by all he holds sacred, to reveal the
name of the destined Number Two. To give the
desired information would probably be the best
way of hastening the sufferer’s convalescence, but
one never hears of a man venturing to try so
drastic a remedy 1 Maybe “ the husband famine”
is responsible for this state of affairs. Formerly
(I am told) a woman, when she had once bagged
her matrimonial bird, took no further trouble
about him, as even were he friskily inclined, she
knew that very few girls would exert themselves
to dance to his piping.
In these overwomaned days, however, there are
belles galore, ready to practise their little wiles
upon men for lack of profitable sport, so
that an affectionate and watchful wife has more
worry and bother shepherding her dear John
(stout and baldish though he be) than ever she
experienced when he was an active Romeo, with
the escape door still open to him. In the time of
glamour he felt no desire to slip away, probably.
All the same, there’s not more than a grain of
truth in most of what’s said about the “ Husband
Famine.” One certainly sees bevies of unattached
maidens everywhere, but most of them will be
appropriated in the end. The fact that people
don’t “set up house ” so early in life as they used
to accounts to some extent for the apparent glut
of marriageable females and obstinately celibate
men.
The girls of now don’t leave school until 19 or
20, whereas 40 years ago a spinster of the latter
age was looked upon as drawing perilously near
“the shelf.” Novelists, who are generally behind
the times, still swear by heroines in their teens.
Hamilton Clarke (late leader of Melb. orchestra)
in his book, “ Two Chorus Girls,” only attributed
14 summers to the leading lady of the story. Yet,
I imagine that Mrs. Ham. 0. was more than twice
seven when he lured her from the stage.
In Westralia, long ago, the girls (most of them
the daughters of uppercrust pioneer families) were
brought out at 15 and married off the following
year.
When in the Perth diocese a devout Anglican
Archdeacon (still to the fore) chose a helpmeet of
“ frolicsome 14 ” no one thought it odd, though
nowadays bishops, clergy and laity would raise a
concerted moan at the bare mention of such green
fruit-stealing. The Archdeaconess in question
soon fell into her groove, though the first batch of
callers heard a good deal of mysterious scuffling
outside the drawing-room door. Their dignified
pastor, as it turned out, was forcibly assisting his
bashful bride into the presence of “ company;”
she, meantime, trying all she knew to hark back
to her interrupted pastimes of story-book reading
and biscuits.
Talking of successors being raised up, young
men will find balm in the thought that the
marriage of the Werribee heiress need not ring
the curtain down upon hymeneal hope. A hand-
some girl, with an annual income of ,£5OOO already
and unconditionally her own, should be a comfort-
ing circumstance to the weary fortune-hunter.
This lucky damsel, not yet out of the school-room,
is the only child and sole heiress of an old cele-
brity who died at Daylesford the other week, and
subsequently “ lay in state ’’ in the local Anglican
church. Happiness and a barouche and pair are
in store for some future drawer of the winning
ticket.
Endowed womanhood doesn’t always have a
rosy time, as shown by the anxiety of a local pro-
fessional man’s rich wife to annul her marriage,
on the grounds of alleged cruelty, desertion, &c.
Before her union she was the belle of an
up-country town, the effect of her beaux ycux
being backed up by the eclat of a moneyed father,
and she chose the partner of her affluence from a
host of admirers in pouting opposition to the
wishesof herfriends. The cominglaw-suit willbe by
no means the first occasion on which this litigious
couple have aired their disagreements. For a long
time the guardianship of their two children, who,
by the terms of their maternal grandfather’s will!
are well worth clinging to, provided a bone of
contention; the youngsters in the end being
handed over, by law, to the male parent’s care.
Re the Australian Judge who required “ a
governess or companion for his only daughter,”
his advertisement caused quite a flutter at the
time. It was worded so insinuatingly that any
number of high-toned though reconstructed
maidens bombarded him with crested and mono-
grammed applications. The advertiser’s location
was specified, so if (as hinted by a recent press-
letter) the whole affair was a fishing-expedition,
the question arises, which of the Judges in that
locality was the joker ? One of them might have
been capable of it once , but he had his wig combed
down a while ago.
A bachelor bank-manager at Beechworth was,
so I hear, lately astounded by a rush of neatly-
written, elegantly-worded offers to undertake the
education of his “little boy.” A facetious friend
had inserted an advt. in the Argus representing
the banker as a widower with a small son crying
out for instruction; hence all the “sport.” Un-
employed, hard-up governesses are the worst
people in the world at appreciating practical
jokes—in fact, fun of this description is pretty
well death to some of them. To awake from
dreams of lonely bankers and possible contin-
gencies to the cold, hard fact that she has wasted
another postage-stamp, is a bitter trial for any
spinster in distress.
Before long, unless an educational advt. states
that there are 12 children in the family requiring
Parisian French, plain needlework, and co’nstant
Christian forbearance from their governess at the
rate of 10s. per dozen weekly, it will be viewed
with suspicion, and shunned as a brilliant snare.
It is pleasant to hear that Bayley, of “Bayley’s
Reward,” came back to Victoria, after his lucky
find, and showered benefits on his delighted
relatives, exactly like the returned Nabob of
polite fiction.
Two of}fhis brothers he rescued from backblock
State schools, and fixed up comfortably at a
station near Ballarat. In calm contemplation
of their flocks and herds they are learning to
forget their early struggles with the native intel-
and the dark shadow of the blackboard is
passing _ away from the redeemed teachers.
Bayley is the sort of brother who deserves to
discover a gold-mine.
Theannounoementof Madame Carandini’s death,
cabled from England, last Monday, must have
brought thoughts of other years to many old Aus-
tralians. Long, long ago the Carandinis used to
make professional tours throughout Australia,
visiting out of the way towns which had never been
similarly favoured. The children in such places
derived their first ideas of public entertainments
and professional singers from these concerts, and
enlightened the monotony of existence for months
afterwards by playing at “ the Carandinis,”
steeping scraps of red ribbon in water to obtain
the necessary rouge and squalling, “ Take care

of kerosene-tins. For many years Madame lived
at Windsor (Melb.), her artistically-furnished cot-

and curios (souvenirs of visits to her daughter,

Poor Mr. M‘Kay, victirnof the Barraba bank out-
rage, was a brilliant pianist, and so popular an
official that he never left a town to take charge of
another branch without receiving a presentation.
His wife, the daughter of a former Melb. Mayor,
and sister of the wife of Dr. Hewlett, of CJarltou
(Vic.), has always shared in her husband’s popu-
larity. Before, her marriage she was a fine-looking
girl, known in the neighbourhood of her up-
country home as “the snake-huntress,” from her
skill in tracking down those reptiles and killing
hem in deft blaoktellow-fashion.
The wife of a certain Australian potentate, being
the daughter of a millionaire, is necessarily very
mean. “ Can’t you compose a few waltzes ? ” she
said to the girls’ music-teacher, “it would be
cheaper than wasting money buying them at the
shop.” Apropos, a big Melbourne inusic firm has
just gone insolvent.
An afternoon-tea shop has been opened in Mel-
bourne by an ex-society lady. Heaps of girls are
learning dressmaking and others millinery. Pre-
sently the poor professionals will have to turn
governesses, a calling becoming less popular every
year. Society has not recruited the ranks of
coffee-palace waitresses yet, but is only awaiting
financial developments.
Art is at such a low ebb in Australia that one
painter who has given to the world some most
excellent portraiture feels obliged to desert paint-
ing for pastry, lining decided to open a pie-shop
at Coolgardie. At one time he would not have
been regarded as an ineligible suitor for a gilded
heiress. In the heyday of his popularity he used
to cook dainty little dishes for his chums, and his
experience as an amateur chef may serve him well
in the Westralian venture.

The Wild Cat Column.
The principal cause of Matthew Henry Davies s
schedule has been “ the failure of various financial
institutions in which insolvent had invested large sums
of money and was a shareholder.” The poor fellow is
another of the many victims of those wretched land-boom
onnpanies.
» * * *
Joseph Bartlett Davies has followed the lead of
Brother Matthew by filing his schedule —Assets, £BO,OOO ;
liabilities, £593,000. J.B.D.’s schedule is supposed to
come as a financial shock to the queer community of
Melbourne. In that paradise of respectable double-
shuffling the insolvency of a small tradesman, or clerk,
occurs in the natural order of things as soon as he fails to
meet his bills, w hereas the bloated bank-bleeder can owe
half a-raillion and yet keep bankruptcy at bay until it
s fits his purpose to swagger into Court and pay pence
i i the £ for an unconditional release. Which reminds
The Bulletin that Judge Molesworth had “much
pleasure” in releasing that “unfortunate victim of cir-
cumstances,”.Mr. James Munro, when he produced his
little sixpence. The Court’s sympathy for Messrs. M. and
J. B. Davies may be expected to ensue. J. B. Davies
reports that his “losses" since October, 1891, have
amounted to £618,000. His “deficiency" is now
£513,000, ergo J. B. Davies might have settled up his
accounts 2i years ago, leaving himself with a credit
balance of £105,000 or so. What hideous nonsense these
land-boomers inflict upon the ignorant!

hadn't left the Courts, and it was taken hot to the

Lawyer Higgins advances the ghastly theory
that there was nothing exceptional in the Melbourne
management of the Federal Bank. And a Victorian
Ju lge answers: “Then I say, frankly, I am quite sure
there were an enormous number of unexceptional things
done at that time of which I utterly disapprove, and
many of which, I should not hesitate to say, were
thoroughly dishonest.” Quite a nasty jar for Smell-
bourne’s unconvicted criminals 1
♦ * ♦ *
From the Federal Bank investigator’s report on
the directors’ accounts: “The conclusion forced upon
me by examination of the books is that Mr. James Munro
did not provide any of the capital whatever for the above
undertakings ’’—meaning speculations out of which the
ancient Jimmy expected to amass abnormal wealth.
“Mr. William M‘Lean is a large speculator, and all the
transactions recorded in these accounts appear to have
been carried on, not with capital furnished by himself,
except to an inappreciable extent, hut with advances
obtained from the bank.” However, the Vic. Govern-
ment, per favour of Patterson, has expressed its complete
sympathy with the Munro clan, so depositors ought to
feel satisfied.

It is practically an open secret in Melbourne
that two banks, at all events, will go down when the re-
constructed deposits begin to fall due.
» ♦ » »
According to latest returns the Queensland
Government has £2,409,000 of reconstructed public funds
in the Q.N. Bank, and £743,000 at current account; total,
£3,152,000. The £743,000 is money lodged since the his-
torical stoppage. By and by the shindykit Qoverwment
will the new Ministry will draw a stupendous
cheque on the Q.N. Bank. Probably it is the prospect of
that cheque which keeps the £3 lOs.-shares down to the
monotonous quotation: “Sellers, 2s. 6d.; no buyers.”
Another interesting item in the story of the
N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Agency Co., is the acknowledge-
ment that £348,000 of losses “appeared in the balance-
sheet as an asset,” and that the “ Melbourne deficit ” of
£480,000 was “ not disclosed to the shareholders.” And
this is only a feeble sample of what would have come out
concerning almost all the other suspended institu-
tions if public inquiry hadn’t been judiciously smothered,
and the reconstructions rushed through before the
creditors got their second wind.

The Southern compromises of the past quarter
include (1) a firm who, as traders, squatters, &c.,
were worth £250,000 apiece a few’ years ago. They offer
2s. in the £, extended over ten years, on liabilities of
£600,000. (2) A country storekeeper who retired in 1888
with £210,000. He compromises on t'409,000 at 7d. in the
£. (3) A very big speculator and trader who, seven years
ago, could get a marked cheque for £150,000. He squares
up at 3d. in the £. Southern banks are the main
sufferers; so it’s no wonder that these estates don’t go
through the Court. Destroy confidence, don’t you know.

With all their cuteness, the Jews have only
just dropped to the fading-ink racket. Four heavy bills
which matured this month, on being taken out of the
portfolio for presentation, were discovered to be blank
papar. What’s more, the usual restoratives of invisible
ink don’t act on these bills, and an expert chemist can get
his own price to restore the signatures.
* * * *
The Australian Rights Purchase Bill is quiescent
just now—scotched, in fact, by the N.S.W. Labour-party.
But it is a noble enterprise and well worth fighting for;
and it will not be abandoned by the promoters until the
last ray of hope has fled. On being floated at any reason-
able figure it would give as a minimum £lOOO for every
£5O invested.
* • * *
Australia, during the last five years, has in-
creased her wool-clip by 32 per cent., or 465,000 bales,
and hasn’t received a farthing more than for the clips
prior to the beginning of the mentioned period. There
has been in that time an increase of 24 million sheep, and,
sc far as wool is concerned, the continent isn’t a penny
the richer. What an awful slump! Also, what liar was
it who said that production stops when prices fall below
the cost of production ? Many stations in the Australias
are not making tucker, but they have to keep going—
the sun and the seasons stop for no overdraft.

Brokenhillionaire Horn has financed the big
expedition of scientists, explorers, land-grabbers and
presstiien, which starts this month from Adelaide to spy
out the supposed wealth lying around in the M‘Donnell
Ranges, Centralia. From this isolated mass of ranges,
gullies and table-land, set in the sandy, waterless bed of a
prehistoric inland sea, great finds are expected. Strathal-
lon, surveyor of the boundary ’twixt S. Australia and
Queensland, some years back reported the discovery of
gold-hearing reefs, probable diamond drift, vast mica
beds, and supposed rubies in the M‘Donnell Ranges, and
explorer Lindsay utilised his subsequent visit by floating
a ruby mining company, along with Henniker Heaton, in
London. Experts declared the rubies garnets all the
same. Lindsay did not lose money by the deal. Horn’s
scientific zeal probably has a solid basis.

Dobie, of Adelaide, as shareholders, stating that, by using

instead of from Tarrawingee, £50,000 per annum could be
saved. M’yes ! Many people will carefully watch this
matter in the light of several things, past and present.

Ernest Giles is very sanguine as to the results
of his prospecting expedition, which is being syndicated.
The writer wishes him the best of good luck ; he also
wishes that Mr. Giles’ venture had been better handled,
as the explorer is just the very man to lead well-trained
prospectors through unknown country. In a venture of
the sort, the man who can find water is quite as necessary
as the man who can find gold.

All faith in N.S.W. gold-mines save one—
Baker’s Creek—has vanished. In proof whereof, take
Tilden’s, at Gundagai. Since the battery started it has
crushed 440 tons for 5320 z. gold —yet you could hardly
give away the shares. Kive years ago they would have
gone to the other extreme. Mining in N.S.W. is either
mad or asleep.

Dear Bulletin,—There appears to be an over
amount of sanguineness displayed with regard to the ex-
pected rainfall at Murchison and Coolgardie, out west, if
one may judge by the hundreds rushing that way. From
what the writer knows of that most patchy of countries
(patchy in soil, manners, and minerals), he being a Kim-
berleyite, the rain-season at the latter district takes place,
usually, at the end of Dec., and extends through Jan.
to middle of Feb. The tropical storms drift south coastal-
ways, extending some distance inland, but tail off the
farther they go south. The rain-storms may or may not
reach Murchison and Coolgardie. When in W.A., in
1886, the Murchison River had not run for two years.
Drought appears in cycles, and it is on the cards
that the season will hi a dry one. The much-talked-
of water expected in the deep lands seems pro-
blematic. In a country with a light rainfall and
an extensive arid background it hardly seems
probable that a strong flow of water will be struck
at a depth. Anyhow, not sufficient to run a 20-head of
stamps throughout the year. Granted a rainfall, then
conservation must be expensive on account of the tremen-
dous evaporation. The conveyance of ore to water
appears to be the cheapest way out of the difficulty for
all concerned. When a railway-line is laid down, and until
then, claim-holders had better hold their hands in dam
construction ; they had much better hurry up matters by
sending round the hat, and contribute to railway con-
struction what each claim-holder would require to expend
upon a dam to work his property,—Yours truly, Taipo,
Melb., 23rd April.

tion, actionable enough.

Dear Bulletin, —You are very consistent about
the Bendigo slump, but please don’t he too hard on us.
Our mines may be having more calls than divs., but we
have a co-operative bakery (run by the labour people)
which has paid 9s. divs. in 18 months on 20s. shares; also,
we are about the only town or city which doesn’t shut
shop on 9tb Nov.—J.M-
-v c ;
Williams, the Argus special, is going across
country from Coolgardie to Murchison, despite David
Lindsay’s assurance that he will never get through.
Williams hails from Ballarat, and should be able to com-
pare the two fields discreetly. His paper is much annoyed
at the storm raised over Pearson’s articles at the beginning
of the year, when the lawyer damned all Coolgardie mines
outside Bayley’s, and died. No expense is being spared
on this occasion.
It is unknown who is acting as Coolgardie
special for the Age, hut the articles are believed to be the
result of amateur labour. One of “ David’s " nephews is,
however, acting as chainman for the Government sur-
veyor, and he may possibly be responsible.
Westralian Customs are being ruthlessly
trampled on by the t’othersiders, whose latest idea is the
introduction of teams two abreast with wide-gauge wag-
gons and welded axles. All axles of local waggons and
heavy drays are in two pieces bolted to the bed. Rock-
liffe, of Broken Hill, is the pioneer of the two-abreast
crusade, which is meeting with bitter opposition on the
road.
Teamsters are now arriving with nothing but
swags aboard, the “swamper’s” baggage being more
remunerative than ordinary loading. With the heavy
roads of winter there will probably be a famine at Cool-
gardie, as the teams simply cannot carry enough food for
the crowd on the field.
* ♦ ♦ *
Queer tales are told up North of fortunes gained
by suppressing information. One manager is said to
have kept boring-rods going ahead of the crosscut where
the reef was expected. One day something came out of the
bore, and the manager knocked off the men in the cross-
cut and put them shaft-sinking, explaining that the reef
had evidently gone astray, and it was useless to hunt for
it. The shaft went steadily down in hard conglomerate,
and the shares fell as steadily from 10s. to Is. Then the
manager changed his mind and put the men back in the
crosscut. The third shift cut the reef, carrying good
gold in slate country. The first crushing went 30z., the
second 70z.; shares went up to 455., and three months
afterwards the manager went ’Ome, where he bought an
estate and was enjoying life thoroughly at last accounts.
* * » *
There is coal in New Guinea, and plenty of it.
Says Gov. Macgregor; “New Guinea may eventually
compete with Newcastle for Australian trade.” A
delightful vision is here conjured up of the Australian
collier competing in the near future with an ill-paid, ill-
fed brood of Papuan heathen.
♦ * * *
A miner writes from Wyalong : Water in the
tank at the main camp is now unfit to drink, the recent
rain having washed the filth and refuse into the tank.
Those who can are buying for 2d. a bucket water brought
in from six miles out. The alluvial reported as found on.
Buddigower was a fraud. The rush was to a gully tried
for years past, which would not give tucker. It took
five days to dish-wash half a dwt.—this in a place
reported in Sydney dailies as yielding two to three dwts.
per dish.
* ♦ ♦ *
Coolgardie road again blocked for want of
water. The escort horses had only two drinks in 130
miles.
» * » *
The crowd is fairly pouring towards Coolgardie.
Many hundreds arrive weekly at Perth, and unless the
finds of alluvial are enormous, Govt, will be compelled to
Start the railway from Southern Gross as a relief-work.

After the usual hanging-up when water runs
out, which it does every month, Mount Allen has made
another start. It is time the shareholders overhauled the
directors, who should give place to better men—al any
rate, to men who have more time to attend to the business
of the mine. And Mount Allen is a mine well worth
attending to.

* * » *
If the stoppage of work for three days is enough
to forfeit a gold-lease in Westralia, the sooner the world
knows it the better. In the Kitanita case only three days
stoppage was claimed by the jumpers, and although this
was denied, the Warden, in the absence of the holder of
the lease, and even without notice to attend, recom-
mended forfeiture. Everywhere else leaseholders are
called upon to show’ cause.
* * » » '
Zebina Lane’s language, on hearing in London
that one of his Westralian leases had been jumped, must
hive been most eloquent.

what life there may yet be for us!—Yours, Suburban
Alderman. , ■;

Lord Percy Douglas’ Premier Extended claim,
25-Mile, Coolgardie, is to he floated in Australia. The
ground contains the Premier reef, with good gold-hearing
stone. There is nothing sensational, but, with economical
crushing, the reef should pay. ‘ s

Cashman’s claim (found by George Lee, and not
by the gent whose name was affixed) is being placed pn
the market, backed by rich specimens. Some months
ago Cashman, who held a-fourth interest, sold out to his
mates. Since then a rich shoot has been found, and
Cashman is perhaps sorry he spoke. .. ,

Personal Items.
Speaker Cowley (Q,), although a supporter
of the “whisky ’’-party, is a staunch teetotaller.
The foreman of the jury in the Austra-
lasian v. Hayes libel-case is a staunch Wes*
leyan.
Commissioner Coombes, Australian Salva-
tion Army boss, has gone “home” to kiss Pope
Booth’s big toe.
Quong Tart has gone on another trip to
China, He is already a sort of Chinese C.M.G.,
and hopes to return a Privy Councillor.
The latest report as to ex- Agent-General
Bray’s health is most unsatisfactory. It is feared
he will not live to return to Adelaide.
Vic. Chief Justice Madden will be 50 on
May 16. Lord Carington will be 51 on the same
date, and Henniker Heaton will be 46 on May 18.
Brewer Frederick Tooth, late of New
South Wales, who died recently in London, left
.£339,472 behind him—representing just 28 million
“long-beers.” Hid Hicjacetl
The Age cableman thus cynically tran-
scribed a recent cable: “Baron Hirsch, the
Jewish philanthropist, yesterday gave a dinner in
London to 60 millionaires.” Feed the hungry !
The Consul-General for Liberia at Mel-
bourne has received a rare mark of distinction
from the Portuguese Government. He has been
created a Knight Commander of the Military
Order of Jesus Christ.
Lord Kintore arrives in Sydney from Van-
couver, per Arawa, May 6. He comes to complete
his reign in Adelaide without either his wife or
his children, and rumour states that he will then
pass on to Melbourne to succeed Gov. Hopetoun.
Salisbury and Rosebery are the only two
British Premiers who ever set foot on Australian
soil —the Conservative in his ante-political days,
so that his personal knowledge is of small value,
the Liberal when in full possession of his political
faculties.
Papa Pinnock, P. M., of Brisbane, when
inebriates are brought before him, wonders where
they raise the money to get drunk with. “ I
know,” says he pathetically, “ I cannot pay for
liquor.” It is melancholy to see any old gentle-
man having to give such broad hints.
Sir Charles Russell, the most brilliant
present-day English advocate, just raised to the
Bench, used always to cry copiously when defend-
ing a woman. He wept reservoirs over Lady Colin
Campbell. He takes snuff, too, so that powder
and tears merged sweetly into odoriferous mud on
his big coloured handkerchief,
Montgomery, the condemned Sydney
burglar, is said by a Melb. detective, who knew
him well, to have been the regal Bill Sikes of
Australia. The skeleton key and crowbar gentry
considered it the height of their ambition to be
allowed to burgle in his company. He was so
expert and brutally fearless.
N.S.W. Speaker Abbott was once compar-
ing notes with Gov. Jersey, and remarked that
his musical ear was so defective that he only knew
one tune, and that was the National Anthem.
“ I know another man,” said Jersey, “who is so
sound-blind that he only knows the National
Anthem by the fact that the people take off their
hats to it; and that’s myself.”
Joan of Arc is to be made
a saint. Saints are the C.M.G.’a
of Theology.
Four members of the S. A.
Ministry—Kingston, Cockburn,
Holder, and Gordon were
Born in 1851, while Jenkins
and Gillen are younger. They
all enter therefore with com-
parative enthusiasm into the
scheme for retrenching sexa-
genarian Civil servants. Apro-
pos, were 60-year-old politicians
ineligible for portfolios, what
would become of the Patterson
Government ?
Sad to think that Sam.
Way, as S.A, Lieut.-Gov., has
the privilege of advising the
Colonial Office as to the con-
ferring of titles upon his fellow-
citizens, but, alas, cannot put
in a good word for that Privy
Councillorship which he so
much yearns for. _He is, by
many years, the senior Austra-
lian Chief Justice, and, as the
dignity has come to Alfred
Stephen, he sees no particular
x'eason why he should not get it.
Rev. Dr. O’Dowd, of
Ballarat, who died recently,
possessed less than _ half-a-
crown at the time of his death,
says the Age. If so, he will fly
lighter on his road to heaven.
Apropos, some time ago there
was published the story of a
Melbourne clergyman whose
friends, when they had closed
his eyes, proceeded to inves-
tigate his worldly affairs. They
found his earthly riches hung
up in his rusty old black waist-
coat behind the door. The
total was twopence.
One newspaper cable states that Queen
Victoria kissed the bride and the bridegroom and
the parents of the bride at the recent German-
sausage wedding. Another cable says that she
“was affected to tears.” For such items as these
has Science harnessed God’s lightning and dragged
it over thousand miles of sea and land.
Neither of the Melb. dailies reported
jockey Hayes’ retort when Purves, Q. 0., asked
him if he had ever seen any reputable gentleman
in company with Goddard, the bruiser. “Yes,
sir,” was Hayes’ reply, “ I have seen the president
of the Melbourne Athletic Club.” Which was
trying for Chief Justice Madden, by whom the
case was being heard, as in Goddard’s time that
learned bruiser held the presidency.
Marie Elizabeth Amy Castilla, M.B.’
Ch. 8., newly appointed house-surgeon of St’
Vincent’s Hospital (Melb.), is a sister of Ethe 1
Castilla, the well-known Melbourne journalist.
This is the first time in Australia that a woman
has been appointed surgeon in a general hospital,
and there is a good, though not here explainable,
reason why the precedent should not be followed.
A certain Australian commandant is an
expert horseman and expects his officers to be
ditto. At a recent parade he ordered an A.D.C.
to tell Captain So-and-so that he wanted him,
“and over the fence is the shortest way.” The
officer went, and “like a soldier fell.” Then the
general, sternly regarding the prostrate one,
“ And be good enough, sir, to obey orders, and go
over the fence, not through it.”
The late “ Jack” Robertson once attended
a memorial mass at St. Mary’s Cathedral, Sydney.
He knelt beside Tom Slattery, and endured it as
long as he could. At length, leaning over to the
devout Tom, he whispered—“ Tom, this is all
(very) fine, but I’d give anything for a brandy-
and-soda.” Tom replied in a reverent undertone
—“Nothing easier. The ceremony is just about
finished, so come with me,” and he led “Jack”
into an ante-room, rang for the housekeeper and
ordered some refreshments. Whisky, brandy,
wine, etc., were soon disclosed to old “Jack’s”
wondering gaze, who, quaffing a brandy-and-soda
with much relish, gave his opinion thus—“ Tom,
old boy, after all, this must be the (only) true
church.”
Answers to Correspondents .
No notice will be taken of communications which arrive
at this office insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must, in every case, appear on MS.
“Merrick”: The Australian students have
practically no original songs. Their ditties consist solely
of parodies “C.L.D.”: We don’t see it thus. So far
as our observation extends the Protestant subscribers to
Catholic charities are much more numerous than Catholic
subscribers to Protestant ones “Brownie” (C.Q.):
Aimed at the head of the local tea-meeting, and doubtless
calculated to much amuse the deacon’s washer-lady
“ Mokee ” : No, but if things progress as at present the
N.S.W. population will need bullet-proof clothes; (2) We
don’t say that the bank-manager was shot as a result of

a bit added to the great N.S.W. pistol-shooting snowball.
.. .“Charles D.E.F.”: The camel has come to stay, and
The Bulletin wants to see the white men drive
and ride it (as they do at Coolgardie every day).
The Bulletin also wants to see alien niggers excluded
from the country. The industrious white teamsters who
lose their living through the operations of nigger-driven
camels are to be pitied ; but their case can’t be remedied
by arguing that the camel should be legally excluded
from Australia—or, what would be almost the same thing,
excluded from towns. You might as well seek to sup-
press the mining stamper-battery in favour of hand-
worked dollies “N.Z,”; You are too Zola-esque. A
horrible tendency to go into particulars will be your ruin.

Dear Bulletin, — Somebody
writes to a Melb. paper from Cool-
gardie : “ A much more serious
question than the shortness of the
supply of provisions is the seemingly
never-ending stream of passengers—
or ‘swampers,’ as they are more
generally termed—arriving by these
teams.” One of the first Hinglish
sneers of the newchum in Australia
is directed against the local tend-
ency to regard him as a “ swamper.”
He has no right to come to the place
patronised by his predecessors, ho is
a grievance to the “ earlier settlers,”
and they would like to know what
the devil he means by it. Within
a week of my arrival in Melbourne
I was made aware of my imperti-
nence in poaching on the preserves
of older inhabitants. Speaking of
this peculiarity to a lady who had
arrived with her husband in the
“fifties” I found that she in her
newchum days was called upon to
answer the aggressive inquiry,
“What brought you out here?’ Even
then the handful of population com-
plained that “ everybody would be
coming to Victoria soon, they sup-
posed”—to swamp the place. Accord-
ing to all accounts, these later emi-
grants to Coolgardie are as likely to
fall across gold as those who have
been a few months on the field
yet_ they have no business to be
trying their luck because they didn’t
get there before. My newchum
sore dates back to ten years ago, or
more, and I thought it was healed
until that “ swamper ” remark
rubbed it hard and caused me to
sneer again at Australian “ hospi
tality.”—Yours, Commonwealth.
W. C. M‘Kay, manager of the Commercial
Bank at Barraba, a small N.S. W. township some
200 miles north of Sydney, was shot dead on
April 18 by one of two men who stuck up the
bank in the dinner-hour, and escaped, but without
any plunder. A Govt, reward of .£2OO each is
offered for the murderers, one of whom is supposed
to have been accidentally wounded, since his
mate had to support him on his horse. A
man named Cummings has been arrested on
suspicion of being one of the men concerned. The
salient point of the tragedy is the ease with which
it was consummated, and with which it may be
consummated again at any of the many trifling
bush townships in Australia. The victim had
actually foreseen his fate. “I often asked him,”
said his wife at the inquest, “ ‘ What would you do
if someone should accost you with arms and de-
mand the cash ? Would you not give it to them?’
He replied : * No, I would not; I should do my
duty.’ He said that robbers would not come at
night; he said they would come at dinner-hour,
when he had the keys and there were only a few
people about.” And so precisely it fell out.
Obviously bank-officials in such circumstances
need both more protection and more precaution.

Dear Bulletin, —Sydney has now three
Ereetrade daily papers, and not one of them has a
single good word to say of the others.— The
Workman.
Premier Patterson on an Age report:—
“Was ever a man more grossly misrepresented
than I?” Now then, electors of Castlemaine,
don’t all speak at once.
The rupture between Premier Patterson
and David Syme is now complete. The Age
“goes for” slippery Jim at every chance, and
Patterson speaks of Syme as “King David.”
How would a clause in the Norwegian
public-house law meet the requirements of certain
Australian zealots ? In Norway no publican is
permitted to employ any woman except his own
wife behind his bar.
The Labour-member lately rated before
the party’s executive at Adelaide for repeated
public drunkenness, has been warned to sober up
under penalty of expulsion. “ Swipeing ” with
political impunity is now only a Tory privilege.
M'Credie, M.P. (N.S.W.), who was elected
as an opponent of payment of members, has drawn
his salary with great regularity up to date. His
legislative efforts so far have not given the country
an exalted idea of the value of unpaid members.

Jenkins, who has been promoted from the
post of Govt. Whip—he is reputedly the worst
whip on local record —in S.A. to that of Works
Commissioner, is a Yankee. He is naturalised in
his own province, but becomes an alien directly he
crosses the border into one oi the others.
Negotiations for a coalition of N.S.W.
political parties proceed by spurts. It is a sign of
the times, however, that in each of the three or
four last spurts the claims of Parkes to _a Cabinet
position were wholly ignored. Parkes, junior, on
the other hand, was considered a possibility.
“Take my cue,” said Premier Dibbs the
other day to a parliamentary aspirantif you
want your private business to gee, stick to it and
leave politics alone. I’ll never advise a friend of
mine to go in for the game. Whoosh ! away goes
your business, and then you are doomed to loa.
about Parliament the rest of your life, praying for
something to turn up. Leave politics to the
knaves and fools now at it.”
If there be a grain of truth in R. Reid’s
assertion that Australia’s gallant volunteers are
equally ready to fight their own enemies on the
spot or England’s enemies abroad, it is more
than time that militarism were abolished in this
country. We ought to guard against Soudan
Contingencies.
Of course America is the land of the free
and the home of the brave, and when the eagle
flaps his wings the hull Creation shivers, but isn’t
there a suggestion of loose screws somewhere
when 50,000 men with arms and dynamite take
trains and levy on towns in their progress to over-
awe your capital ?
N.S.W. Treasurer See, M.P., will be
opposed at next general election by a local Scotch-
man named Munro, and will have to propound
some better reason for re-election than his
favourite: “ I assure you, gentlemen, as I re-
marked before, that the Government are animated
by the very best intentions.”
That journalistic boycott in Hungary is
said to have fired the imaginations of sundry Aus-
tralian politicians. Why not insult newspaper
reporters until their several organs agree not to
publish any more parliamentary business ? What
wouldn’t Eat Man give for a few months of unin-
terrupted legislation in the darkness !
The last three S.A. Agents-General have
all been men who made politics pay. Blyth, as
Minister and Pseudo-Ambassador, drew .£32,000
in State salaries; Bray, as Minister, Speaker and
Agent-General, has thus received more than
=£15,000; and Playford, up to now, has pocketed
about .£ll,OOO as Minister and member.
Yic. Government continues its policy of
displacing stationmasters at small stations, and
giving the jobs to women. “The women do the
work for nine or ten shillings a week, the rapacious
men want thirty at least,” explains Minister
Richardson. A younger woman might be got to
fill Richardson’s position for a twentieth of his
screw, by the way.
Ex-Judge Manning, M.L. C., fights
spiritedly against the reduction in the Govern-
ment grant to the Sydney University, of which he
is Chancellor. But when half-a-year ago Govern-
ment took between .£OO,OOO and .£70,000 a year
off the grant to the State primary schools he
agreed with his fellows in the Council that the
retrenchment was a just one.
Dr, Bowker’s final vituperative on the
N.S.W. Mining on Private Lands Bill: “It is
irreligious.” As a professor of medicine the
doctor treats encroachments on his domain, either
by quacks or by patients who refuse to pay, as
ordinary incidents of business; but as a property-
holder he considers himself cousin-german to the
Almighty and orders hell-fire for trespassers.
The Crown Minister who enters an op-
ponent’s constituency and says, “ I will build
this, that, or the other for you if I am in office
three months longer,” not alone offers a bribe but
hits his opponent below the political belt. Three
members, with vitriolic tongues, have practically
absented themselves from a certain Parliament
during the current session for causes like this.
They know the Minister will not build this, that
or the other, but they know that if they open
their mouths in the House he will go again to the
constituency and say: “That is why I didn’t
build.”

PATTERSON: “I say, M‘Intyre, did you read about that Maoriland
girl refusing her prospective husband at the altar?”
MAO.: “I did so—strikes me if much of that goes on there’ll soon be an

PATTERSON: “What’s that?”
MAO.: “Babies?”
Apparently U.S. Supreme Court has decided
against Gov. Tillman, of South Carolina, for it is
cabled that all the local State pubs, have been
closed as illegal. This means triumph for the
liquor-sellers who appealed, on the ground that it
was ultra vires, against the law which destroyed
their traffic ; and the world must sorrowfully find
another field than South Carolina for its experi
ments in national hotel-keeping.
Much disafcisfaction was caused iu Adelaide,
during last Assembly general election, because the
dailies sold, at per column, the space for candi-
dates’ speeches. The poor man got half a column,
but the rich man, if he felt inclined to pay, might
get five or six columns. At the present Council
elections the Advertiser fixes a maximum of a
column for each speaker, and rigidly refuses to
exceed that amount whatever coin is offered.
“Law and order” has been arrogated as
the shibboleth of the upper classes, but with what
monstrous unfitness is evidenced by the attitude
they have taken up towards the Court of Concilia-
tion and Arbitration in trade disputes in N.S.W.
In every case of serious trade dispute since Parlia-
ment established that court, the employes have
applied to make law the arbiter; in no case, save
one, have the employers accepted that legally-
authorised way of terminating the quarrel. In
every case, except the one referred to, these latter
have declared their determination to fight the

N.S.W. Mines Minister Copeland’s con-
fession, at Hillgrove, that he is as Liberal, ad-
vanced, and democratic in his opinions as a man
in his position ought to be, recalls the story of the
gentleman who squinted; “Ah,” said his wife,
“he only squints as much as a gentleman should
squint.’' It also brings to mind the exalted poten-
tate who was warned of the future consequences
of certain of his acts. “Don’t you think, he
asked, “that God will take my social position
into account ?”
Robert Reid’s mission last week was to
thank the Duke of Garnpridge, in the name of
Australia, for having sent such magnificent
warriors as Hutton and Co. to enlighten our
defence force, and also to express the anxiety of
“ colonial volunteers ” for a first-class European
war in which they might be allowed to die for the
dear old Motherland. Unless a providential
anarchist should chance to blow up Robt. Reid
with a bomb that brass-fronted military draper
will certainly get liimself knighted.
A distribution of blankets to the Queens-
land aborigines is to be made as usual on Queen s
Birthday. Concerning which Brisbane lele-
gkaph :—lt is not intended that blankets shall be
given to aborigines unless their condition has been
prejudicially affected by the occupation of the
country by white people.” The calm assumption
that there are in existence aborigines whose con-
dition has not been prejudicially affected by white
occupation is magnificent. Evidently the coming
distribution of blankets will be the shortest on
record. .1. Bull may be addicted to cant, but
certain of his Antipodean descendants can give
their progenitor points in the matter of cold-
blooded hypocrisy.
There came a witness many miles to attend
a trial the other day at Rockhampton District
Court. Case —a cheque forged on the B.N.Q.
Judge Miller, however, refused to try the case on
the grounds that he (the Judge) was a shareholder
in the aforesaid bank and consequently an in-
terested person. While respecting Judge Miller s
honourable scruples, which do him all credit, we
rise to enquire of the various Australian Govern-
ments which appoint and pay judges, what a
private employer would say if he found one of his
staff incapable of performing certain of his duties
by reason of his being engaged in private com-
mercial transactions of his own. Would not the
subordinate be promptly invested with the ancient
and honourable order of the sack? A Judge’s
only investment should be Government bonds.
X.L. : “ Here is an idea of mine, which
has grown like a snowball, all through life. The
currents of business and society do not run
parallel, but antagonistically. In other words, a
man’s home seems to be a drag on his work,
instead of a help. Baker, the Vic. Education
Minister, is weeding out the woman teachers.
Now, no one can deny that his principle is
essentially (false, but it is justified on necessity.
The women who have husbands must be the
victims, wholly irrespective of their ability, as
compared with the single women. Then there
comes in a second false distinction. The women
•whose husbands are unable to support them must
stop. This again without reference to compara-
tive ability. Such are the ideas that have under-
lain tha whole scheme of Civil Service retrench-
ment, in Victoria. The Service is thus a huge
benevolent asylum.”
Dear Bulletin, —Be Pope’s advice to
Poles, Russia is “at the root of Polish liberty ”
such as it is. Says Irish-Secretary Morley :
“ About 19 out of every 20 of the inhabitants [of
Poland] were serfs without a single civil or
political right; one in 20 was a noble; and the
Polish nobles were the vainest, most selfish, most
cruelly intolerant, most lawless aristocracy of
ancient or modern times.” No person who knows
the facts can deny that the partition of Poland,
though much mourned over by canting senti-
mentalists, was a real gain to the people of Poland.
Certainly their progress has not been, since then,
such as it might have been under more favourable
rule than the autocratic Czar’s; still, d tout
seigneur, tout honneur— Yours, R. EaLCONEK,
Dunedin, M.L.

“It is only the last love of a woman that

Dalgety, the absentee Australian merchant
who lately died in London, has left nigh half-a
million. He even shunted his business over to a
co., without any of the heartburnings which have
ensued in the cases of Goldsbrough, Mort, and
Stevenson. He was a wise man and nobody ever
heard of him.
Stared at by the Dead.
E Qne , norn ; ng the guards on the elevated
• York noticed a middle-aged man
Darent j v kneeling beside an open window.
was a raw and cold morning, his head
wag uncovered. His eyes seemed to be staring
infcentl across t he street. All day long, as the
ins thundered past, the man seemed still to be
watchjngi and even w h en night came on a glimpse
of a w hite face could be seen staring out into the
darkness. The next morning the guards were all
on the lookout, and still the man could be seen
with his chin resting on the back of bis hand.
Coroner Donlin, who chanced to be looking
out of the car window during tl' ed ay,Baw at once
that it was no common face that glared at him.
He left the train, went to the house, and there
found kneeling by the window the stiffened corpse
of a man. For two days he had kept the vigil of
the dead. Awaking in the right, alone and
oppressed, he had struggled to the window, and,
gasping for breath, died. The coroner s examma-
tion revealed the fact that death had been caused
by Bright’s disease of the kidneys, which came
unannounced, sudden and sure.
j Y es, but how common have these sudden
deatbs become. You note them in the local press
every day- They give no warning. There is a
quick pain, a struggle, and all is over. Statistics
gbow tbat qq per cen t. of these sudden deaths are
oaUBed by kidney disease. Slowly but surely these
greafc been disintegrating, and when
b fi e climax comes it comes without warning. But
there were warnings. The strange pains, peculiar
sen sations, and unaccountable feelings were
N ature > s warnings. They may have been con-
aidere(j only the symptoms of a cold, but all the
wbde disease was working and death surely
approaching.
Can nothing be done ? Yes ; exercise care. Act
p rom ptly. Use something to sustain the kidneys,
j} o) as m any physicians are advising their
patients to do, take Warner’s Safe Cure. If this
great preparation which has saved so many men
and women f ro m death by Bright’s disease were
no t a well-known household remedy and the most
p o p U i ar in existence, we might hesitate to so
wa r m ly endorse it, but being the only absolute
g p eo ifi c f or this great modern disease it is com-
mended rnos t unhesitatingly. It is no ordinary
med i o ; ne) but a scientific discovery which has re-
cejved mo re commendations than any other dis-
covery Q f the present century. The well-known
j)r. James D. Bragg says ; “ Warner’s Safe Cure
has proved the best medicine for humanity yet de-
v;sed-»
When the uncertainty of life is remembered ;
wben the certainty of disaster where care is not
exerc ; ged j s realised, the importance of the above
truths should come home solemnly to all.

LIGHT ON THE POLICE FOORCE.
A Waverley Alderman suggests that other Forces in Nature might be made to co-operate with the Police Force, and suggests an incandescent electric-lighting apparatus
be laid on to each policeman.
No. 1.—Certainly the old-fashioned method of looking for burglars with a lighted match seems out of step with the times. No. 2.—Instead of the light being affixed to
the policeman’s stomach by means of his belt, this paper suggests that the helmet would be the most effective. A storage battery might be fitted to its upper storey (as seen in
the above sectional view) with incandescent burner on top. No. 3. —The objection to attaching the light to the stomach will be apparent on recalling a recent police episode.
The most unscientific may perceive at once how, by such a method of attack, the glim would be instantaneously doused. No. 4-5.—An illuminated constabulary would be
open to objections from some quarters. In the Domain, for instance. No. 6. —There is no good reason why “clever captures” should not be made by means of electricity. A
generating dynamo, say, in each of bobby’s boots, attached by means of wire to metal handles, would do away with the present clumsy and unsightly handcuffs. No. 7-8. —The
ordinary electric-light, for instance, might be mistaken for the regular “foorce” to the [?]mecessary terror of some of our citizens, or mislead others to inquire their own addresses
of insensate lamp-posts.

Twenty-two deaths on the Murchison from
typhoid, in March. No time to be clean in the
rush for gold. There is not much typhoid on
Coolgard-ie yet, but only because there has not
been enough rain to spread the ground-filth.

company they are compelled to keep.
• * •
Last year, when working in a very poor
Sydney district (Miller’s Point), a certain clergy-
man’s wife wrote to the wife of a Sydney Croesus,
asking if she could possibly spare some of her old,
half-worn blankets, as influenza and measles were
prevalent and women and children were in much
suffering. The great lady wrote back:—“Dear
Mrs. , I am so grieved to hear of the distress
in your district; one would like to help, but one
has so many calls on one. I have heard that
newspapers are very good to keep poor people
warm. —Yours, &o.” And she did not even send
the newspapers!
There is little sentiment amongst the
prisoners in Melbourne Gaol. Last week the
writer noticed two confinees actually tailoring on
the “drop” of that institution, whilst some half-
dozen other unfortunate gentlemen cracked jokes
and followed their trades on the ground that
covered Ernest Knox, Frances Knorr and Deem-
ing. Ugh !

Dear Bulletin, —All honour to Brigham
Brown, M.D., F.R.C.S., if there is such a man. '-
Monogamy is a failure and Polygamy has always
been successful. I have tried both. The disas-
trous case is a lapsus from either ; e.g., a cause for
divorce in so-called civilised countries or a selection
of a favourite in the harem. I never lived more
happily than with two or three wives. I never
was more worried than with one. Ido not know
liow many I have now alive. There are none with
me. And wives fairly treated and on one equality
agree to yield to one another in their “fortes.”
Thus my cook-spouse was acknowledged at meals
to be a dear old thing ; my music-half (or was it
third or fifth?) applauded; the dressmaker-help-
meet was a Worth to us, and the beauty-love was
Queen of Beauty. But, then, I am a just man
and know how to keep my aquus animus. And,
again, I say Hipperay for Brigham Brown !
Yours, Brigham Scotty Wrinkled.
• * •
Dear Bulletin, — Apropos of your para-
graph last week about the officers of the Salvation
Army travelling first saloon in the ocean liners.
1 was a passenger per Austral from Naples to
Australia 18 months ago, and we had on board
Miss Lucy Booth, on her way to take charge of
the Indian mission. She travelled second saloon,
spent a good deal of her time, between seasickness,
in the steerage conducting services and nursing
the sick; and put on no “side.” When we arrived
at Colombo, Ceylon, she and her lady companions
assumed the Indian costume of the Army, which
is almost identical with the native dress, and
went ashore in their bare feet. Personally my
religion is nondescript, but I could not help
admiring the pluck, the real heroism of this
cultured and refined girl a skilled musician,
and evidently anything but strong physically,
going ashore in her bare feet on that roasting day,
when the ground would almost blister one through
the soles of one’s boots. Add to this the moral
heroism —for the haughty Anglo-Indians look
with scorn on native races, and with contempt
and disgust upon a white assuming the native
costume —and you will agree, as all the sceptics
did on board that ship, that at any rate one
officer of the Salvation Army was not a feather-
bed soldier. —Yours, Sceptic.
Another young man came to Sydney
Hospital last week with a revolver-bullet in his
head, which struck him as he was walking along
George-street, Nobody heard a shot or saw the
shooting; but there are so many police practising
these times that a bullet may meander very far
from its source ere it finds its billet. A stranger
might have been surprised at such an occurrence
in the chief street of the chief city of Australia ;
but, luckily, the young man was no stranger and
knew what it meant. So the bullet was taken
out of his head and labelled a visitation of the
ferocious Premier Dibbs, and everybody is as
cheerful as can be expected.

One Kanaka leper lately deported from a
Central Queensland town, has been known to be
“ suffering from some disease” for the last four
years; but nobody was quite sure what the

The first transference of dates under the
new S.A. Public Holidays Act will be in re Queen
Victoria’s birthday, which will be celebrated on
Monday, May 28. Several other similar imperial-
istic festivals will also be postponed to the
subsequent Mondays so as to let Civil-servants
have the benefit of the preceding Saturday after-
noon and Sunday.
* * *
An Australasian Club has just been formed
in London. It originated in the offices of the
various Agents-General. The first social gather-
ing—a dinner —is to take place this month, with
Ambassador Samuel in the chair. Graham Berry’s
nephew is the secretary.
* * *
N.S.W. State-schools run Penny Savings
Banks, ostensibly to engender thrift among pupils.
Recently it was noticed that one little girl’s
account had grown unduly. She banked four or
five shillings regularly every Monday, and was
fast becoming the Fat Girl of the school; so the
teacher asked her whence came the wealth,
“Please, Miss,” answered the young capitalist,
“mother is saving up to buy a baby !” The baby
was bought shortly after, and the account closed.
Apropos , the little round tin money-box
with “Take care of the pence and the pounds will
take care of themselves” embossed thereon, which,
along with the big Bible, used to stand on the
old-fashioned round table placed in the exact
centre of the “front room,” and to which the
enfant terrible was wont to draw your attention
by rattling under your nose while_ “mamma ”
gently reproved him for being rude, is a thing of
the past now, and the more terrible penny-bank-
book has taken its place in N.S.W. With
practice one could surreptitiously slip- a tin-tag
into the money-box and make it sound like
sixpence—a convenient and strictly honest way of
getting rid of bad money. But the Public
Schools’ Penny Bank-book has changed all that;
and, in future, the thrifty young man, who always
strikes his friend’s house just about tea-time on
Sundays, will find it cheaper to dine at his hash-
house and do his visiting when the youngsters are
abed.

Dear Bulletin, —You enquire for the
champion mean man. I am on his track. Two
blind men who blow the organ-bellows at St.
Andrew’s Cathedral, Sydney, have hitherto re-
ceived the princely screw of £l5 each annually ;
in future they will get j£lo a year only.— Puff.
Rev. Alex. Marshall, of Scots Church,
Melb., preaching on popular sport and pastimes,
laments that the chokered oyster whose shell is
the pulpit has not taken a keener interest in these
things in order “ to direct the people in the choice
of their amusements.” But hasn’t the parson
been striving for generations to induce the people
to revel in bun-struggles? And yet they don’t
revel. The parson follows the people, he does not
direct them. He continues to insist with bland
assurance that he is driving the team, whereas he
is only the flea on the tail of the dog under the
waggon. Parsons like the Rev. Marshall are
called “ advanced ” when they talk the platitudes
of the man in the street on such subjects as sport.

A Melb. matrimonial agency advertises —
Marriages celebrated by ordained clergyman, fee
10s. fid. ; gold wedding rings, from ss. 6d.” These
are prices to suit the times. A large stock of
qualified parsons in attendance, and rings war-
ranted to last out the honeymoon supplied—on
time-payment, perhaps. The ad. somehow re-
minds one of the man who rushed into a jeweller’s
for a golden circlet, and was shown a solid article
that would “wear for a lifetime.” “ A thinner
one will do : I only want it to wear from Satur-
day to Monday,” replied the Don Juanesque
customer.

A gmi-armed masked man lately tried to
stick up a little N.S.W. country store. When the
storekeeper’s wife opened the door, he of the mask
presented his gun with the usual “ Your money or
life.” He got neither. She told him to “Clear
out! ” and slammed the door in his face. “ Bluff”
often wins.

President Rainford, of N S.W. Licensed
Victuallers, when eulogising certain barmaids
before Minister Slattery and denouncing others,
spoilt his case from a moral point of view. Instead
of urging that certain girls shall be shut out, he
should have insisted that certain publicans shall
be shut out for breaking the law by subletting
their bars. The girls’ misconduct is beside the
question. Throw the law-breaking publican into
the street and away goes the erratic girls’ occupa-
tion.

The divorces in N.S. Wales, during the
years 1893 and 1894, will, it is estimated, exceed
in number the total divorces of the previous 20
years. The past 15 months give 276, or nearly 18|
per month.

One critic says the best thing Mrs. Caffyn
has done in “ A Yellow Aster ’’ is to invent a new
exclamation—“ Qru ! ” Everybody in the book
says “Qru!" on provocation, or without any
provocation ; and it is hailed as a distinct im-
provement on the unrealistic “ Humph ! ” and
“ Tush ! ”

gelists were farewelled in Sydney last week. They

• # •
The Queensland Civil Service Board
graciously assure their subordinates that officials
in Northern or Western districts, desirous of a
change, may attain their wishes if they only wait
patiently “a year or two.” This to a man down
with malaria, chronic dysentery, ague, or Gulf
fever is tidings of an eminently cheerful and
hilarious nature.

A branch of the International Christian
Police Association has been established in Mel-
bourne, and its influence on the “fource” is
expected to make itself evident in a few weeks.
This new body should be strenuously opposed by
W.G.T.U. and the Victoria Alliance. The writer
knows a Melbourne journalist who always goes
home sober when he has his watch with him.
Isn’t it an axiom that a good man is a bad police-
man?
A couple aged respectively 60 and 66 years
were judicially separated the other day at Goul-
burn, N.S.W.
# * *
The Bundaherg (Q.) people intend starting
a Kanaka museum. It is presumed that they will
commence by collecting a few choice Polynesian
skeletons from the burying-ground of each planta-
tion—for the planters generally inter defunct
islanders in some corner of their estate to save
cemetery-expenses. Photographs may also be
taken of domestic scenes among the New Hebri-
deans. Group I.—-Tommy Tanna (25 of him) and
Mary Tanna (one of her), with yams and curios
in the background. Specimens of local sugar
might also bo exhibited with interesting cal-
culations as to the amount of Kanaka guano
necessary to ensure a decent yield per acre, to-
gether with specimens of shoddy clothes, fearsome
knives and axes, consumption-germs and leprosy-
bacilli. There is not the least doubt (as a local
paper truthfully says) the collection could be
made instructive in the highest degree.

HER ANSWER.
(AS IT OUGHT TO BE.)
You ask me—“ Can I marry you ?”
(’Twas thus her answer ran)
And I tell you frankly, freely,
“Well—Yes! I think I can.”
I can marry one who buzzes round
A dozen girls a year;
Whose mental vision never soared
Past billet-doux and beer ;
I can marry—tho’ I know full well
The love-lorn swain who sues
Has passed his life in waiting for
His aged father’s shoes ;
I can marry one whose gospel is—
“ The tailor makes the man !”
Whose highest aim is but to shine
A fop in fashion’s van;
Whose boots and headgear spotless shine
Altho’ his morals don’t—
I can marry him—of course, I can,
But then, you see —I won't I
P. LUEPIG.
TWO CUPS.
’Twixt the lazy light
And the drowsy gloom,
Away from sight
In an upstair room ;
A liqueured lip
And a ductile soul;
A deep-drawn nip
Prom a swimming bowl;
A dimpled throat,
Iridescent nose,
A greedy gloat
And quiescent doze
Sixpence, please !
A pewter pot
Of “colonial ” beer
From a churlish sot
With a vulpine leer.
A pawky fist
And a carious shirt
A pisan twist
In a nose alert,
A quickened quaff
And cash down slick,
A lusty laugh
And extempore kick—
Thripence 1

Mc. G.
Policeman Ryley wanted nous ;
From sin he was converted;
He loitered by no public-house,
Nor with a cookie flirted ;
He turned from ladies of the pave,
With pious exclamations,
Or else their souls he sought to save
With scraps from “Revelations.”
The roughs he plied with tract and text,
And prayed with pocket-pickers,
And never festive clubmen vext
By borrowing their “ tickers."
He ne’er forsook his beat at night.
Nor in a doorway slumbered,
But preached a sermon, strong and trite,
To every man he lumbered.
He spake no ill, and on a ruse
No prosecution built he,
And scorned to “ run in ” or accuse
A chap who wasn’t guilty.
He was a perfect man, they knew,
But sacked him, saying slyly,
A worse policeman never grew
Than that same Mister Ryley.
