Tasmania’s Grand Old Man—ADYE DOUGLAS.
Dear Bulletin, —The yarn about Father
Muldooney’s cushion is only a variant of that told
by Billy Little, then M.L.A., now miner, in
Q. Parliament on Totalisator Bill, 1892. Said
William : “No, I don’t go in for raffles. Me and
a mate went once to a bazaar and put down .£ 1
for agoon a suite of furniture. At the drawing
we threw 18 ; and then we went home. Next day
I called round with a van for the suite of furni-
ture, and they told me the parson of the church
had thrown 19 and scooped the pool. Ever since
then I’ve put my little bit of scuff on the totaliaa-
tor.”—Yours, S.
One or two questions in reference to that
Barraba murderought to be answered. 1. Why did
Mr. M‘Kay lock that door? 2. If it is true that
one of the two men who rode away from the bank
was wounded, how, and by whom, was the wound
inflicted ? Mr. M‘Kay appears to have had no
weapon, and all the three shots said to have been
fired are accounted for. 3. Why was none of
the loose cash lying about taken ? It would not
have required three seconds to cram a few hand-
fuls of coin and notes into the murderers’ pockets.
4. Was robbery the real object ?

Dear Bulletin, — Apropos “fire-madness.”
I was in Melb. Opera House when Greer shot his
wife, her paramour, and himself in the dress-circle.
Pitites, thinking it’s a fire-alarm, rise for a stam-
pede. Sharp voice from back row, “Sit down 1”
and they sit, but rise again. Louder—“ Sit down,
fools !” and they sit again, but rise a third time.
Stentorian yell—“ Sit down, you infernal, blanky
idiots !—-sit down !” and they sit and rise no more.
Novy, didn’t that single son of Belial save more
lives than all the prayers of all the parsons ? —N.
• • •
Dear Bulletin, —“D.W.’s” generalisa-
tion that man-and-wife (plus other girl’s man and
other fellow’s wife) love-story never has children
doesn’t quite hit it. They do have children; but
the children are killed off, partly for artistic
reasons, because they crowd the canvas ineffec-
tively and distract attention from the principal
figures, and partly for moral reasons, because it
would be dangerous to let children grow up in the
company of such parents. Without touching old
classics, take Hardy’s “Tess,” or the “Dodo”
and “Ideala” in every shallow reader’s mouth.
Tess has a child, but it dies—natural causes.
Dodo has a child, which dies —convulsions. Ideala
has a child—diphtheria. Then there’s Ouida’s
“ Moths”—slaughtered infants; and lots more, if
I could put my thoughts on them. “D.W.” must
really revise his theory.—Yours, A.G.
In 1885 historian Froude wrote of Adelaide
as “a city of 150,000 inhabitants, not one of whom
has ever known, or will know, a moment’s anxiety
as to the recurring regularity of his three meals a
day.” The italics are ours because a man died of
starvation there the other day.
A male tabby recently told Sydney
Women’s Literary Society “It was a part of the
English nature to insist on character more than
deeds.” Yet no man was ever more wor-
shipped in England, or lived a viler and more
scandalous private life than Nelson ; Fox was an
inveterate gambler and rou6, Wellington was
famous for his “ successes with women.”
Melb. Evening Standard refers to Sir
Walter Scott as England’s greatest writer ! Eng-
land’s ! Why not Wales’? This is in keeping
with the usages of the Victorian press. Any good
invention or creditable event happening outside
Victoria is “ Australian ; ” inside Victoria, “ Vic-
torian.” Everything discreditable to Australia,
whether Victorian or otherwise, is “Australian.”
To wit, Brennan’s Victorian torpedo, Dailey’s
Australian Contingent— not that Dailey’s Con-
tingent was creditable, but the Melb. papers
thought it was.
HOW WESTRALIAN ABORIGINAL PRISONERS ARE TREATED.
The blacks here shown are being driven a distance of 340 miles to serve a six months’ sentenoa
in Rottuest prison, at the instance of a local squatter.
There have been ructions at the Savage
Club, says The Bulletin’s London correspon-
dent, in re the election of a new committee.
Rightly or wrongly, Somers Vine is supposed
to entirely boss the place, and fault has
been found with the elasticity of the necessary
qualifications under the Vine regime. In conse-
quence, ever so many men have got in under the
guise of “science.” Matters have culminated
and the camp is divided into Yineites and anti-
Yineites. Twelve names were legally proposed
for election as members of the committee, but at
the general meeting an almost entirely new set
were elected, and there are actually two com-
mittees at present. What the outcome will be it
is hard to say—possibly law —bub whatever hap-
pens the club is bound to suffer to some extent.
In all probability Vine will throw up the hon.
secretaryship and clear out.
The writer knew a temperance evangelist
whose vogue it was to inspire newspaper reporters,
who came under his magnetic influence, to write
paragraphs, in good faith, asserting that some of
his admirers were about to present him with a
valuable token of their esteem, &c. His particular
day-dream was a presentation of paid-up mining-
scrip. Somehow the ceremonials predicted never
came off; or, if they did, were never publicly
chronicled. The conclusion ordinarily to be
arrived at was that the social reformer hoped to
suggest to overmoneyed fools the desirability of
acting as he dreamed and hoped. The writer
knew the godly man to be badly baulked on one
occasion, by a reporter whom he had invited to
his house to lunch. “Some friends of mine in
(say) Fossickhole,” said the water-man, “have
promised me some mining-shares in a real good
thing, and, as you have done me and my cause so
much real good, I shan’t forget you when I get
them. It will be something for your old age.”
“ Thanks, no,” replied the young reprobate, “ the
(say) Unhungtown Hatchet pays me my screw ;
but I’ll wire up to your friends for confirmation
and give the thing a paragraph, if that’s what
you want?” “Pray, don’t do anything of the
kind,” urged the Nevertouchit, “I wouldn’t let
my friends dream that I had mentioned their in-
tentions. You may give it as from an outside
source, if you like ; but don’t mention that I told
you, although it is the real truth.” “Well, I
don't like,” said the bad young man, as he rose
and went straight for the front door.
Comedians : Rosebery, Chamberlain.
The London Commission on Labour says
of the Great Australian Adjective: “It is in
general colloquial use among the lowest classes,
and is frequently used as a qualifying adjective,
but its derivation attaches no sanguinary meaning
to it.” The said derivation being from the Anglo-
Saxon “ blodig,” which means “very.”
Dear- Bulletin, — If “J. McG.” really
saw a 4-foot sting-tailed adder, he is to that ex-
tent ahead of current ophiology, which knows
only of anlS-inch “deaf-adder” repeatedly demon-
strated to have only one “business-end”—the
head. I have personally dissected four, one of
which had killed (by the mouth) a large dog in
7 min., and the finial in each case was a solid
“claw,” unconnected with any poison apparatus.
—Experiment.
“ A woman’s tongue is only three inches
long, but it eari kill a man six feet high.”l—
THEY SPORT WITH HUMAN LIFE.
I’ll give you the plain facts in the case, and you
shall help me to judge whether there was a mistake
made or not.
It seems that some time in 1889—in the winter,
no doubt —Mr. Strong, of Leicester, was taken
down with influenza, often called the grip; and
truly a strong grip it has when it once takes a
hold. Well, lam glad to say he was able to fight
out the battle and get the best of it. But the best
wasn’t anything to brag of. The influenza hadn’t
exactly beaten Mr. Strong, but left him in a very
bad condition. And here is where you and I
properly pick up the thread of the story.
His meals didn’t tempt him; the most savoury
of dishes had no charms for our friend. He had
lost his appetite, and in the long run a man had
better lose his money. He forced down something,
of course, but it gave him such a pain in the chest
that he wished he hadn’t swallowed a mouthful.
Then, naturally, he got so weak and nervous he
couldn’t sleep o’ nights. This, as everybody
knows, is the straight road to the graveyard,
through the madhouse. Lots of people are making
that trip all the time. We must eat and we must
sleep. If we don’t we are done for. Neuralgic
pains, too, made matters worse for Mr. Strong.
He saw a doctor, and what did the doctor do?
This is what the patient says on that point:
“This doctor gave me all kinds of strengthening
medicines, but none ol ' hem did me any good, and
I continued to suffer for month after month.”
Just what we might have expected, Mr. Strong
further says : ‘‘ln October, 1890, my friend, Mr.
James Webster, of 28, New Walk, Leicester,
advised me to try a medicine called Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup, and I followed Ids advice. The
first bottle gave me great relief, and I began to
digest my food and to have an appetite ; and after
I had used three bottles I was quite another man.
I was completely cured. All my aches and pains
left me, my strength returned, and I have been
all right ever since. What astonished me was
that the Syrup cured me so quickly, and I shall
never cease thanking Mr. Webster for making it
known to me. You are at liberty to publish my
casefor thebenefit of others. Yours truly, (Signed)
W. Strong, 41, East Street, Leicester, December
30th, 1891.”
Now, was there a mistake in this matter, and
if so, what was it? Yes, there was a common
mistake made. It is an old and seemingly hope-
less blunder.
“ The doctor,” says Mr. Strong, “gave me ail
kinds of strengthening medicines.”
Open wide your ears and remember what I’m
going to tell you now ; remember it for the hour
of your own helplessness and pain : There is no
such thing as strengthening medicine, neither in the
earth nor in the waters which are under the earth.
That is the truth ; all the high-class doctors know
it. As for the others, well there ! the less said
about them the better.
Mr. Strong took “strengthening medicines,”
and what happened to him? Why, he “continued
to suffer month after month.” Did you ever see
a horse made stronger and fatter by spurs and
lash-whips, leaving off the oats and hay ? I think
you have not. And that is what “strengthening
medicines ” do, and all they do. So-called tonics
are like making a sick man walk far and fast by
kicking him at every other step.
Nothing under the sun but digested food imparts
strength ; no drug ever does.
And herein is the perpetual success and victory of
Mother Seigel’s Syrup ; it cleanses the system of
disease-poison and leaves Nature sweet and free.
The stomach then cries, “Feed me," and so power
and health come back like the green grass after a
shower. Do you see? Mother Seigel proclaims
“I destroy disease!” Nature responds, “Only
d (that, and I can take care of myself.”
The disease is indigestion and dyspepsia. Mr.
Strong had it and was badly treated. Should you
have it, try the Syrup first —not last, for I have
told you why. Experience.
London, March, 1892,

For pelf we dip in learning’s pool,
For power we gather knowledge,
For place we toil in Wisdom’s school
And drink the lore of college.
For gain we soar in science high
With flight that naught can fetter;
Just as the condor seeks the sky—
To view the carrion better.

A GOOD STOVE.
A WORD OF CAUTION.
Everyone knows what a good stove is—at least,
the women do ; and every woman knows that, of
all the treasures of the household, a really first-
rate, reliable oooking-stove is the greatest desider-
atum in a house. A great many stoves are
advertised every year, many of which are like
jerry-built houses—thrown together to sell A
word of caution is therefore necessary to intending
buyers, because there are few things so annoying
and vexatious as to buy a stove and then find that
it does not possess all the qualities that are neces-
sary to give complete and lasting satisfaction.
Now, the points in choosing a good stove are: —
1. Strong cast-iron frame.
2. Good draught.
3. Facilities to burn either wood, coal, or
even turf.
4. Simplicity of parts, and thus, easy to clean.
5. Duplicate parts kept in stock, so that
bieakages can be replaced at small cost
and without delay.
And if, in addition to these five points, you can
get a stove that is also an ornament to the kitchen,
all the better.
Price, of course, is a point, but that is a matter
which need scarcely be mentioned, since, if the
above points are combined in any stove, it would
be cheap at any price within reason.
It only remains for us to say that, with con-
fidence, we can honestly recommend “The
Housewife,” sold by Holdsworth, Macpherson
and Co., 250 George-street, Sydney, from <£3 10s.
upwards, as one that fully and entirely “ fills the
bill.”
We should suggest our readers writing to this
firm for an illustrated catalogue, with further
particulars.

I am the Mergenthaler—
“ Merg,” they call me
When they want to be, like me,
Expeditious ;
Like the doom of Ulysses I come
From across the water;
I cast ray lines and build my home upon the
Tottering throne of printerdom.
1 am king, jack and deuce,
With the ace turned,
And am trump at all suits.
When I spring myself
Things had better take a tumble,
For I come to stay.
The poor printer man,
Who used to have to work all night
And then drink beer all day,
Can now have all his time to lush,
For I have
Spilled him out.
His erstwhile sixty lines aWhour, and
Rotatory fat,
Are in the fire, for I can
Double-discount him at his own game.
He is behind the procession,
And unless he learns to
Paw my keyboard
He might as well go dead.
I'm going to make or break
Somebody—and I don’t care-a-
Dam who.
The sand’s in my gizzard
And my dander’s up ;
And if I’m not the winning double, then
What can win ?
Like a nigger,
I’m a good servant
But a bad master,
And if all hands don’t look out
Something’s going to drop.
See !
When I say “ 800 !”
The boys all booze,
And when I sing my little song
They dance,
I si rut in pride
And mock at Faust and Gutenberg ;
For lo!
1 am the Mergenthaler—
And I’m a dizzy swell. —Ex.
M'Burney, Wallis & Co.’s Report.
Wool.— The usual fortnightly sales were held this week.
The offerings, as usual at this time of the year, were made
up chiefly of country scoured assisted by several lots from
local establishments, the greasy portion of the catalogues
comprising odd hales of stragglers and dead wool remnants
of station clips, together with country dealers’ lots. The
whole of the buyers were in attendance, and bidding may
be quoted as on a par with prices current at previous
auctions, though both owners and purchasers seemed
somewhat inclined to hold back for news to be re-
ceived in regard to third series of London sales
opening there on the Ist May, so that a rather
larger proportion than usual of catalogues sub-
mitted from the box were withdrawn, though no doubt
some private sales will be effected. Acting on our own
suggestion, most of our friends with wool in store decided
in favour of holding their parcels until next auctions,
when London news will be public property, and though
we do not mean to say there is much to base any prospect
of a rise upon, still other trades generally have lately
reported a slow yet steady improvement in business, so
that, at all events, we venture to think that a hardening
tendency in prices may be looked for.—Sydney, April 19,
1894.

Saturday, 12 th May.
JOHN CAIRNS has received instructions to
sell by public auction, on SATURDAY,
12th MAY, the whole of the New Montalbion
Silver Mining and Smelting Co.’s Property,
situate at Montalbion (N.Q.), in one lot. The
property offered is as follows :
Smelting Machinery.
1 60-ton capacity water-jacket furnace ; 1 30-ton
ditto ; 1 15-ton ditto ; 1 tram-elevator; platform
scales ; “ Hope ” stonebreaker, 15" x 7"; 1 30-h.p.
Robey engine; 3 roasting furnaces; 3 baker’s
blowers; 1 Manchester pump; 1 Tangye do ; 1
Blake do (together throwing about 20,000 gals,
per hour); 3000 ft. assorted W.I piping; tanks of
6000 gals, capacity; 2 Multitubular boilers,
14' x s'; and all appliances complete and in
thorough order.
Concentrating Machinery.
2 30ft. Linkenbach patent Buddies with 2 Spitz-
gasteus, &c., 6 Jordan and Cummins patent
jiggers, 2 seta Cornish rolls, 3 elevators, 1 Blake
and Marsden’s stonebreaker, 7 revolving screens,
sampling tables, &c., &c.
General Machinery.
Spare shafting, piping, belting, firebars, fire-
doors, steel rails (about 3 miles), trucks, bullion
moulds, slag pots, slag trucks, 60ft. archirnedean
screw conveyor, Bullivant’s wire tramway com-
plete for 1500 fathoms, spare pulleys, Briquette
machine, cupel furnaces and frames, 2 Avery’s
6-ton weighbridges, 1 Ruston and Proctor’s 12h.p.
portable engine, 6in. centrifugal pump, 2|in.
rotary pump, 1 Bh.p. loco, boiler, donkey feed
pump, Bh.p. winding engine, lOh.p. winding
engine, lOh.p. multitubular boiler, Blake feed
pump, and 20 tons charcoal and a quantity of lime
and iron and firewood.
Buildings.
Office and dwelling—two storeys, 36' x 30':
Kitchen, toolhouse, cartshed, stables, store, and
weighbridge office. Dwelling and office furniture;
smelting shed, 210' x 110'; concentrating shed,
172 ft. x 60ft. ; laboratory and shed, 93ft. x 50ft.
Bathrooms, blacksmith’s shops, &c. Engine,
mine, and blacksmiths’ sheds erected at all the
mines.
The movable tools are estimated at, at least,
£lOOO ; the stores at .£lOOO.
There are 1500 tons of payable ore on the sur-
face, and 2000 tons of matte estimated to contain
40 oz. silver per ton.
The mines are exempt from work (or let on
tribute) until 26th April next, and all workings
are in thorough order.
The assaying and analytical plant is complete in
every requisite and material.
This is the most complete plant for smelting and
concentrating silver ores that has ever been placed
on the Australian markets. The machinery is all
good, and the purchaser may commence operations
without a week’s delay. Fullest enquiries as to
probable success are solicited.
An unfortunate entanglement with the banking
institutions during the late financial crisis, at
which time the capital of the Company had been
called up, forced this Company into liquidation,
the outcome of which is that the property must be
realised upon for the benefit of the creditors.

Dear Bulletin, — Five hundred miles in
five days is certainly good work for a riding-camel,
but the “record” —put up in North Africa—
stands at COO miles in 4 days, with a 2501 b.-load !
The six ompeting “cracks” finished (at Tunis)
within a few minutes of each other. —Dromedary.
One of two Southern evening newspapers
is almost certain to retire shortly, but they seem
unable to settle the question as to which is the
least fitted to survive. Why don’t they both
die and give the beautiful Truth a chance to make
itself known ?
Speight’s move for a writ of attachment
against the Age publisher (for alleged contempt
of court in reporting boss Syme’s speech to his
“national” subscribers) has mysteriously gone
astray. People are asking whether the railway
man was indisposed to proceed, or David Syme
was afraid to let him ; also they have remarked
that the libel-case was reopened without either
side making reference to the writ which never
came off.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
It is now 11 years ago. In his first speech
at the very commencement of the tour he crowed,
the little bantam I “My team I your Lordship! my
team I my team ! my team comes ! my team sees! my
team conquers!” And he boasted so that the other
members of the team shot him despairing glances which
said: “Don't give us away like that! For God’s sake
speak more moderately ! Be temperate ! ” But not see-
ing them he crowed and crowed still louder. He reached
the painful and ridiculous climax when he strutted out
upon the floor, waved his hand and shouted, “ Now, Aus-
tralia, step forward and give three cheers for his Lord-
ship. Hip! Hip! HOO-rayl” In the very middle of the
hooray the disgusted glances of Englishmen and Austra-
lian overwhelmed him. The HOO was the bumptious-
toned HOO of a pompous and dapper personage ; the
ray was weak, sickly ; it came from a crushed worm.
* « ♦ *
The jury in the Hayes v. Australasian case
took care to prove their unfitness for the task. In the
first place, they wanted the Judge to come and have a
quiet chat with them in the jury-room. That was pain-
fully absurd. Then the foreman put a finishing-touch to
the legal farce by reading a paper, the joint production
of six gigantic brains, from which it appeared that the
jury “ recognised the serious loss of time to this honour-
able Court, and the great expense to the country which
has occurred without a more definite result being ob-
tained.” Which side did they imagine to be the
“ country "—jockey Hayes or the newspaper?

At a receftt Vic. race-meeting, after the steeple-
chase, in which a horse and jockey have fallen. Jockey’s
wife (meaning jockey): “Is he hurt?” Punter (meaning
horse): “Yes, fearful; leg broke in two places. I think
they’ll have to shoot him.” Here a woman faints.

Letter ( verbatim ) lately received by a certain
big Australian sweep-promoter:—“Dearsir,—l have sent
an order for a ticket, and I have added 4d. extra for the
return postage, and oh how earnestly I pray that God
will bless and prosper me with good luck this time! I
l ave always believed your works to be fair and honest or
I would not have supported them so long as I have done.
0 i, dear sir, my earnest prayer to God day by day is
t lat He will bless and prosper me with a favourable result.
1 am a believer in prayer and I trust sincerely that God
will bless and prosper me, if it should please him to do
8). To express my gratitude to your works most willingly
do I give you permission to deduct 2s. out of every £1 I
receive after you have taken your discount off; yes 2s. in
every £1 for I am trusting in God’s blessing to prosper
me. My reason for asking His great blessing this time is
because the Building Society has closed and locked up
poor depositor’s money for two or three years, and oh my
sincere prayer is that the Lord will bless me this time.
It, is the Lord alone that knows that I have wrote these
few words to entreat His blessing upon me. May God
bless and prosper me as well as prosper you if you want
it is the sincere prayer of your subscriber and may God’s
blessing rest upon all.” And yet, so hard-hearted is
Providence, after all he didn’t draw a winner. Tatt. is a
callous rascal.

Now that he is “broke” as a publican, efforts
are being made to once more boom Slavin as a boxer ;

another burst bladder, Slavin should keep clear of Corbett.

rated all along. In his cups, Mitchell always bragged
that he could make mince-meat of Slavin, and on the
stage at Liverpool did trounce him. Yet, how did
Mitchell fare against Corbett ?

The camel cannot be abused as a gambling
machine. A camel-race was tried at Coolgardie. A beast
with a strong lead squatted down 50 yards from home and
refused to budge. Then the first three were disqualified
for going outside the course, and the prize went to one
that came in 10 minutes after the others.

A couple of days before the latest Victorian
Civil service defaulter “cleared,” he paid a cheque for
losings to a well-known bookmaker. When he had gone,
the “book” remarked : “That’s the first time I’ve ever
collected from him, but many a time he’s collected from
me. He’s the luckiest fellow I ever met.” And yet one
iookmaker may lose, but the bookmakers always win.
» ♦ * *
Rev. Alexander Marshall, of Melbourne, preach-
ing to a crowded Soots Church congregation on horse-
racing:—“Obviously what was required to meet and
minimise the betting evils was that, it there must be bet-
ting, there should (1) be no betting till the day of the
race, and (2) that there should be no betting on credit.
These conditions would be secured by one project; that
was the introduction of the totalisator.” A sensible sug-
gestion. But then tar. Marshall proposed that the leading
newspapers should agree not to publish betting-quota-
tions. How childlike! What a harvest there would be
for the sporting press, or for some unregenerate daily
started chiefly for the express purpose of giving fuller turf
news than had ever teen given before 1

* * * •
In re the recent boxing controversy, the “noble
art ” is without doubt a grand national institution
amongst the “lower classes," where Britishers alone are
congregated. Take the focsle of a ship, for instance. If
two Britishers have a row they are at it in a jiffy, smash-
ing at one another with Nature’s weapons, with no worse
result than a broken nose or black eye. But where the
crews are mixed the Britisher has all the worst of it, as if
he strikes a foreigner the odds are he is knifed. Amongst
the better classes, in the city or 'out of it, boxing is not
much use to a man in a row. The story of the young
British blood who thrashes the bargee for sport is of
frequent occurrence in England—on paper; but the
writer’s experience has been, with very, very few excep-
tions, that the scientific gently-nurtured amateur gets all
over his working man for a few rounds, and just as he
thinks it time his man ought to cry enough, the rough
takes a hand and knocks the stuffing out of the winded
scientist.

3 to 1 against Australasian, 7 to 1 against Hayes.—
Yours, Long Neck.

A. 14-year-old lad who stole a “Euclid”
from a Melb. bookstall explained that he wished
to compare it with a different edition which he
was studying at school. The Bench let him off
with an apology and a caution not to pursue
knowledge so ardently in future.
Another priest yarn—from the French. A
couple went to confession, and while the wife was
recounting her peccadilloes the confessor slept.
The woman had gone and the husband had taken
her place before the good father awoke. Not
noticing the change he said : “ Let’s see, where
were we ? Oh, I know, you were telling me you
had deceived your husband ! ”

If the gay, erratic Ti tail’d
As a two-year-old dropt dead !
Still, when everything is said,
There’s a satisfactory solace—
We have still got Donald Wallace.

The Meat Export Trade:
The old-time squatter has faded in the light ot
science—or the darkness of finance. The days
when it was the ambition of every match-making
mamma that “dear Jane” or “sweet Lionise”
should marry a squatter; when the squatter
was the type of all that was powerful, and
patriarchal, and princely; when his port was
erect, his shipments direct, and his credit-balance
rarely failed of five figures; have passed away,
never to return. The new-time pastoralist is a
careful, anxious man, harassed by murrain and
mortgage, drought and flood, foot-rot and pleuro;
with the fatal shears of strike-organisers, like
pastoral Parcae, ever raised to sever his slender
thread; and with the rhythmic burden of ten-
per-cent. continually ringing in his ears.
The national policy of Australia, opposed to the
aggregation of vast estates in the hands of a few
monopolists, proposes to replace one great squatter
by a thousand small graziers, and to breed men
instead of sheep. The change is not to be
regretted ; but to be successful it must be gradual.
The pastoral industry is still the leading Austra-
lian industry, and no sudden reversal of its con-
ditions can take place without shock and loss to
the whole community. But the pastoralists them-
selves are preparing for the change. Most of
them even now would prefer to work profitably a
comparatively small area of land which they could
certainly call their own, rather than nourish a
vast estate for a dozen years, spend time and toil
and money to improve it, and see it fall into the
hands of a mortgagee at last.
The current N.S.W. movement for the utilisa-
tion of surplus stock in the shape of frozen meat,
to be exported and sold at a low price in European
markets, is on all-fours with the similar enterprise
in Maoriland, now firmly established, which has
done so much to forward the past five years’ pros-
perity in that province. The system offers the
best possible means for the establishment of the
small farmer. The present writer last year met,
on an ocean-liner, a young Englishman who had
emigrated seven years before and leased 200 acres
of land near Wellington. In 1893 he had raised
and fattened 520 sheep, which returned him 13s.
per head nett, or >£3BB. He started with £2OO
capital, and in seven years he had bought his land
and built a house from the profits of sheep-breed-
ing alone. He had tripped to England for his
wife, and considered himself a made man. And
there are in Maoriland hundreds like him.
If the meat export trade can similarly profit
New South Wales farmers, the work of the newly-
initiated Graziers’ Meat Export Company, whose
prospectus appears in this week’s Bulletin, may
fairly be denominated “a national undertaking.”
The excellent articles by Mr. Cuthbert Fether-
stonhaugh, collected in pamphlet form by the
Pastobalists’ Review, leave little doubt of the
Company’s success, and no doubt whatever that
some such company must succeed if the pastoral
industry is to rise from the slough of despond in
which every year under existing circumstances
sees it more steadily settling. Singularly enough,
the only real obstacle in the way of remunerative
meat export from New South Wales is the supine-
ness of those who should themselves be meat
exporters, and whose inactivity in the present
crisis is as disadvantageous to their interests as it
is discreditable to their enterprise.

In the Eight-Hours Procession at Melb.
on Saturday, Dr, William Maloney, M.P. for
West Melbourne, walked in the procession of
“tinkers” that followed the Tinsmiths’ banner.
Maloney has the courage of his own opinions on
all subjects, and is probably the most kind-hearted
man in the ’Ouse.
The New York Life Insurance Company
offers liberal terms and good territory to gentle-
men of ability and energy who desire to become
agents in town or country. The popularity of the
New York’s life policies is firmly established by
the fact that it secured, in 1893, the largest
amount of new business ever written in a single
year by any company in the world. Address,
with references, and stating previous experience,
if any—Josiah Howell, general manager, Bond-
street, Sydney.
Melbourne Eight Hours’ commemoration
was this year spoilt by a dreary Scotch mist.
There were no additions to the procession, which
fact, as it recurs now every year, suggests the
reflection that perhaps the Eight Hours victory
has been celebrated enough, and that it is about
time the Trades Unions stopped crowing over the
little concession gained nearly 40 years ago.
“ Labour Day ” would be a far more consistent
and comprehensive workers’ celebration. Also,
whooping at the reigning monarch’s representa-
tive, stuck up at a window, should be strictly pro-
hibited. The Typographical Society, of all others,
was the only one that stopped in a body and
whooped for Hopetoun. The Jingo cheer was led
by Hancock, too, if The Bulletin mistakes not.
As usual, pioneers, &0., headed the procession in
shiny bell-toppers.
‘ Faith is belief in things that can’
happen, Hope is belief in things that won’t happen,
and Charity belief in those that don’t happen.”
Judge Williams, who is hearing the second
Speight v. Syme trial, is much more in-
tensely interested than was the pure, posing
Hodges in the first. Williams has been laid up
for a week, and looks feverish still.
A course of lectures on “Democracy,” by
Professor F. Anderson, M. A., commences at the
Railway Institute on May 2. In all, ten lectures
will be given, dealing with the theory and practice
of all forms of democratic government. Nothing
could be more timely or valuable for intelligent
citizens.
An important sale of mining property and
machinery takes place at Herberton, N.Q., on
May 12, when Mr. John Cairns offers for sale the
whole of the New Montalbion Silver Mining and
Smelting Co.’s assets. This company has been
into liquidation by recent banking recon-
struction, and plant and property so valuable are
rarely submitted to auction. Full particulars are
given in advertisement on page 16.
Absconder Kemp (now arrested in South
Africa) it was who complained to the senior con-
stable in charge of Caulfield that the police-station
was not conspicuous enough. His house is not
far off. He said there ought to be a large board
put up, with “ Police Station” on it, so people
could see it coming up from the railway. Fancy-
ing the officer was rather offhand with him, he
carried the matter to headquarters, which, of
course, had to inquire on such an influential
motion.
Gov. Duff’s Grafton visit was made
memorable by a poetical address presented by the
children of St. Mary’s Convent School, from
which :
Oh, that the muses would a share impart
Of their poetic treasures—richest gems
And fadeless garlands meet—this glad event
On this auspicious day to celebrate
In deathless song - ,
For us to know
La reyne le veult suffices in each heart
To wake for your excellencies the notes
Of loyal welcome.
We for you,|
Your Excellency, and for Lady Duff,
Pray that all life may breathe but happiness,
With rose-hued visions of unclouded joy,
Blessed with the knowledge of new heights attained
New good achieved each day, till noble deeds,
Combining, form a lustrous starry crown
For each throughout eternity.
All this for Duff —plain Duff!

The QEAZIEES’ MEAT EXPOET CO. OF NEW SOUTH WALES (Ltd.).
(To be Registered under The Companies Act, 37 Vie. No. 19.)
Capital, £250,000
In 250,000 SHARES OF £1 EACH (WITH POWER TO INCREASE).
No Shareholder will have any Liability beyond the amount of One Pound per Share on the Shares he applies for.
First issue of SHARES upon the following terms
2s. 6d. on application, 2s. 6d. on allotment, the balance in calls not exceeding 2s. 6d. per share as may be required
in terms of the Articles of Association.
The Company may be declared floated when 60,000 shares have been applied for.
It is proposed to forthwith issue 5 per cent. Debentures for the purpose of providing additional capital, in order
to carry out the objects of the Company, such Debentures forming a first charge on the freeholds, leaseholds, plant,
and uncalled capital of the Compan}’, and giving to the holders thereof a voting power in connection with the
administration of the affairs of the Company, in accordance with the powers conferred on them by the Articles of
Association.
Several Banks have agreed to take up Debentures aggregating £30,000 conditionally on Share Capital to the
extent of £lOO,OOO and Debenture Capital to the extent of £50,000 being subscribed in addition.
This Company is initiated under the auspices of the principal Banks and Pastoral Institutions, who recommend it
to their constituents and to the sheep farmers of New South Wales as a NATIONAL UNDERTAKING, based on
sound business lines, and one that, economically managed by its Directors-who will be appointed by the shareholders
and debenture holders—should return fair interest on its capital, and, in addition, give a satisfactory minimum value
to stock, and, as a consequence, to pasture land.
The recommendations and data embodied in this Prospectus are the outcome of an exhaustive inquiry made by
the following Executive Committee, appointed by a General Committee elected at a Public Meeting held in Sydney on
9th February last:

On the formation of the Company the Shareholders and Debenture-holders will be called together for the purpose
of electing its Board of Directors, in terms of the Articles of Association. The Head Office of the Company will be in
Q om p any being formed for the purpose of exporting or otherwise dealing with meat and other produce in a
frozen or preserved state, or treated by any other process which may be found advisable, and to act as Agents for the
Grazier or Exporter, reserving to itself power, if necessary, to purchase and export on its own account.
It is proposed that this shall be essentially a Graziers’ Company—at the same time the Company will be empowered
to issue Shares at the discretion of the Directors to persons not immediately connected with the grazing industry.
The system that has been adopted by the Committee, after careful consideration, as the safest and most econo-
mical, is that of killing and chilling at a number of inland works, and freezing and storing for shipment in large central
factories at port; but should works be erected at comparatively near distances to the seaboard, it may at such works
be deemed advisable to freeze outright and rail direct to the ships.
In arriving at this decision the Committee acted on the principle that the first axiom in preparing meat for export
is TO KILL THE STOCK AS NEAR AS POSSIBLE TO THEIR OWN PASTURES, and this can be best effected by
adopting the Inland Chilling system, under which some NINE INLAND CHILLING WORKS can be erected at the
same cost as THREE INLAND FREEZING WORKS.
Together with the Inland Chilling Works it is proposed to erect all necessary adjuncts, such as boiling-down and
blood and manure plant, tinning, and, if deemed advisable, fellmongery plant.
The attention of Pastoralists is specially directed to the fact that the enhancement in the value of FAT STOCK,
resulting from a large export, will equally apply to STORE STOCK, for which a large increased demand must follow.
Every carcase exported will tend to improve the value of the remainder, while the ready means of disposal will
accentuate the importance of breeding early-maturing stock ; and thus, by enlarging the turnover, render grazing
lands more profitable, and further result in securing to graziers a satisfactory minimum price tor surplus stock.
The number of Inland Chilling Works will necessarily depend on the amount of capital subscribed—THE MORE
CAPITAL THE MORE WORKS. The Directors will appoint a thoroughly experienced and competent General
Manager to superintend the business from the outset.
This Company hopes, by effecting a reduction in the charges, insurance, and freight, to be able to put meat on the
Heme market, from works 350 miles inland, at a consolidated rate of not over 2d. per lb., and eventually rather under.
That an immense future lies before the meat export trade of Australia can be inferred ;
Ist. From the enormous expansion of the frozen meat trade in New Zealand, which, commencing in 1882
with an export valued at £54,000, increased in 1885 to an export of half a million sheep, valued at
£345,000, and culminated in 1891 in the enormous output of nearly TWO MILLION SHEEP, valued at
£1,200,000.
2nd. From the fact that the prejudice against frozen mutton is steadily dying out.
3rd. That, as yet, our mutton has scarcely reached the masses, and that when good mutton can be retailed
at 4£d. per lb. they will, without doubt, become large consumers.
4th, That if hut 5 per cent, of the population of the United Kingdom become increased meat consumers to
the extent of only 11b. a week each, we shall barely be able to supply their requirements.
Bth. That if the Continental armies would but take the article—and it can be supplied at far less than it
can be produced on the Continent—the whole resources of Australia would be taxed to supply these
armies alone.
oth. That there are other countries which are likely, within a few years, to become customers for our meat -
to wit, Japan, China, Canada, eto.
We shall probably have in New South Wales an average annual surplus of over eight million sheep to dispose of,
in the shape of some three million fats for colonial consumption, two million freezers for export, and three million in
the shape of tallow and tinned meat. Of the latter three million, however, we may reasonably hope to export frozen
at least one million at no very distant date.
If this great NATIONAL UNDERTAKING he supported by sheep farmers and those connected with them in
business and be carried to a successful issue, then the advance in values of fat and store stock cannot be less than
from 20 to 25 per cent., while the value of every acre of good grazing freehold or conditionally-purchased land would
be raised proportionately. An advance, say, of 20 per cent, all round on the eight and a half million surplus sheep of
the colony amounts to nearly half a million sterling per annum, an enormous return on a capital of £250,000. IF
EVERY SHEEP PARMER IN NEW SOUTH WALES WOULD TAKE SHARES TO THE EQUIVALENT OF
EVEN ONE PENNY PER HEAD ON EACH SHEEP, THE AMOUNT THUS RAISED WOULD PROVIDE THE
NECESSARY CAPITAL. . , t .
To secure good and steady prices it is essential that shipments be concentrated in the hands of as few consignees
as possible. The market can then be steadied and controlled.
In recognition of the principle that shareholders should enjoy special privileges, the Company proposes to grant
them—(lst) a preference over non-shareholders in the_ treatment of their stock, and (2nd) such concessions on a fair
and proportionate basis and in such form as may hereinafter be decided.
The preliminary expenses will be kept as iow as possible. No promotion commissions have been incurred; the
Company, therefore, can obtain a quick, clean, unfettered start.
The Articles of Association can be inspected at the Offices of the Company, or at Messrs. Johnson, Minter,
Simpson, and Co.’s, Hunter-street, Sydney.

“BULLETIN" OFFICE, SYDNEY.
Waiting.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
*‘ Wait,” said the bride. She was young and fair
Fair as a dear, delicious rose
Which, like a recluse inardent, blows
Veiled from the sun and the grosser air.
“Wait, for my husband is old and grey,
Bent is his back, and his step is slow ;
Soon he will sleep with the earthworms low.
Wait, oh ! my love, for another day.”
“Wait,” said the wife. She was fair and round.
“ Wait, for the years we lose are few ;
Yet we are young, and our world is new,
But he—he is moving towards the mound.”
“ Wait,” said the wife. She was hale and red.
“ Wait. Though thy sorrow is plain to see ;
Time, w ho hath seasons of joy for thee,
Striketh his scythe at that old, grey head.”
Over a mound in a graveyard lone,
Chuckling with glee on the mouldy bed,
Straddleth a man with an old, grey head.
‘ Waiting ” is writ on the chiselled stone.
EDWARD DYSON.
“ The only person who can cure one of a
woman is that woman herself.” —Bourget.
Melb. Age says that “ the defence of
India is a problem intimately connected with
Australian defence.” What is the connection
between a country of black people, controlled by
a British army and always threatened by a neigh-
bouring Power, and a sea-protected continent like
Australia, where militarism is merely a Tory
excrescence? The conditions of Indian govern-
ment are unique, and the Age’s rigmarole about
the proportion of resident white troops to black
troops has no more bearing upon Victorian defence
than the appetite of the Emperor of China has
upon i If Australian rabbit question.
Melb. Age once dealt thusly with a poet
who pressed hard for a notice. “We have
received a volume of verse by —" av . e
much pleasure in saying that the spelling is
unexceptionable !”
Theorists who would move on towards
Australian Federation by first establishing an
interprovincial customs union should note that,
under present Australian political constitutions,
there is no authority given to impose discrim-
inative duties even between the Australias, and
that all Acts attempting such a change would
have to be reserved for a foreign “Queen’s con-
sent.” Whether is easier, the struggle for a
number of small piecemeal Acts, each to be thus
humiliatingly reserved, or to attempt one com-
prehensive change, including all others, or power
to make all others, and coercing our present
foreigh proprietor to assent to it? Does the above
language insult Australians in their self-esteem .
The Bulletin hopes so, since it is only a mild
representation of the truth about Australia s
despicable condition of dependency upon a foreign
owner—a freckle on the other side of the globe.

HERR RASSMUSSEN, PH.D.
A Few Sketches of His Interesting Life and
Work.
Herr Rassmussen, who is known throughout
the world as “The Celebrated Danish Her-
balist,” was born in the little kingdom of Den-
mark, shortly after its last disastrous war with
Germany. His parents being wealthy, and
belonging to the most influential class, he
received a first-class education, and displayed
exceptional talent in the science of botany and
botanical chemistry, and very soon surpassed
everyone of his fellow scholars in those sub-
jects, and eventually made the science of botany
his life-study.
Finding his native country too small, and its
flora too limited to extend his botanical re-
searches, he left for Australia, and landed here
in the beginning of 1879. His first object was
to learn the English language and become
familiar with the customs and habits of his
adopted country, as well as with its botanical
history and medicinal flora. Herr Rassmussen
had quite his share of the troubles and difficul-
ties which usually befall a newcomer in Aus-
tralia ; but he soon overcame every difficulty,
and after five years’ travelling through all the
colonies, investigating every known Australian
medicinal plant, and discovering many new
ones, he eventually settled down permanently
in Sydney in 1883, and then commenced a
most noble mission—that of turning his most
wonderful botanical discoveries to the benefit
and relief of suffering humanity. Herr Rass-
mussen succeeded in this gigantic undertaking
far beyond all expectations, and his name very
scon became a household word, not only in
every Australian camp, village and city, but
also throughout Europe, Africa and America.
Space will not permit to mention the
numerous cures effected by his now world-
famed Alfaline Remedies, suffice it to say
that thousands of original testimonials may be
inspected by anyone at his Head Depfit, 547
George Street, Sydney, many of which may
also be seen in his celebrated work, “ The
Natural Doctor.”
There is no doubt whatever that Herr Rass-
mussen deserves all the success and praise which
he has met with, because he is a most scientific,
conscientious and genuine herbalist—his last
ten years record being so wonderful that his
remedies must have cured more human suffering
in that period than is usually effected by a
hundred average doctors in a similar number of
years. He does not hold any consultation
similar to a doctor ; his whole business is
purely a Proprietary Herbal Remedy Business.
He simply prepares a genuine herbal remedy
specially for the nerves, and another for the blood,
and so on, which can always be relied upon.
He prints on the box containing the remedy its
price and all advice as to doses and diet and
other particulars, and advertises same, which
is the fairest way possible to conduct a business
of this kind. Any information or particulars
desired are given or sent absolutely free of
charge. Pamphlets are sent post free to any
person desiring one, containing all particulars
and many useful and valuable hints on health.
Herr Rassmussen has very large branches
of his extensive business in Melbourne,
Brisbane, Adelaide, Perth, Wellington (N.Z.),
and throughout the world. He is an
honorary member of several scientific socie-
ties, a gold medalist, diplomee and Hon.
Corr. Mem. of the Academic Parisienne des
Inventeurs, France ; last year he received the
Honorary University degree of Doctor of
Philosophy ; in 1887, he was presented with a
magnificent gold medal by several of his cured
patrons in Sydney, and also awarded a silver
medal in Denmark, 1888, a bronze medal,
Melbourne, 1893 ; and in 1891 he issued his
“ Natural Doctor,” a herbal medical work of a
high standard for which he received great
praise from the press, and which is now trans-
lated into many languages.
When My Hair Goes Up.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When my hair goes up, and my skirts go down,
O the gay girl that I shall be !
I will make a bonfire of tiresome books,
Dragging them out from their hidden nooks —
Grammar and history, French and all—
For they’ll be no use when I grow quite tall ;
So I’ll pile them on as the flames upspring
And in the blaze I will dance and sing
To the wildest tune, in the shrillest key—
I shall be the happiest girl in the town
When my hair goes up, and my skirts go down.
And lovers will come, will come by the score
When my hair goes up, and my skirts go down.
But I will be haughty, so haughty, and cold
That the boldest among them shall grow less bold;
I will frown—like this—and stamp my foot,
And into ray glance such scorn I will pub,
They shall sigh all day, and toss all night,
While ever their pain will be my delight
When my hair goes up, and my skirts go down.
Let them call me coquette—l will plague them
more !
When my hair goes up, and my skirts go down.
Yet—if there is one—l do not say—
When my hair goes up, and my skirts go down—
One who is gentle and brave alway,
With plenty of money, a soul without guile
(I should like him to dress to suit my style),
Tall —to be sure; and wit must not lack,
And his eyes will have to be black, quite black ;
And if he plays planet, and lets me oe sun—
Mind, I do not promise—but if there is one—
Why, I’ll be the happiest girl in the town
When my hair goes up, and my skirts go down.

Any manager to any squatter;—“ Sir, I
beg to apply for the post of manager on your
station. I have had twenty years’ experience in
management, and can honestly say I have never
met my match. Should you favourably con-
sider this application, I will have pleasure in
breeding horses of my own on your station, and
by putting a higher price on yours will be able to
dispose of mine readily. If carriers prefer yours,
I will give them no wool until their judgment
improves. Should any part of your run be
unfortunately opened for selection, I will secure
it by putting the bailiffs on at your expense, re-
taining the deeds for myself. After shearing I
will let the storekeeper have the screenings at a
low figure, and stand in with him for the profit.
Should I invite some ladies from the township to
come out and have a picnic, and find unexpectedly
that you would b« visiting the station just then, of
course I should be glad to postpone the picnic.
I have had a great deal to do in lambing, and
know exactly how to make the squatters get small
percentages, while selectors’ ewes have three and
four lambs apiece. During mustering it is my
practice to rush the sheep all over the run at the
pace Carbine went when he won the Cup. This
prevents the accumulation of too heavy fleeces,
which are very unhealthy for sheep in hot weather.
In conclusion, I may say that I have no doubt
that I can, by judicious management, reduce the
carrying capacity of your run from 350,000 to
150,000 in less than two years, and remain, yours
hopefully ” —Baldt Jobson,

G. R. Sims, in London Referee : “I
know one man in England at the present moment
who makes two thousand a year comfortably out
of a paraffin preparation for the hair and judicious
advertisement, and who before I made ray dis-
covery public was behind the counter of a chemist’s
shop. Why, O why, did I not keep the remedy
for baldness to myself ? ” Sirns has deluded him-
self into the idea that he discovered paraffin as a
hair-stimulant, whereas wonderful stories on the
same subject were in print several years before the
Ref. started or the cockney dramatist had been
heard of. It was said that shiny-headed men
working in paraffin factories, had acquired dense
crops of hair through the trade habit of wiping
their oily fingers on the skull from time to time.
So much for the origin of the paraffin “cure,”
which doesn’t seem to be recognised by medical
science. Dr. Pincus, a recognised German authority
upon disease of the hair, or rather, of the hair-
cells, declares that he can do nothing for the person
whose wool has wasted out of sight. Pincus says,
“ Almost all my patients have used stimulants for
months before coming to me (spirits, brandy, eau-
de-cologne, castor-oil), and have done themselves
much harm. All these stimulants shorten the
existence of the hair, or to make use of a meta-
phor, they exhaust the hair-soil. I wish to caution
against these tonics. The diseased head-skin can-
not endure them. It is not weakness, but a diseased
irritation that has to be dealt with, and our task is
to cure this; or if it is no longer possible, at
least to lessen it. Many people come to me, sup-
posing me to have a certain ‘ hair remedy.’ There
are no such remedies; there are certain medica-
ments which counteract the causes of premature
wasting, shortening, and thinning.” The shorten-
ing and thinning of each individual hair on the
affected portions, almost invariably the front and
crown, of the head, is the beginning of baldness in
every case. But so much for the German
specialist. If anyone can solemnly testify to a
case in which baldness, of two or three years’
standing, has been cured by the application of
paraffin— i.e., a case of hair growing thickly
where hair had previously disappeared in a slow
and seemingly “natural” way—then it follows
that Dr. Pincus knows nothing about his subject,
and the sinful, old ballet-gloater in the front row
of the stalls may anoint himself with lamp-oil in
defiance of medical treatises.

“There is no appeal from the popular verdict—
the people think and judge for themselves.” The
Waterbury Watch Company once more “comes
out on top.” Here is valuable and incontrovert*
ible testimony from a New South Wales colonist,
Mr. John Archer writes as follows from Rylstone,
under date of April 2, 1894
“ The Ladies' Waterbury Watch came safely to
hand. It is going well, and keeping good time, as
the Waterbury Watches always do. If I was ever
so rich it is a Waterbury Watch that I should carry,
as I find that they are quite as good as any high-
priced watch, and they always give satisfaction. ”
This is a fair sample of hundreds of testimonials
that are being received every week, and reason-
ably so, for the new perfected quick-winding
Waterbury meets all the requirements of an ideal
low-priced watch. The new Waterbury is a watch
with the best features of a high-priced timepiece,
yet at only a fraction of the cost. It is an accurate
timekeeper, while taste, elegance, and genuineness
are combined in its outward appearance. The
Waterbury watch can be obtained in every style—
nickel, coin-silver, and goldfilled.

Alhambra Variety Show.
At Sydney Lyceum, “The Second Mrs.
Tanqueray ” continues with unabated patronage,
and the performance has improved until it stands
one of which any company might be proud. Mrs.
Brough remains better in the first two acts, which
suit her powers, than in the last two, which do
not. Contrast, for example, the absolute perfec-
tion of her speech beginning “ What is my life?”
with her comparative ineffectiveness in the final
duologue with Aubrey. Her very naturalness
tells against the artistic finish of her rendering;
since (the best art is so refine and spiritualised
as to be beyond nature, or so as, rather, to
reach that highest expression of nature which is
too rare to be easily understood. Mr. Bouci-
cault has modified by a shade his juvenile en-
thusiasm, and for him and Mr. Titheradge there
is little but praise. Mr. Renouf remains unsatis-
factory. His make-up is not military, and not
romantic, and one cannot conceive such a hasty
and commonplace lover as realising the enthusiastic
ideals of Ellean. Mr. Carey and Mr. Mclntyre
fill small parts well; and the others, particularly
Mr. Ward and Miss Temple, deserve much
credit.
• * •
Several Beasts in the audience attract par-
ticular attention. There is the Beast who will
persist in . applauding loudly exactly when the
action of the piece demands silence, as if it helped
an actor or actress to be interrupted at a critical
moment by expressions of appreciation which is
generally unintelligent, and which in any case could
be restrained till the close of the act. Then there is
the Beast who does not hear what is said, and
who asks his wife, in a loud whisper, “ What was
that?” and when she replies, in a loud whisper,
laughs “Hu ! Hu ! Hu!” to the distraction of 19
people round about him. There is also a numerous
Beast who bubbles oyer with mirth in the tragic
parts, and goes into a fit of laughter when Ellean
enters to announce the death of Paula. It is
probably to suit this Beast that the orchestra
invariably plays the audience out with something
light and cheerful, jarring anybody whom the
play has really impressed just as it jars one to
miscalculate the bottom step and tread on air.
With these drawbacks, “The Second Mrs. Tan-
queray ” is a pronounced success.
** * *
“ The Lights of London” still twinkle at
Her Majesty’s to steady business. Also,
the wild warbler mysteriously described on the
programme as “ A Voice,” who sings Louis Diehl’s
ballad on the subject from the bowels of the
theatre, nightly exemplifies the attraction of the
hidden vocalist as compared with the re-
vealed article standing gasping before you
and holding its music like a pound of
loose tea. The hidden singer suggests angels—
the revealed generally reminds you of angles. It
is really interesting to observe how even a medium
voice gains in general listenableness when the
owner thereof is singing beneath a bushel. King
of Crime ” pops up at Her Majesty’s when the
“ Lights of London ” go out.
** * *
Sydney Bijou, Criterion, Opera House,
and Standard are now closed, awaiting better
times. The Royal opens again on Saturday, after
a week’s intermission, with the Holloway Co. on
the boards, and “Judge Not ” as the play. Harry
Rickards’ co., at the Tivoli, continues to thrive on
its own merits and others’ misfortunes, and the
entertainment, brightened by new stars, is one of
the best variety shows that Sydney has seen.
Fillis’s Circus season approachesjts close, but the
performances are still well patronised, and deserve
all their patronage.
** * *
Owing to the weather, and other causes,
“A Woman’s Revenge” has not been a huge
success at Melbourne Princess’s, nor does Bland
Holt’s latest improve much on acquaintance. The
play is a careful collection of things that are old
and unrealistic in dramatic effect, capped by a
seriously impossible trial for murder in the Old
Bailey. It is a consistent work, insofar that the
people who can be moved by the first three acts
are bound to be impressed by the trial scene, and
vice versa. The nature of the plot prepares them
for the trial, whilst the picturesque fallacies of
that judicial ceremony are worthy of the previous
course of events. One genuine touch of nature is
discernable in “A Woman’s Revenge. The
murderer’s name, Jepthah Grimwade, compels
him to mis-doing, and although the possessor of a
Scriptural cognomen usually draws the line at
financial fraud, and contents himself with cleaning
out the depositors, it is clear from the outset that
if blood must be shed during the evening,
Jepthah has a baptismal right to do the deed.
Jepthah figures at the trial as chief witness
for the prosecution, but his guilty conscience is
oozing out of him all the time, and his manner
invites mistrust. . When Judge Windeyer has
summed up in a couple of lines, and the squared
iury have acquitted Miss Watson without leaving
the box, nobody wonders at hearing his Honor
order the clammy Jepthah into custody. One
almost expects to see the common hangman sum-
moned right away by the Attorney-General in
order that Jepthah aforesaid may be executed on
the premises before the curtain falls. For nothing
was impossible with the late dramatist, Pettitt.
W Baker’s rendering of the craven criminal has
gained a little force and vigour since the opening
night, also Sass’s speech for the defence is given
with more earnestness, but neither of these minor-
key actors displays his stagey part to the best
ad'vantage. Cosgrove does very well as the ever-
lasting villain who plots with “papers’ and
gloats over the fact that the game is in his own
hands, whilst Bland Holt and the ladies are
all of them at home in characters they have
olaved in at least a dozen other dramas
under different names. The hit of the piece is
little Brightie Smith, a reliable child-actress, who
says and does all that she has been taught to say
and do in the exact way prescribed by her
teachers, and who is very natural, or perfectly
mechanical, just as one chooses to regard the
damsel. Clever mounting and stage-management
are the unbroken rule with Bland Holt’s shows,
and the present spectacle is admirably conducted.
On the first night the thunderstorm was marred
by the enthusiasm of an unseen Jove, who be-
laboured a big-drum after each flash of lightning,
but since then the elements have been under the
personal supervision of 8.H., and their resem-
blance to the Salvation Harmy has terminated.
But the storm rages for the last time on Friday.
On Saturday “The Prodigal Daughter,” which is
a cheerful play, will present a new phase of a
woman’s vengeance.
** * *
“Charley's Aunt,” from Brazil, re-
appeared at the Bijou last Saturday, and the
theatre was nearly crowded. The promise of a
few alterations in the cast would, no doubt, have
brought a packed house, but seeing that the two
young University Johnnies and their lady loves
turned out to be the same as before, an excited
rush was out of the question. Thornton goes far,
yet he doesn’t make up for all the shortcomings of
his company, who had created a considerable sense
of disappointment in Melbourne by the time their
first season was through. However, “Charley’s
Aunt” is a notoriously good joke, well worth
hearing for a second time, and she ought to flourish
for seven months, or so. By-the-way, what are
those “awful things ” that the amorous guardian
is alleged to say to Auntie when he chases her
round ?
** * *
“The Octoroon” is keeping the colour
question to the fore at Melbourne Alexandra.
Boucicault’s tricky old drama likewise diffuses
new light upon the suicide question, for Dan
Barry has arranged a heavenly ascension of the
heroine who poisons herself. The apotheosis that
served for little Eva, a fortnight ago, is recon-
structed to suit Zoe, and up she goes at 11 p.ra.
Just now, when the Church of England seems
disposed to revive damnation for all self-
destroyers, the broadness of D. Barry’s views will
arouse discussion in theological circles, and
it may be as well to mention that Daniel is nob
related to the bishop who bears his name. A
stealthy, relentless Red Indian (played by Speed,
the music-hall identity) is an important feature of
“The Octoroon.” He follows E. B. Russell
through some cane-brakes and changes of scenery
with a baleful eye, and his burst of gibberish, as
he plants his moccasin upon the chest of ms dead
prey, makes a favourable impression. “Queens
Evidence ” is billed for Saturday.

Alice Holbrook, so persistently and undeservedly
boomed in London, probably owes it to the fact that she
is sister-in-law to B. Barnato, the millionaire diamond-
merchant.
Dan Barry has followed the usual custom of putting up
“The Octoroon” after a good run of “ Uncle Tom’s
Cabin,” and will probably reap the usual disappointment.
Uncle Tom, to a certain class, is a sentimental fetish, but
the very same class only regard “The Octoroon” as un-
mitigated rubbish. Managers never thoroughly realise
how much they owe to Mrs. Beecher Stowe in the matter.

name for herself in London as a contralto.
British Australasian speaks of Johnny Toole as
“beloved by Australian and English playgoers alike.”
M’yes. Ask the firm.

manager of London Alhambra.
Camilla Urso, as per her Coliins-street portraits, deve-
lopes a cheerfully Ansonian likeness.
Old Madame Carandini’s death is cabled. She warbled
in Sydney somewhere up to half a century ago, and thou-
sands in fairy gold trickled through her fingers in auri-
ferous Melbourne. Her husband is termed a Marchese in
the obituary advt., but, all the same, he danced as
harlequin in Tom Pavey’s first Melb. Royal pantomime
in 1855. Madame was born in England, and arrived in
Tasmania as a girl. She was the mother of some of the
most charming women, both as to appearance and manner,
by whom Australia has yet been graced.
The once well-known Madame Sibley, mesmerist, etc.,
died last week near Tenterfield, where for some years she
had kept a small store.

The late Hans van Billow is said to have been very
grateful to Wagner for eloping with his (Von Billow’s)
wife. This erring lady had a changeable disposition, as
became the daughter of her amorous father, Liszt, and
after she had taken herself off with quite unnecessary
secrecy, the deserted husband declared himself dead in
the matter and called her his “poor widow.” Yet there
are dull, mercantile fools who deny that genius and
common sense can co-exist. Who has ever heard a city
man speak of his own relict, with a beaming countenance
and a twinkle in his sordid eye? He howls and scowls
merely because he became insufferable to the woman who
went away.
Qiieerly enough, the horror of being crushed under a
lift which Vi. Varley realised in Melb., is being acted in
London by Marion Terry. It forms the chief sensation in
the new Adelphi drama, “ A Cotton King,” by Sutton
Yane, who is looked upon as Pettitt’s successor.

The Salvation Army band of a very small town in
Victoria once tried to shut up Fitzgerald Brothers’ circus
by blowing tunes outside the tent, and praying generally
for the salvation of all hands, including Commodore, the
lions and the advertising agent. The prayers were more
than audible inside the tent, and pious exhortations
and allusions to the devil got mixed up with the clown s
iokes while the sacred hymns and solemn tunes of the
band outside interspersed themselves with polkas, gallops
and quadrilles by the band inside. At last a circus
arrived—no, a crisis—and the whole of the company
inside with the band at their head, ranged themselves
outside the tent and opposite to the Salvation Army
band. The spectators inside came outside also, and
ranged themselves with the rest of the town (which had
been trying to get in under the canvas without paying) to
watch the proceedings. Both bands struck up simul-
taneously two different tunes in separate keys. The
clown and the Salvation captain harangued, the tambor-
ines banged, the horses neighed, and the lions roared.
At last, the circus bandmaster, Herr Von der Mehden,
blew one terrific blast on the cornet, at which the Salva-
tion Army, thinking the last trump had sounded, fled
precipitately, leaving the circus in possession.
William Archer says there are a hundred dramatic
moments in “The Second Mrs. Tanqueray ” worth all the
attitudinising situations ever conceived. The play, in his
opinion, is absorbing, but not moving. “ Not once during
the whole evening were the tears anywhere near ray eyes.
Yes, once—when Mr. Pinero came before the curtain, and
the house rose at him. Then I felt a thrill of genuine
emotion to think that here at last, in spite of all the
depressing and stunting influences of our English thea-
trical world, was a man who had had the will and the
talent to emancipate himself and give the artist within
him free play—to take care of his soul, and let his pocket,
for the nonce, take care of itself.” Which reminds Tub
Bulletin that divoroe-Judge Windeyer absolutely wept
at the first performance of Mrs. Tanqueray in Sydney.
Both Argus and Age have lately changed their dramatic
critics.

When “ Professor ” Baldwin and his clairvoyants wife
were in Brisbane, they fairly squandered a smart press-
man who boasted he had in his hand an unanswerable
sealed query. The clairvoyants went through the usual
“ business,” and jerkt d out: “ I see a man, his face con-
cealed in a sort of iron shield ; his enemies seek his life.”
And then Baldwin: “Any gentleman in the audience
want to know about a man going around in an iron face-
guard?” “Yes,” replied the pressman ; “ I asked who
was the man in the iron mask.” The clairvoyants waded
in again; “I see policemen shooting at him; he fires
back. Alas ! he is seized.” This new rendering of an old
story was not explained till the lady in a trance climaxed
her brain-vision with the words: “Itis, yes—” “ Who?”
queried Baldwin. “Ned Kelly!” Then the pressman
went out to ruminate on the nothingness of encyclopaedic
knowledge.
Cabled that George Edwards’ London burlesque co-
visits Australia next year, engaged by the duumvirate.
New songs, new legs, new dances. Joy 1
Fitzgerald Bros.’ Circus caught on in Launceston
recently. When the brothers were counting up the
takings, they found a number of two shilling pieces, which
had been in use as brooches and from which the pin
attachments had been wrenched. A melancholy sign of
the times.
The success of Madame Melba, Frances Saville and other
Australians goes to upset the old theory that a meat-
eating people cannot produce great singers—founded on
observation and comparison of the meat and corn-pro-
ducing districts of Italy and Norway.
A recent production of “Maritana” at Perth was
honoured with a 1J col. notice in the West Australian.
The critic started off by describing the circumstances that
led to the writing of the opera in 1845 ; then came a dis-
quisition on the rival merits of “Maritana” and “The
Bohemian Girl,” followed by a few remarks upon British
music In general, and finally a burst of hysterics
over the unparalleled performance of Leumane, Marie
Elster and company. It seems to have been about the
best show in the whole annals of drowsy Perth, where old
friend Julius Herz has begun to teach them the art of
chorus-singing.
Florence Young goes to London soon.
“ Every effort is being made to eschew the abominable
system of bringing into prominence one or two star artists
surrounded by a bundle of sticks, and aspire to a com-
bination of all-round excellence made all the stronger by
the energy and loyalty inseparable from the true artist.”
—Garner’s advt., Melb. Bijou.
With Edith Blande, Annie Taylor and Jenny Watt-
Tanner as heavy-weights in Arthur Garner’s new 00., and
Garner and Harwood on the male side, one wonders where
the light comedy element is to squeeze in.
Universal title for melodrama—“ Falsely Accused.”
Herbert Flemming is with Warner, at the Adelphi.
Where is ? Well, no matter.
The human tableaux, shown at London Empire, are
secured by Williamson for Australia.
Ryley having long itched for London, Williamson
engages Brownlow to replace him.
On dit that Victoria’s favourite soprano is not going to
Europe! Things in the benefit line did not pan out as
expected; too closely following the recent big emptying
of sympathetic pockets on Eva Crossley’s behalf.
Arthur Roberts comes with the Gaiety troupe to Wil-
liamson and M. All Lonnen’s plod cannot bring him to
the level of Leslie and Roberts, who may fairly be
bracketed with departed Fred. Roberts is more even
than Leslie in being a born improvisatore, whereas Leslie
had to con every effect, like a schoolboy, or fancied he
must do so. He was afraid of being spontaneous. Roberts
has a kaleidoscopic revelry in the scene.
The absolutely best things in “ A Woman’s Revenge ”
are George Gordon’s bit of Devonshire north coast in
Act 1,, and Henrietta Watson’s neck.
Arthur Garner, Walter Bentley and Phil. Stuart
(Garner’s Bijou biz. manager) inspected the house on the
first night of returned “ Charley’s Aunt ” with evident
interest. It was a big crowd, considering the comnany,
and the trio thought it a useful housewarming.
Now it leaks out that England and
Portugal tried to put Humpty Dumpty up again,
in the Brazilian Monarchy. Mello’s first idea,
like Boulanger’s last, was General Monk.

Optimist to Pessimist.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Though all your plans may “gang nglee,’
Though every man may seem your foe,
Though flies may tumble in your tea,
And passers-by compress your toe,
Though shares you hold in every Co.
That’s busted, do not act the jay !
Here’s-something to relieve your woe—
“lt is not always washing-day ! ”
Sometimes, of course, things will not gee:
Sometimes your row is hard to hoe ;
Still, do not lie down wearily,
And blaspheme all things high and low.
Besides offending Bishop Goe,
Curses don't hold the world at bay.
Just climb up on your fence, and crow—
“ It is not always washing-day 1 ”
Come, live with us in Arcady ;
Sell that sad face to Holy Joe;
To gladness give your heart in fee;
You’ll find life’s tempests gentler blow.
You’re sure to reap whate’er you sow ;
Who sows sad tares won’t reap ripe hay ;
Throw back your head and laugh “ Heigh ho!
It is not always washing-day 1 ”
Friend, when your cake seems turned to dough,
Just take podophyllin, and pray
That God will teach your soul to know
It is not always washing-day 1
Melb.
STEELE GREY.
One or two S.A. village settlements will
collapse very soon. There are two notable suc-
cesses, “Holder” and “Waikeri," both on the
Murray.
The Church of England and its head have
just been described by Canon Scott Holland as
“ the symbol of all that is aloof from the life of
the workers, cold to their aspirations, suspicious
of their aims, helpless in their needs, the type of
all that is privileged and propertied, and jealous
and unsympathetic, and everything the least like
Christ.” Apro/ws, there are in the square mile
known as the City of London over 60 Anglican
churches, most of them built at a time when the
merchants of London lived in the city. The
city’s resident population now is only between
50,000 and 60,000 ; and not one-half of the people
of London go to any place of worship, and there
are about as many churches belonging to other
sects and denominations. St. Magnus, near
London Bridge, is closed, but the parson, never-
theless, draws an income of .£1080; St. George’s,
Botolph-square, is closed, but the parson from
public property draws an income of .£741; St.
Mary-at-Hill is closed, but the parson draws .£500;
St. Mary Abchurch has an average congregation
of a dozen persons, the parson drawing .£740;
St. Stephen’s, Walbrook, has a congregation of
some thirteen persons, the parson drawing .£Bl5 ;
St. Andrew’s, Undershaft, has forty-five of a con-
gregation, and the parson draws <£2350; and so
on through this whole list. With congregations
averaging from a dozen to a score, we find incomes
varying from .£5OO up to over £2OOO. Probably
the congregations of these sixty city churches on
Sunday would not amount to more than 1000
persons; yet for their so-called services the
parsons receive the yearly total stipend of £62,000.
What a hideous swindle!
Luck?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Scene I.—He was stone-broke, and the last few
weeks had been living on the odd drinks that he
got from the publican, in remembrance of what ho
had been. So they raised a few pounds amongst
the townspeople to pay his fare to a large mining
town.
Scene ll.— He (always a swell with a gift of
the gab) hobnobbed with the big-bugs, and they
“laid” him on to something which turned out
good. He made a pile, and spent it like a man.
Scene lII.—He is broke again, or nearly so ;
some more kind friends lay him on. He strikes it
richer than ever this time and is spending it like
water.
Scene IV.—He is standing on the steps of Scott’s
Hotel, when comes along one who had “passed
around the hat,” evidently “stiff,” “Ah, how
do you do, Mr. ?I am glad you have got on
so well. lam hard up. You couldn’t let me have
half-a-sov., could you?” He (putting hand in
pocket): “ I never lend money.” (Pulling out and
offering him a shilling.) “ Here, that will buy you
a meal and bed—good day !”
The irony of it. The poor devil who took the
“ bob ” had chipped in 20 bobs when he passed
around the hat.
FLONG.
THE WIDOW’S DEATHBED.
In the resurrection, therefore, when they shall rise
whose wife shall she be of them, for the seven bad her to
wife?—Mark, xii. 23.
The widow lay on her dying bed,
With candles at foot and candles at head,
And feebly turned to the priest and said :
“Father, good father, my soul to-night,
In an hour, perchance, takes its last long flight;
In an hour, perchance, I shall stand in the light
Of the great white throne, borne down with
affright—
For the weight of my sins is crushing me quite,
But deeper than this is my present care—
Deeper the depth of my dark despair,
For my greatest trouble is how I shall fare
When I meet with my various husbands there.
For each of the seven—or was it eight?—
I promised to meet at the great gold gate ;
Each as the death-mist dimmed his eye,
Each as his soul was about to fly.
With a gasping sigh that was half a cry
Said to me, ‘ Sweetest! you and I
Must part, but ’tis only to meet on high*—
Or some such conventional bathos as this ;
And then, with a lingering, ultimate kiss,
They successively started for Heavenly bliss.
But, father, the thought that oppresses my soul
Is what will occur when I reach the goal, _
And find eight cherubs in white robes wait,
My coming, at old St. Peter’s gate?
Will they in the shocked archangels’ sight
Disgrace themselves by a stand-up fight ?
And if they don’t, but agree to share
My charms, will celestial society stare
And turn up its nose? And, oh ! need I fear
The unmarried seraphim’s maidenly sneer?
And if I flirt with each and all,
Will respectable angels refuse to call ?
Will the inner circle around the throne
Begin to talk in a spiteful tone
Of fast young minxes, and purse their lips.
And gather their skirts round sanctified hips,
When we meet to drink water of life in sips ?
Will?”—here came a cough, a smothered sigh,
A moan—and the lady had gone to try.
Sydney.
D.W.

Dear Mr. Thuunius,—On Christmas Eve, 23rd December,
1893, I had been in bed for nearly three weeks suffering from
Rheumatic Gout, when Mr. Hamburger, of 156 Rourke-street,
persuaded me to try a bottle of your Rheumatic medicine,
and its results were simply surprising. On the following
Wednesday 1 was able to sit up in a chair, and the swelling in
my legs had visibly decreased, on the following Saturday I
could get about, and on Tuesday, 2nd January, 1894, I was
able to go to my work without pain, and I rapidly improved
in health. I send you this that you may make whatever use
of it you may think best. I shall recommend it to all whom
I know.—Yourg respectfully, FRANCES DUMAS.
