It is commonly accepted, lately remarked
one of the most experienced of Australian ex-
plorers to The Bulletin, that primitive man, as
represented by the Australian savage, is a peerless
bushman, and can go with unerring directness to
any point. This, like the patience of the camel,
is a popular error. The blackfellow knows his
own particular country by rote, but take him to
another district and he is entirely astray. More-
over, he has no idea of either distance or direction.
If he wished to direct you to a point which, in
reality, lay due west, he would probably point
N. W.; after going a short distance on that course
he would then point S.W., then, perhaps, W.,
until he finally arrived at the spot by the parti-
cular way which he had always been accustomed
to traverse; but he would have no idea of going
straight in the first instance, This inability to
appreciate the beauty of a straight line often leads
to much misunderstanding between whites and
blacks, and has often been the means of the black-
fellow being accused of wilful misdirection,
whereas ho has been doing his best according to
his lights.
Kaffirs are flogged iu Johannesburg, South
Africa, for walking on the footpath, which is
strictly reserved for whites. It is alleged that the
mines produce a class of Kaffir snobs who jostle
white women and children at every opportunity—
hence their relegation to the middle of the road
with other “cattle.”
The successful removal of Piles of the
most stubborn description is being effected by
Sharp’s new “Cannalin “treatment, large quan-
tities of which are being forwarded to sufferers
throughout the colonies. It is obtainable, with
advice, from 257 George-street, Sydney.
Baptist Parson E. Price holds that an
eminently applicable name for his sect is—“ The
Holy Catholic Apostolic Primitive Methodist
Bible Christian Church.” A fine mouthful,
reverend christeners ! What’s the matter with
Baptoepiscopopresby tomethody b i bl i ocon g r eg a-
tional Catholics ?

BOKO.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
All the riding-gear is rusty, all the girths and
straps are dusty,
And the saddle’s old and mouldy where it’s
hanging on the wall;
While the stockwhip and the bridle on their pegs
are hanging idle,
And old Boko comes no longer to the sliprails
when I call.
No, because his bones are lying where I lay
beneath him dying
When the game old stockhorse blundered at the
jump, and broke his neck,
And I got a woeful smashing when the poor old
fellow, crashing
Through the timber, crushed me under to a
bruised and sightless wreck.
With his single eye to guide him very few could
live beside him
Though he was no thoroughbred, but just a poor,
old grass-fed moke,
And we held the reputation, crack-scrub dashes
on the station ;
You could track us through the raulga by the
timber that we broke.
And the day we got the buster was just after
Bangtailed muster;
I had asked the super.’s daughter to become
head-stockman’s wife;
She had answered, “I am ready. If you’ll promise
to be steady.
If you’ll give up drink and fighting, Jack, and
lead a decent life.”
And from that our quarrel started —both grew
angry and we parted,
And that night I started drinking at the shanty
on the Flat
Where the o.p. grog is snaky, and next day all
wild and shaky
I rode over to a picnic that I knew she would
be at.
She was there all mirth and gladness, but I masked
my sullen madness—
Held aloof, and would not see the sorrow grow-
ing in her eyes;
All around were gay and busy, bub my brain was
hot and dizzy,
When an old-man kangaroo went bounding past
across the rise.
Spurs and bits and stirrups jingled, shouts and
glad confusion mingled,
While we urged the dogs and horses, fresh and
eager for the fray ;
Horses, too, with plenty breeding, bub the old
bush nag was leading,
Once we left the open country Boko showed
them all the way.
Dead Box Rise and Sheoak Hollow taxed their
horsemanship to follow,
At the old marsupial fence I had them pounding
at their top;
Half-insane and wild with liquor, still I led and
urged them quicker,
Though the rest were pulling up and some were
calling out to stop.
It was only reckless flashness, only harebrained
drunken rashness,
I looked back and laughed to see them drawing
rein away behind,
Then I turned and spurred him to it, but he struck
and toppled through it;
When they dragged me from beneath him he
was dead and I was blind.
When I woke to know my blindaess, then I woke
to know her kindness,
For she stood beside my bed and bandaged up
my shattered brow,
Whisp’nng, “ Let me help to bear it. I waa
wrong and I will share it.
Won’t you have me, for I love you just as much
as ever now ?”
And she would have shared my sorrow through
this night that has no morrow,
But I loved her far too well to let her be a
cripple’s bride.
And at times when I am able just to ramble to
the stable,
Where I sib and dream of Boko and of many a
merry ride,
I can hear her children playing—l can hear the
cattle neighing—
I can hear the stockwhips cracking when the
cattle reach the yard,
But my sightless eyes may glisten—all the world
is one dark prison,
And the gates to light and gladness shall be
never more unbarred.
For the riding-gear is rusty, and the racing-tackle
musty,
And though Boko’s bones are bleaching there
are colts upon the plain,
Fiery colts just fit for breaking; but ray heart is
sadly aching,
For I know that I will never ride nor show the
way again.
CURLEW.
Dear Bulletin,—About Queen Yicfcoriaa
When I was very young I was imbued with a
near a belief in the “divine right of kings ” as the
intelligence of the times would permit, for my
father was a Church and State man in whose
nostrils stank the very name of Dissenter. I
started out for a walking tour through Scotland,
and arrived at a Highland inn, called the “Spittle
of Glenshee,” on the same day that the Queen
wag t 0 change horses there on her way to Bal-
moral. A number of the local ladies and gentle-
men were waiting to meet her, amongst them
being the Countess of Fife (mother of the present
Duke). She was a very handsome woman, of the
Spanish type of beauty, and, seated on a big.
thoroughbred horse, looked magnificent. Watch-
ing her, a sort of hazy idea got into my head that
jf that was a countess, what must a queen be!
Up to the inn dashed the royal carriage, drawn
by four splendid grey horses. But where was the
Queen? As the carriage stopped, a dumpy, ordi-
nary-looking little woman held out her hand to
Farquharson, of Deeside, one of the crowd present,
with the salutation “Well, Jamie, howareyou ?”!!!
Away in a flash went all my dreams of royalty,
and I have never gone a step out of my way to
gee a crowned head since, although I have tra-
velled the world.-DuNCAN Macallum.
Dear Bulletin,—The average Australian
does indeed prefer the poorest imported literature
to the brightest productions of his own country,
A proof of this “ anti patriotic bias” is the fate
of that admirable work “ Halek,” written by a
Queenslander, John H. Nicholson. Though purely
the produce of the Australian bush, it is one of
the finest allegories in the language, marked, as it
is, by graceful English, picturesque description,
great dramatic power, delicate humour, and
original philosophy. It has been ten years before
the public, yet not one in a thousand has ever
read a line in it, nor, so far as I can ascertain, baa
it found its way into any of the schools of art*.
There is an author’s presentation copy in the
Johnsonian Club Library —all books by Australian
writers in that library are authors’ presentation
copies—but few of the members have opened it,
though most of them are noisy as to the unrivalled
humour of Jerome, and are absolutely deafening
as to the matchless sublimity of Marie Corelli. I
would send you the book, on the chance that you
have not read it, but it is no longer on sale in
Brisbane. 1 asked a bookseller for it the other
day—the same sapient tradesman who sent me
“ Butler’s Analogy ” when I had ordered “ But-
ler’s Hudibras”—but he had never even heard of
“ Halek,” and wanted to know if it were not
“Vathek” I was in search of.—Yours,
BOOK-
WORM.
Probably amongst Australian bushmen, no
squatter was ever held in higher estimation than
“Good old Jacky Dow,” and, excepting Jimmy
Tyson or Lauchy M'Bean, no other is the hero of
so many bush yarns. Here are one or two of them,
partially stale :—Jacky Dow was always ready to
listen to appeals for a job, but in almost every
case would express his disbelief in the genuineness
of the applicant’s desire for work, and, by way of
test, would sometimes set absurd tasks. Thus, a
swagman on one occasion, after the usual feed,
was shown a big heap of firewood and directed to
pile it up against the kitchen wall. He set to with
a will, and an hour’s hard work completed the job.
Jacky expressed his satisfaction and told his new
hand to shift the wood back where he got it from.
There was a stare of astonishment, but the boss
was all serene, so the wood was put back. When
that was done, the orders were to pile it up
against the wall again, and so Jacky kept the
game going for three or four hours, till at last
the victim put his coat on and remarked that
“this ’ere job was too bloomin’ muttonous.”
Jacky paid him and sent him away, saying
blandly; “Ah! I thought you didn’t want
work.’’ On another occasion, after the usual
colloquy, he set two men, one to sib on a gate and
the other to swing it back and forward, and to
continue at this till something else was found for
them to do. The men grinned, but took the con-
tract. After an hour or so, the division of labour
appeared to the swinger inequitable, and he made
overtures to his mate to take a turn at swinging
while he did some of the sitting, bub the mate
naturally objected, so the other, after some high
words, knocked him off the gate, and there was a
great and glorious fight. Presently, having
battered each other to their mutual satisfaction,
the two appeared before Jacky, who affected sur-
prise at their leaving their work ! but they said
they saw no points in it, so he let them go, telling
them also that “ he thought they didn’t want
work.” Another “casual” had a pannikin given
him with instructions to dip water out of the
river Murrumbidgee and to keep on doing so till
some other job turned up. The “ bushie” looked
Jacky square in the eye, and says he : “ Looky
here, Jacky Dow. They do say you’re off your
nanny, and, blow me, if I don’t believe it now.
Set a man to bale out a river ! Well, I’m !’’
But Jacky only replied, with the invariable for-
mula : “Ah ! I thought you didn’t want work.”
But (this is the stalest yarn) he met his match
for once, when he was accosted by an unmis-
takeable Yankee, who respectfully enquired if
there was “ any kind of a job hanging around ?”
—he was “ willing to turn his hand to anything,’*
and so on. Jacky, after the usual incredulous
remarks, set him to turn tha handle of the grind-
stone. The Yankee made ao sort of objection,
and Dow left him turning as if he had no other
object in life, and whistling “ Yankee Doodle.”
In about a oouple of hours Jacky came back, and
there was his new acquaintancecomfortably perched
on a board fixed in the framework of the grindstone,
reading an ancient paper and the grindstone
going at about five hundred revolutions a minute.
The son of the land of “notions” had rigged
up a rough but effective treadle, and was
working it at his ease, with his foot. Jacky took
it all in without a smile. Said he, “ Here, ray
man, you’re the very chap I want,” and thereafter
the down-easter was permanently engaged.
“ Those who sing the praises of poverty
always accompany themselves on a golden harp.”
—Ajikr.

Dear Bulletin,—Would you like to know
my tramps? Well, on Monday morning a poor
old fellow, dirty and tattered, 1 walks up boldly;
“ An’, ’av ye plaze, miss ” —I’m near 40 and quite
motherly— “ Can yez give a poor man a bite to
ate? I haven’t had any since yisterday.” I give
him some bread and meat. “May the blessed
Vargin look down and bless yez and yer childer,
and may yez niver want bit or sup, and- ” Bub
I leave him—I am used to the blarney. Then, on
Wednesday, come three all together. Two come
down and ask if there is any chance of a job. I
tell them no ; I have no money to pay big wages.
“ Then, can you gi ve us something to eat ?’’ I tell
them to go and dig in the garden awhile, and I
will give them a meal; but they don’t like it, so
off they go. Thursday comes a well-dressed man
—says he can do anything; a sort of Jaok-of*all-
trades. I tell him to chop some wood and I will
give him Ins dinner, as I am a widow, and cannot
feed all travellers. He goes to the wood-pile and
picks up the axe. Chop! Then a spell. Chop!
Another spell. And soon for about a quarter of
an hour, when he sits down for a good spell.
Thinking he will work better when he is fed, I
send him his dinner and a basin of tea. He eats
what he can’t pocket, puts the rest in his bag,
picks up his swag and away he goes. One for me.
Saturday come two, with a miserable pack-horse
between them. One comes up to the house and
asks for tea and sugar. He has a wide-brimmed
hat, with two red bands round it, bell-bottomed
trousers and a watch-guard fit for a trace-chain.
But you are weary ?— Bush woman, Vie.
England.
(FR THE BULLETIN.)
Old outpost of the nations, standing there
Encompassed by the stormy northern sea,
God’s ancient citadel of liberty.
His flag of freedom streaming in the air.
The Church and State have stripped thine honour
bare.
But thy polluted shores no more shall be
Earth’s golden lazarette of infamy,
For God has heard our universal prayer.
The tempest of His vengeance soon shall sweep
The apostate priesthood from His holy place,
And purge the bloody shambles of thy power.
Thine utmost strength shall founder in the deep,
And Freedom, rising on the human race,
Shall light the triumph of thy mortal hour.
Parramatta (N.S.W.)
DOWELL O'REILLY.

The “Sending of the Woman.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“ This must not b«, Amat!”
“ It shall be, Tuan 1”
“Thy father would not have had it so 1”
The chief took his nipa-wrapped cigar from his
mouth and flicked the ash to the ground. “That,”
said he, “is my father! This” —pointing to the
glowing end thus exposed—“ is myself! The
dead are the dead, but I live, and it shall be as I
say!”
He half turned away, then faced quickly round
again as Drake’s hand wandered, half-meohani-
oally, to his side.
“Be not a fool, white man ! Two of us might
thou shoot—or three—before thou also shouldst
be but as burnt tobacco !”
“Thou art mistaken, Amat! To what end
should I shoot now ? But I will shoot, when the
time comes, sooner than let this thing be !
“ Then, instead of but one, many will follow rny
father into the darkness. Is this the white man’s
wisdom?” And Amat went. .
“ Doesn’t look much like wisdom, either,
Drake told himself, as he looked after the hard-
headed little Dyak. “I might, as he says, pot
two or three of ’em before I’d be slashed into
ribbons, and then this infernal thing would be
carried through all the same.”
* # •
Said Mrs. Drake : “ And Amat was right!
it is white man’s wisdom ! But it isn’t white
woman's
“Oh, I see! Let s have the woman’s, then 1
and Drake, prepared to blow the cloud of consola-
fcl°“ First, then—when’s this horrid thing to be
done?”
“ To-morrow’evening.”
“ And the way of it?” . ~
“ Make the girl fast in a boat, put in the old
man’s swords and jars and things, and send the
lot adrift 1” . , , , . ,
Mrs. Drake covered her face, and her husband
caught something about “ the dark places of the
ea *‘ Get rid of that notion, wife !” he said. “ The
light places are just as cruel in their own way. No
varnish about the matter here —that’s all the
difference!
Mrs. Drake waived the point. “ How long will
it take the girl to drift down river ?” she asked.
“About two hours. They’ll start her on the
quarter-ebb—about four o’clock—so as to go out to
sea with the setting sun.” ....
“ Well, Jack, since you can t nip this horror in
the bud—why not get down beforehand and wait
for the poor creature ? ” .
“ Because, my dear, I’ve showed my hand—like
the ass lam ! All to-morrow I’ll be shadowed,
and, now that the old chief’s dead, some of these
fellow’s wouldn’t think twice about—well, never
mind that! ” , ...... Q .
Dorothy Drake was no mechanical pietist, bhe
knew that the Lord didn’t always interfere upon
what appeared to be the right side, and she
shivered, despite the 80-degree night air. But her
courage —like her Christianity was the real
article.
“ Would you go, Jack, if it were not for me .
“ Yes ! and within the hour ! ”
Palo little Mrs. Drake put her hands on his
shoulders :
“ Go ! ” she said.

Galun-Puli, of dubious and indefinite
memoiy, had been “almost persuaded.’ ler-
sonality—always more or less in abeyance under
savage oonditions—comes readily enough out of
its shell at the contact with more sophisticated
intelligences, and Galun, who, before Mrs. Drake
took him in hand, had been simply a Dyak of the
Dyaks no more bloodthirsty and vindictive than
the ruck, and no leas—had developed, under the
touchstone of external suggestion, the makings of a
very tolerable Christian. Tribally honest, to
begin with—he had easily enough universahsed
that idea; the hunting of heads he had trans-
ferred from the category of imperative necessities
to that of questionable indulgences; and, if the
central good-for-evil doctrine was for him but a
blank incomprehensibility, he was at any rate no
worse off than the millions of Christians who give
themselves the lie more persistently over this than
over any other of their lip-laws.
But now Galun —his instructress hoped—had
“ gone up,” and Amat, the reactionary, reigned in
his stead. Amat would have made an excellent
sectary, with the difference that, though he
utterly despised all beliefs but his own, he didn t
pretend to love the dissentients. He hated them,
and said so ! Things should be as they were
before the coming of this white woman with her
Christa —who was only the Prophet of the Malaya
over again. Amat was sick of these prophets,
who wanted a man to go the way of a woman !
and the short of the matter was that if any Dyak
wanted to run after any Prophet but him, Amat,
he would have to do so without his head !
So the defunct Galun, gorgeously arrayed, lay on
his lofty platform and awaited the “sending of the
woman,” which Amat swore should, as of old,
take place before the body could be earthed.
Beside the withered old carcase lay parang and
kris; around stood jars of rice and palm-wine,
bundles of tobacco-leaf and sirik ; and below sat,
night and day, such of the body-watchers as had
not figuratively lost their heads enough with tuak
to risk the literal loss thereof by speaking a word
or stirring from their post. But fearfully rolled
their jetty eyeballs as, just upon the break of day,
a shot flashed redly from the gloomy wall of
jungle, and, clump 1 into one of the platform-posts
plunged a rifle-bullet, aimed, not at them, but at
a lomr-haired Dyak lad that fled swiftly past them
towards the house of Amat.
“Good is the Tuan’s gun!” muttered Drake s
savage old Sooloo servant, as he stepped back into
cover. “But my eyes are as the eyes of bats!
This imp of Sheetan was a spy ; he is with Amat
by this time ! Vain is the Tuan’s journey 1”
# • •
“ In my father’s day, Tuan, these heathen
dogs of Dyaks did always thus at the death of a
chief. One boat did he find himself, outside this
very river, with swords, and jars, and silver rings,
and many precious things besides !”
“ And a woman ?” Drake asked.
“ And a woman —eya ! —young and fair to look
upon ! Her, my father sold for many dollars to a
Kini ” (Chinaman). . ,
Drake significantly tapped his pistol-pouch. 1
know thee, Hossun, and thou knowest me ! Give
heed, then, when I say this woman must be cared
for as thy sister. No Kinis, and no dollars—save
what I will give thee. And all things in the boat
shall be thine, as well !”
Hossun laughed and nodded. “It shall be so,
Tuan. The girl shall be to us as a sister, until
thou takeat her away. Paddle, ye dogs !—the sun
wears fast to the water !’’
Hossun was a coast-Malay— three-parts a pum-
pak (pirate)—but, like most of his reckless sort, a
man of his word ; and glad was he, in any case, to
do the Dyaks an ill-turn and the white man a
service at one stroke.
Like furnace-flame the surf upon the bar flared
up against the crimson sun as the boat dashed
through into the placid, palm-fringed river. Upon
the polished surface was a single speck—a quarter
of a mile above, but coming down fast with ebb
and current. It was the drifting boat, and they
paddled up to meet it.
Not a sword !—not an armlet! —neither jar nor
jewel!—no, nor robe ! The Malays invoked Alla,
the Prophet, and the Devil, but Drake didn’t hear
them—he was cutting loose the motionless figure
in the stern,
Hossun came to look. He lifted the plump,
brown arm—dropped it—raised the eyelid lis-
tened at the naked bosom —and nodded a reply to
Drake’s unspoken question,
“But, how?” the white man said. “There is
no wound !”
“ Datyorah !” said the Malay, and Drake under-
stood. Datyorah— the datura of the Hindu Thugs
—is stramonium.
Amat had been beforehand with him !
ALEX. MONTGOMERY.
Penrith (N.S.W.) has a lady watchmaker.
At Belltree station, N.S.W., last shear g.
two shearers had an egg-eating contest for a i 25
wager. Now, the “meat” of an egg steeped in
vinegar will shrivel up to the size of an ordinary
pigeon-egg a circumstance each centestant
thought his opponent unacquainted with. When
the guzzle started, however, it turned out that
both were aware of the fact, and had prepared
their eggs accordingly. Scores ; 9 doz. and 7 eggs,
and 7 doz. and 9 ! Both lived—unfortunately.
Colemane & Sons’ Vie. Ointment is marvellous-
_ r . ,
The remarks made by the Yankees on the
N.S.W. exhibits at the greatest show on earth in
the visitors’ books provided for the purpose, have
just been noted. They are all highly flavoured,
but those on the mineral section are particularly
exhilarating. The “grands” have it, but the
adherents of “immense” have a large following,
sight - welfto'thefom‘ ojffltei Mvidull
remarked that they “beat the Dutch”; and
another person who had the patriotic spirit strong
within him committed himself to “ way up in G.”
A man whose French was a steeply-limited quality
let off “Bon-ton”as his outpouring, and another
inhabitant of the great continent, whose thoughts
turned chiefly to the political, expressed a wish
that “ South Wales be forthwith annexed.” Bv
the way, the ’Murkans could never be got to call
this country by any other name than “South
Wales.” Returned officers also report that fre-
quently the men of expectoration came in and
“wanted to see an Australian.” One youthful
member who prides himself on his English was
congratulated on having picked up the tongue so
quickly. Altogether, however, the minerals of
N.S.W. seem to have impressed the Americans as
among the best features of the Exhibition.
Serenade.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Good-night ! sweet dreams and soft repose I
Good night! bright star of angelhood !
Good-night! my garden’s one queen-rose—
Nay, who would say good-night? who would
The day’s dear memory-casket close?
Shall other days bear fruit as good ?
Alas ! who knows.
My queen ! although thy rule extend
Through day and night, through Life and Fate,
I call thee vision still, not friend,
Mine eye’s delight, not my heart’s mate,
Through many devious ways I wend ;
But find thy sweet soul’s entrance-gate
Too soon—or late.
I meet thee, kiss thee—lip to lip
And eyes on eyes, the stars behold
Our souls in seas of passion dip.
Outside, the world is dim and cold.
We two are one ! but who shall slip
The bolts of Fate, till, kindred-souled.
One crown we keep ?
On two far shores made separate,
Our spirits watch the waves that cast
Our hopes upon the rock of Fate.
We met not once in ages past,
Not once —and even so desolate
The deathless years our hives outlast.
Thou comest not, I cannot wait,
The cold stars bind the spirit fast
Until —too late !

Tarrago and Boreera stations (sheep) are
own ed and managed by a board of directors in
Melbourne. One of the directors had just been up
on a tour 0 f inspection and was on his way back,
Addressing a fellow-passenger, he remarked ;
<< s; r> b y your conversation I believe you come
from Queensland. Do you know what sort of
stations Tarrago and Boreera are?” Oh, yes,
m yown place is quite close, and those stations are
bW o G f the best in Queensland, only they are most
damnably mismanaged by a set of old women in
Melbourne.” The director (colouring up); “Sir,
a u ow me to present my card.” The Stranger;
“Well, sir, you have heard some very wholesome
truth.” Then the journey ended in silence.
